THE RED PAGE.
ON the whole, the undertakers’speeches,
asked for in competition, were not
individually satisfactory, though here
and there came gleams of what a
really jovial undertaker might think and
say. The prize of 10s. fid. goes to Mri C.
W. Jones, who sends the following—
An Undertaker’s Speech.
“ Gentlemen,—Here’s to the cloth—the cere-
cloth. A beer to the bier. Long life to death.
Success to the greatest and gravest profession of
all. Does any one doubt that ? How is it, then,
that, while every enterprise is an undertaking,
common usage has confined the term ‘ undertaker ’
to one special form of enterprise ? Does not
language thus embalm a confession that the under-
taking par excellence is that which meetly and
duly closes the drama of life? Call no man
happy till he is dead, says the sage; suspend your
verdict, that is, till the pall falls on the last scene
in the tragedy. Accoucheurs and monthly
nurses are not not called undertakers save
by occasional grim humour of sarcasm.
They are mere assistants in providing fresh raw
material for the final artist. Solemnizers of
matrimony merely mark an earlier stage in that
production : physicians but provide that delicate
suspense of postponement which gives due dramatic
interest to the climax. What are good generals
but our jackals ? What is the greatest statesman
but a prospective national funeral ? And would
that every man was a great statesman ! The three
stages of life are birth, marriage, and death. You
can be born, at a pinch, for nothing : you can be
married, ring and witnesses provided, for a guinea:
but, to our credit be it said, you cannot be buried
in the humblest way under a tenner.
“So I repeat, without fear of contradiction,
success to the grandest occupation of all. What
does that wish imply ? Is it a prayer for a visit
from the plague ? By no means. * Plagues may
attack even undertakers; and the sight of cart-
loads of subjects hastily and unceremoniously shot
into a pit does not mean the success we desire. Is
it an increase in the birth-rate? Gentlemen, I
have seven already. Or in the marriage-rate ?
Well, that helps us. Small families and plenty is
what we want. An only child is worth to us
seven tmies one of seven. Do we want the death-
rate increased ? Not that exactly; but we
deprecate the growing fashion of longevity. If the
maximum age was lowered to sixty-five there would
not be more deaths on the whole, since no man, alas!
dies more than once : but our interests would be
greatly advanced. The very old and the very
young are equally undesirable subjects : grandpa
at eighty compares very unfavourably with papa
at forty-five, and Mabel at two months is a deplor-
able waste of material. No : we ask not for more
deaths, but for a nicer discrimination in dying.
We ask also for a higher standard of taste in cere-
mony and circumstance: we want to see hatch-
ment and catafalque, mausoleum and obsequy,
take the place of the plumeless hearse, the solitary
coach, the plain shell and the bare earth, which
modern lack of taste makes so painfully common.”
Loud applause; audience burst spontaneously
into “ Down among the dead men/’
C. W. JONES.
This, by J. A. Wilkinson, was received
too late for competition—
An Undebtakeb’s Speech.
Ma freens, —I’d hae ye drink “ Success to under-
taking.” Oors is the trade-that has the best
claim o’ ony tae be considered the auldest, for
ever sin’ the time o’ Abel there maun hae been the
happin’ up o’ the deid, an’ oor business wull gang
on till the Last Day, when Gabriel’s, trump, I’m
feart, wull make short wark o’ a’ trades, an’ dae awa
wi’ the necessity o’ coffins for those wha wull be
changed i’ the twinkling o’ an ee. Hoos’ever,
as that’ll no be in oor time, we can look
forrat tee a continuance o’ the auld-fashioned
way whiles we are i’ the warld, and ev’n should
oor auld-farrant ideas gie place to yon cremation
I’m thinkin’ there’ll still be need o* undertakers,
for the deid—wi’ a’ due respeo’ tae Scripture—
canna bury theirsels. That maun aye be dune for
them, and therefore there’ll a’ways be wric-hts,
or as thae English term them —undertakers.
“ But I’m sair mista’en if the guid auld days,
when a funeral wi’oot whusky was unheard o’, hae
no clean gane, departit indeed like them we hae
putten under the mods. Folk noo-a-days. seem
tae think they honor them that’s deid by no tastin’
ere they set oot frae the hoose, an’ tae hae a week’s
enjoyment at the efterhend wad e’en seem tae
lead a man tae fair damnation. Forbye that
there’s but little tae complayi o’. People dinna
quite tak up wi’ a’ thae ‘ trappings o’ woe, ’ as the
poet ca’s them, in the way they ance did, but still
there’s a wheen bit pickin’ tae be made oot o’ the
weaknesses o’ human natur’. There’s lead coffins
an’ oak, there’s siller plates an’ han’les, tae say
naethin’ o’satin linin’s, an’first, second, and third
class hearses, mournin’ coaches, an’ what not.
It’s a mortal peety, I’m thinkin’, that hat-bands
an’ gloves are no the fashion still; they aye make
han’some items i’ tha account.
“But Providence tempers the win’ tae the
shorn lamb, ye ken, an’ noo we juist hae the em-
balmin’tae mak up for what we wad lose by the
change o’ fashions. Losh me ! but yon embalmin’s
a graun’ notion for the trade. I’m no’ feart that
cremation wull make any difference tae the under-
takin’, tho’mebbe it wad gin the practeece becomin’
general, still it wadna a’thegither abolish oor pro-
feshion. But it’s no gaun tae tak a hold upon the
public yet awhile, for folks seem feart that the
Lord micht tak offence at the inoeenerary urns,
and no care tae tak the trouble o’ resurrectin’ a
pickle cinders, wi’ which He has tae gang owre a’
the bother o’ creatin' a new body ; and they’re juist
a wee thing dootfu’ about takin’ leeberties wi’
their Maker.
“Weel, ye’ll juist aff wi’ the toast. While
there’s life there’ll aye be death, and whaur there’s
a corpse there ’ll be work for the undertaker ; sae
here's ‘Success tae undertaking ! ’”
J. A. WILKINSON.
In order that the virtue of other competi-
tors may not. be lost, their work has been
concentrated in the following :
Associated Undertakers ' Picnic.
(From, the N.S. W. Undertakers'' and Gravcdieaers ’
Gazette.)
The annual picnic of the Associated Under-
takers’ Society was held yesterday on the
heights of Rookwood, and ' proved' in every
way successful. The weather was inappro-
priately gloomy; but there was no gloom over the
proceedings, which were eminently joyous and
festive. Upwards of four hundred undertakers
and others connected with the Profession attended
t7«5 fef, fami l ie f ? and by th .« ir happy faces tea-
tihed to the gratifying prosperity of business. In
7,hV ,a r SP ? competition was keen. The
■ » Head Handicap of 440yds., prize ldoz
Samnil W 6S ’ WaS WOn by Mr ‘ Beu Sorrow, Mr.
bainuel Worms carrying off second prize of two
I®* hearse-plumes A grave-digging contest be-
tween the caretakers of the Old and Now planta-
tions was won by the Old, after an exciting
struggle, by three shovelsful. K
At the luncheon, after Mr. W. Dykgrave had
sung “Down Among the Dead Men,” the toast of
man MV? pr ° po^ d by the respected chair-
man, Mr. I. Buryem, who spoke as follows
“Fellow Undertakers, Mutes, Gravediggers
Lmbalmers,— Ladies and Gentlemen,—l rise—as
I hope all our patrons will some day (hear hear)
to propose ‘Success to Undertaking.’ I will ask
urge a vo,i° to nn h thiH toiiSt - beer ' but 1 »eed nS
Profession^ 0 S° Ur gIaSS ? S ’ because 111 our
otession ha, ha ! we charge everything
(Laughter and applause.) y %
ai ? rl -? t to make a grave speech : yet,
formal? u laV ° not bac * an opportunity to
my remarks > 1 feel that I ean-
“ute <» this happy occasion ;
for I am delighted to state that never, in all the
cheerful Prof”- 1 1 — Ve en cbnne cted with our
cheerful Profession, have I seen a more jolly lot of
faces than those which you now present before
me. (Dear, hear !)
x ™ hy should we n ?f be jolly ? lam glad
to sa y that, as you know, business has never been
better than m the last twelve months. Though
the local birth-rate lias decreased, the death-
rate has kept up wonderfully. You will no
doubt have observed that the number of medical
degrees conferred by our local universities is
swelling yearly -a most gratifying omen for our
welfare. lam afraid that our friends of the allied
Profession of Medicine dp not always realise that
there is a mutual bond of obligation between us
We are deeply sensible of the benefits that they
confer upon us ; but do we not also benefit them
wu f ° L OWI 1 I j g 1 — t .°, cover U P th eir tracks ?
Why should Death s right hand quarrel with his
if- ' What are our licenses, and their diplomas,
but different stages of the same application of
habeas corpus heard before the Filial Judge ’ So
I congratulate my brethren of the medical profes-
sion not only on the number of young men who
are coming forward locally to join us ; but also on
the number who have arrived from Great Britain
and Ireland, with very beneficial results, giving
an extraordinary impetus to all branches of the
undertaking business. (Laughter and applause.)
Ladies and Gentlemen, we welcome every
birth into this world, more especially still-births,
tor a bird in the hand is worth two in the bush—
we welcome every birth, I say, because to be born
is sooner or later to die, and to die is to invoke the
Profession. We are glad of a large birth-rate :
j a J ar ß’ e death-rate. The birth-rate without
the death-rate would- profit little. For consider
what they imply. Large birth-rate : world a
nursery ; everything sticky with jam. One large
silence at the bottom of the Pacific ; another
smaller one in the Sahara; not a fragment else-
where. Life a mournful procession of peram-
bulators to the christening shops. Large death-
rate : a world peopled by men getting on well
with their neighbours ; because, as a rule, they or
their neighbours are dead. Rich relatives less
vexatious ; poor ones not so persistent; life en-
livened by the brisk march of numberless
iunerals ; and last, but not least, a prosperous
body of undertakers taking the current as
it serves, and never losing by their ventures.
Yet as people must be born to die, seeing that
corpses rarely come into the world ready-made,
we are content to subordinate our natural im-
pulses, and merely ask that in return our friends
will make a little concession to us by dying as
quickly as possible. There are many men in Aus-
tralia to-day, Ladies and Gentlemen-—men whom
the country could .very well spare—they are dead
—yes, Ladies and Gentlemen, dead; but they
won’t lie down—that’s where the trouble is. So
we ask the public to remember our motto : ‘Short
lives, and quick returns ’! (Loud applause.)
“ While I am dealing with this subject, I may
say—with I trust excusable pride—that nine hun-
dred and sixty-three and a quarter corpses was my
total last year’s plant, if you will allow me to class
a few odds and ends such as a couple of Chinese
and an aboriginal as legitimate corpses. The
fraction, I might mention, consisted of the leg
of a gentleman who went bathing at Bondi.
It was all that his friends were able to rescue from
the shark which took the remainder. I may say,
in passing, Ladies and Gentlemen, that I detest,
sharks. They inflict great losses on trade, as you
might expect from anything connected with the
Sea. I hate the Sea. No self-respecting under-
taker can do anything but hate a. blackleg who
works at prices that are outside fair competition.
(Applause.) But, returning to myself, if you ask
me how I reached the total I have mentioned to
you, I answer—and I trust my words will be
kept in mind by the younger members of the
Profession-Watch the crows that is, the
doctors. When I was beginning business
1 used to tip the doctors’ housemaids for
a list of clients, and kept a smart boy to
follow the dog-cart wherever it went. Then 1
waited—some were tougher than others, and took
longer to succumb ; but I think three weeks was
about the average. At the end of that time I was
generally able to get in a nice little varnished pine
shell, and do the business handsomely—though I
needn’t tell you, 'Ladies and Gentlemen, that I
always charged oak —or rosewood-,-some prefer
rosewood : it’s all a matter of taste. People ask
for rosewood, oak, mahogany—all sorts of ridicu-
lous things; but, Ladies and Gentlemen, as I am
speaking to friends, I need not tell you what they
get. (Loud laughter.) Ladies and Gentlemen, I
fear that undertakers live in an ungrateful world ;
but we must oppose the mind conscious of worth
to the slings and arrows of fortune. (Hear, hear.)
1 once had a dear friend ” (here the speaker’s voice
failed him for a moment) “who broke the ties of
half a lifetime in a wanton and cruel mariner.
We were dining together in a city restaurant,
and had reached the fish. Suddenly a bone
stuck in my friend’s throat. Whether I looked at
him iii a professional Manner or not I cannot say
(if I did, it was purely mechanical), but he cursed
me for a ghoul (groans)—yes, Ladies and Gentle-
men, a Ghoul between his gasps, and hurriedly
left lne. (Groans.) I had the—er —melancholy
pleasure of burying him two days later. (Applause.)
I am proud to say that 1 gave him the very best
send-off which my establishment was capable of.
(Cheers and applause.) But I must say that some
people are very inconsiderate. The other day, I
was Wivtching a nice little twenty-pound job on its
way to Rookwood, and a dismal-looking fellow
said to me, ‘Sad sight! ain’t it?’ I answered
him pretty sharply (and I trust the ladies will
excuse me), ‘lt would be a dashed sight more
melancholy to see that there elegant turnout
empty in the stables, eating its head off.’ (Ap-
plause.) He could n’t answer that , so he moved
on. It came into my mind that he looked like
one of those cremation-advocates. (A Voice :
‘ What about cremation ? ’ Groans and hisses.)
“ Ladies and Gentlemen, referring to matters
of current interest, I may say I don’t hold at all
with this pioposal to give up Hyde Park for
a railway-station. A beautiful burial site like
that ! —and so handy ; so much time saved in busy
seasons. I need riot say we could ensure that there
would be no saving in price. Again, you will n
doubt have observed with disgust that Australian
contingents are going to the Transvaal. (Groans.
Where’s the sense of that? (Applause.) War i
a curse. The corpses are shovelled into huge pit
by clumsy amateurs, regardless of the grief o
relatives and the professional feelings of under
takers. As artists we recoil from the thought
As men with wives and little children who look to
us for bread, we abhor it. And I say .emphatically
that the shipping of hundreds of our prospective
clients from this country is a gross breach of trust
and a disgraceful violation of vested interests
(Loud applause.)
“My fnend asks: ‘What about cremation?
I trust that no disturbing element will intei'fere
with our natural enjoyment. But, if I may be
permitted the comparison : Fancy the joy of see
nig the dear departed neatly screwed down in a
superfine rosewood, satin-lined and silver-plates,
at L 24, and the soothing drip, drip, drop o 1
the sweet juices of Mother Earth to lull him to his
eternal rest—and then the other style ! Faugh !
Where’s the poetry ? and, more, where’s the profit?
(Applause.) As I said the other day, the creina-
tionists are merely anticipating their ultimate fate.
Besides, we have Scripture on our side. Look
what happened to Daniel’s friends —Sliadrach,
Meshach, and Abed-nego ! Are not we told that
they stood in the fiery furnace and were not
consumed? Take my word, if the King had
screwed them down into three lovely cases I could
supply, and put six feet of good earth ’on them,
he would have had none of that trouble after-
wards. (Hear, hear !) No, Ladies and Gentle-
men, this cremation is merely an offshoot of
French cookery, and its surroundings are too de-
pressing for any British person to contemplate.
(Loud applause.)
Ladies and Gentlemen, I need not tell you
that undertaking is the oldest and most beneficent
Profession in the world. Ever since man lived—
died. He couldn’t bury himself. So when he
died he had to look round and get someone to do
it for him. Then We came in. (Cheers.) We are
m truth and in fact ‘the salt of the earth’—
a noble army of phagocytes, who painlessly
remove Humanity’s sloughs, and add with
embalming* disinfectants a new. and delight-
ful perfume to the odor cadaveris. It is our
highest joy to make the hideous, grinning
death’s head interesting and lovely, and to
straighten and beautify the gnarled and rigplr-
mortis-stricken corpse. As private individuals we
are modest and retiring. In our public capacity
we lead : decomposition follows. To kill is
human ; to inter divine. A man can be born for
nothing ; he can be married, ring and witnesses
provided, for a guinea ; but to our credit I say it,
he cannot be buried under a tenner. (Cheers.)
Certainly the incursions of amateurs and the baby-
farming industry have pressed a little upon us,
but we have not got below our legitimate three-
foot-six and a clay bottom, so to speak. Our friends
the doctors are first in the hunt ; but we are in-
variably in at the death. (Laughter.) We accom-
pany the pilgrim on the last stage towards his eternal
goal, and give him a friendly shove a few feet
nearer to it. We stand in the unique a.nd proud
position of men of whom their clients have never
been known to utter a single word in disparage-
ment ! Can this be said of Medicine ? the Church?
the Law? or, in fact, of any other Profession ?
Ladies and gentlemen, I venture to say that there
is no recorded oase of one of our clients changing
Ins undertaker. (Cheers and laughter.) Then, is
there any other calling that embrace? such a
variety of Sciences and Arts as ours ? Architec-
ture ! Horticulture !• Bacteriology ! Chemistry !
Anatomy ! Poetry ! We plant this poor mortal
body in the bosom of our mother Earth, confident
that it will soon blossom into a luminous and
ethereal spirit on the shores of that wonderful
city beyond the starry firmament. (Loud applause.)
Let cynics speak slightingly of ‘ the blacks and
obsequies’ of Death these things are the silk
linings and the gold fringes to our clouds ; the
plumes on which we soar.
Ladies and Gentlemen, our business cannot
be called a live business, nor a going concern. It
is a, dead business, concerning the gone. Other
businesses make their living out of the living •
their motto is, ‘Live and lot live.’ We make
our living out of the dead ; our motto is, ‘ Die
and let live.’ The name ‘undertaker’ has a
happy and humorous meaning—it means ‘One
who carries out,’ and we are the champions in the
carrying-out line—in fact, we are the Wright-
Heatons of the dead. (Cheers and laughter.)
But we concentrate the virtues of many trades. In
the undertaker you have the hotel-keeper who is
never ‘full,’ who gives beds to all and lets them
sleep'as long as they like without ringing a single
breakfast-bell. He is the kind policeman who
restores the wandering children of Earth to
their mother’s breast. He is the modest waiter
who at the end of the feast of life asks but for the
bones de moj’tuis nil nisi hones. (Loud '■ uigbter. )
We give many a man a bath, a clean shirt, and a
ride in a carriage, who never had such tilings
before ; and though our game is invariably ‘ whist, ’
and our favourite suit spades, we would scorn to
take-away from any man his last trump. (Ap-
plause.) Our noble profession has outlived gilded
dynasties and imperial governments, and has done
more for the closer settlement of people of eve,ry
rank on the land than any law or constitution
ever invented. (Applause.)
“Ladies and Gentlemen, there is one matter
which I touch witli diffidence, and yet with
strong conviction—that is, the attempt of women
to gam a footing in our Profession. It is easy to
understand that women envy the undertaker, for
he is a public ornament. In his official robes,
sitting straight and solemn in the forefront of his
black-plumed chariot, with some dear departed
packed ami labelled for the Silent City behind
ninp and his coal-black steeds w before him—their
bps hanging down in sympathy—the undertaker
forma an imposin'' picture which especially ap-
peals to women. In fact, it is so captivating' that,
as you are aware, it is no uncommon thing to
receive marriage proposals from widows on the
day after a funeral. Women will have heroes,
gentlemen ; and as the dear departed is the her
of the funeral, and she can’t have him, she goei
for his best man—the undertaker. (Applause.
Ladies and Gentlemen, I submit that this is th
true vocation of woman —to be the under
taker’s wife, but not to be the undertaker
(Applause.) What woman of sense will no
jump at the chance of being tucked in for her las
slumber by a husband’s loving hands? one vvh
will see that she is not aged more than 27 on tin
coffin-)date, and give her a gorgeous funera
which will make all the neighbour women wisl
they could be dead on the same terms. (Louc
cheers). But what I say is, Ladies and Gentle
men, that women as undertakers will ruin ou
business. Because they’re bound to have fashions
You’ll be reading in the papers that ‘Mrs. So-and
So was neatly attired in a black silk shroud, with
heavy jet trimmings,’ or that ‘Lady What’s-her
name was beautifully dressed in velvet and real ok
lace, and the weepers were the latest from Paris.
Where will the dignity of our Profession be then
So I say to women, marry us as much as you like
but never bury us ! (Applause).
“Ladies and gentlemen, I am sure you will be
glad to hear I have just received a telegram say,
ing that orders for six adults’ and three infants
funerals have been left at my establishment for
to-morrow —three of them first-class. (Loud ap-
plause. )
“Ladies and Gentlemen, when I cast my eye
around the festive board, and see happiness and
mirth glowing on every face, I see in these signs a
tribute not only to our host’s excellent luncheon,
but also a testimonial to the joys which our Pro-
fession is naturally calculated to afford. Cer-
tainly, I know that outsiders catalogue us as
sombre, morose—the most dismal of mortals. For
why ? They always see us respectably sad and
becomingly grave, and think that the facial
appearance, is an index to our habitual frame of
mind ; not knowing that a grave exterior may
cover a heart brimming over with jollity and con-
tentment. Show me a country where Under-
taking is flourishing, and I can show you one that
is prosperous and happy. Where undertakers are
oftenest pulling long'faces, at remunerative rates
per pull, you will see most smiles and happiness.
Why is this? Because a nation must be pro-
sperous to be able to pay our bills, which
are designedly made large in order to take
people’s thoughts off all their other sorrows,
and thus to relieve a little the weight of public
woe, iis it is our duty as men and Christians to do.
The immortal Burns says ‘Man was made to
mourn.’ What he evidently meant was ‘ Man was
paid to mourn.’ (A voice— 4 10 s. 6d. a head;
weepers extra.’ Loud applause.) So I say,
Ladies and Gentlemen, I scorn and despise the
slanders that are flung at us by ignorant people.
Let them say we are a glum and sorrowful crowd
—let them say ! If he laughs best who laughs last
then we laugh best of all. For we end all; we are
the apotheosis of sentiment; we consider every-
body’s feelings ; and when we send in the bill—
that’s where the laugh comes in. (Cheers.) So
I ask you to drink success to our glorious profes-
sion. I say ‘ glorious profession ’ because, the
paths that we daily travel are paths of glory ;
seeing that 4 the paths of glory lead but to the
grave.’ Now, “ Success to Undertaking !”
The toast was drunk with musical honors; after
which all joined in singing 44 We won’t go home
in mourning.”
The picnickers returned to Sydney at 6 p.m. in
a special funeral train, having spent a delightful
day. ' , ,

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
M.L.A* Browne moved an amendment on
Clause 3of the Queensland Protection of Abori-
gines’ Bill to the effect that no permit (to em-
ploy aborigines) be issued to any Asiatic or
African alien. Looks like protecting the blacks,
but Foxton alleges that a Thursday Island depu-
tation to him on the matter consisted wholly of
white employers who wanted to secure a mono-
poly of aboriginal labor. Well, let them secure
the nigger while they can, for he’ll soon be beyond
the reach of monopolists—European or alien.
There is this to be said in favor of the
Lvne Government’s proposed Cobar-Broken Hill
railway. The western lines of N.S.W pay on the
whole, despite the apparent hopelessness of the
outlook, and it isn’t quite certain that the new
enterprise will be a loss. It looks like being one,
yet other sections equally hopeless in appearance,
have turned out well. The western lines are
cheap to build, cheap to work, and though popu-
lation is thin, they have an immense area of
country to draw business from. The Cobar-
Broken Hill line will have about 20 counties to
support it, and they ought to scrape up enough
business among tbem to pay for axle-grease, if
the management is careful with the grease.
Under the Night.
All upon the water’s faoe the peaoe of God was
brooding ;
Washless the silent rooks, glittering and white :
Whence came the tiger thought shamefully in-
truding ?
Wherefore the red deed ruining the night?
O there and ho there !
What shames the water-waste ?
Who feels a ghostly thrall ? Who heard a woman
call?
Oars in the row-locks, and haste, comrades, haste .
Yon goes a black craft round a headland creep-
ing; , i
One sits where two sat —row, my comrades, row .
O, the little fishes, a-flashing and a-leaping!
Would they make a merry time did the fishes
know?
Hark ye and mark ye !
There where the shadows float—
Heard?ye a gurgling sound ? Saw ye a swirling-
round? . . .
Silent the sullen rocks and empty the boat!
Wherefore the stormy deed in this joyous
weather ?
White sails the royal moon, a silver ship above ;
Back oars, my comrades all, and back oars
together !
Strange deeds are done in the sacred name of
Love.
Sighs here and cries there !
Full of sobs and moans the place ;
Winds are on the wing again, all aheave the placid
plain ...
Hither comes a gale of gales to scour the water’s
face 1
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN
Fancies.
I.—The Gouien Years.
The old creek sings of the Golden Years
The dull lagoon, the oft-trod way,
The bending reeds, have voice to-day;
The ring-barked trees are dead, or dying
Gray, spectral forms —I catch their sighing ;
The lazy swans are homeward flying
They tell me, too, of the Golden Years,
Their fluted notes are as sad as tears.
Ah ! then his soul was holy ground,
Where not a vagrant tare was found.
’Twas sweet with blushing Love to wander ;
The joyous heart of Life to ponder ;
His song had thrill, and Love grew fonder ;
But where —ah ! where are those Golden Years ?
lI.— A Winter’s Reaping.
A Hermit sits in his dungeon cold,
In darkness crying for the light,
When all the world around is bright,
And Friends are true and Love lies weeping ;
He finds no soothe of rest in sleeping;
Alone he mourns a Winter’s Reaping
Of faded flowers in a garden old ;
He digs up dross where he buries gold !
Of faded flowers that hermit sings,
And strikes a harp of fretful strings.
He had his kindly years of Gladness;
He had one ashen night of Madness ;
He has his withering days of Sadness.
The flowers all fade when the heart grows cold.
HI.— A Voice.
Come out and sing with the birds of Spring I
They call again to you, to me :
What might have been, perchance may be.
The Night works but the Day’s refining ;
The Light of Love hath no declining,
Through mists of Earth may dim its shining.
How green the leaves from the ashes spring !
How sweet the scents that the night-winds bring 1
It may be ours to wring from pain
A goodly recompense of gain.
Come out and sing while Love is waiting ;
In wild-flower air the birds are mating,
Their ecstacies in song relating.
Come lie with me in the lap of Spring !
V.
ANDREW SARD.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 4, 1899.
Prick Gd.
The latest manifestation of this
’Anglo-American amicableness is made in the
semi-official announcement that the Govern-
ment of the . United States only remains
neutral with respect to the British-Boer war
so long as no other Power interferes between
the combatants. The meaning of this is,
assumedly, that if another Power does inter-
fere the United States will claim a right to
come into the quarrel and see that Britain
gets fair play—in other words, that she may
■become a British ally.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 4, 1899.
War Literature and Other Matters.
Evek since these Seven Provinces resolved to
rise up and hit an enemy who already had the
biggest empire in the world on top of him,
and whose entire numbers (men, women, and
children) are about equal to the population of
Adelaide, or about three times that of the little
town of Auckland, or about one-third of that of
Melbourne or Sydney —ever since this valiant re-
solution was arrived at, the pouring forth of anti-
Boer sentiment in Australasia has been a misery
and a great flood. There is something pitiable in
the hysteria which builds so much frenzy on an
unpleasantness with a handful of Dutch farmers.
And when it is remembered that there are in
England alone (saying nothing of Scotland, Ire-
land, Wales, and all the rest of the empire) 17
towns, the smallest of which contains more people
than the Boers of the Transvaal and the Free State
put together, the hysteria looks all the more
squalid. The literature which this hysteria has
produced is about as abject as the cause thereof.
The average man isn’t logical, and he is least logi-
cal when he is shaking a large battle-axe in the
columns of a newspaper, and calling out for Blood.
And the country which never saw any real war,
and never did any real fighting, and never waved
anything in actual strife more desperate than a
cotton umbrella, is much more desperate when it
starts at this kind of literature than one which
knows something about real campaigning and
which has waved much deadlier things than the
harmless seven-and-sixpenny brolly. Tasmania,
which is sending about 125 men to help get on top
of the Dutchman when he is down, cheers about
125 times louder than Britain which is sending
some 80,000 troops.
* * * *
The indictment which the press of Australasia
lias presented against the Boers is very large and
very angry. Also it is very loose and promiscuous.
Melbourne Age, for example, in its leading article
of 21st Oct., goes on like this :
As it has been with agriculture, so also has it been with
minerals, which are of surprising richness, not alone in
diamonds and gold, but in coal and copper and iron.
Those things might have lain under the feet of the Boers
till the Day of Judgment, and no human being would
have found employment or benefit from them. Yet
within a decade and a half of the first chance diamond
being picked up at Kimberley the Consolidated de Beers
Company was annually taking diamonds out of the
ground to the value of £3,250,000, and paying 12J per
cent, on its capital to its shareholders, besides disbursing
from £7 to £3 12s. per week to 1261 European workmen,
and from 30s. to 17s. 6d. per week for 5250 natives.
With the coalfields it was just the same. Coal is found
in Cape Colony, in Orange Free State, in the Transvaal,
a u j n Natal - And > what is more, it is found in great
abundance. But the Boers would never have found it,
or, if they had, would never have used it. The backward-
ness of its development may be gauged from the follow-
ing comparisons, given in Worsfold’s book on South
Africa:—
Estimated Sq. Miles Annual Output
„ , of Coal Fields. in Tons.
South Africa 56,000 ... 600,000
New South Wales ... 25,000 ... 3,500,000
United Kingdom ... 4,000 ... 180,000,’000
That table shows the difference in placing great natural
resources within reach of the unprogressive Boer and of
the enterprising Anglo-Saxon. It was just the same with
the gold. . . . In the interests of humanity, the Boer has
no more right to lock up these great resources of nature
than had the Bantu race which he displaced.
By the Age’s own showing, the Boer hasn’t looked
these things up ; the biggest diamond output in
the world started on Boer territory, and the biggest
gold output in the world is on Boer territory now,
and, with these riches being dug out as fast as
men can extract them, the hysterical Age wants
to go to war with the Boer for “ locking them up.”
And the same paper points out that in South
Africa, where Britain bosses about four times as
much territory as the two republics put together,
coal-mining is shamefully neglected; and, as
the Boer has neglected coal-mining in the
Transvaal for about 50 years, he should be thrown
cut to make room for the Anglo-Saxon, who has
neglected it at the Cape for about 90 years.
The smallness of the South African coal output is
all put down to the Boer, as if he owned all South
Africa. _ And if the fact that the Boer slept on
top of his gold and coal deposits for 50 years shows
that he should be fired out of his own country,
where does the energetic Anglo-Saxon of Wes-
tralia come in ? He slept and dreamed and grew
a few sheep or a bull or two on top of his coal and
gold deposits for longer than the Boer did, and he
is dreaming yet while other people dig his minerals
for him. And he wasn’t distracted, as the Boer
was, by almost ceaseless war, and forced into isola-
tion. And the Boer, at all events, has done about
as well with his country as the energetic Anglo-
Saxon has with the Northern Territory, or with
Newfoundland. In Newfoundland the Anglo-
Saxon has dreamed for 300 years on top of magni-
ficent coal, iron, gold, silver and copper deposits—•
and lived miserably on fish. Isn’t his day of
ejection due ?
* * * *
Sundry N.S.W. papers rise in the same “train
to say that the Boer’s neglect to develope the
great resources of his country shows that he
should make room for the more active Anglo-
Saxon, who will do so. Which is not so
convincing as it might be, coming from
New South Wales, which owns most of the only
great river system in Australia, yet hardly irri-
gates an acre of ground ; which can’t build a single
mile of railway or do a single public work unless
Britain lends the money; and whose great gold
mines, silver mines, and even coal mines are
largely owned by more energetic people abroad.
And all over Australia indignant Jingo papers
complain that the Boers are not moral, and there-
fore should be wiped out. The Boer’s alleged
lack of morality is a regrettable circumstance,
though there is no evidence of it available in Aus-
tralia except the assertion of these papers. But
seeing that, according to statist Coghlan’s latest
information, it seems that half the young women of
N.S.W. are not moral in the conventional sense
of the term, and there is no reason to believe the
other provinces are any better, and considering
that in Brisbane one out of every seven births is
illegitimate, and making due allowance for the
hideous frequency of incest and the like horrors in
this great country, Australia ought to be able to
speak with some authority upon immorality. It
speaks, at all events, as one having a large know-
ledge of the subject.
* * * m
But the loudest curse of the people who want to
go out hunting after Boer-meat is that it is neces-
sary to put down a lawless and brutal race, and to
protect the British population of the Transvaal.
Wherefore the changes are persistently rung on a
few weary anecdotes. Some time ago one Eng-
lishman was shot in a scuffle; also, a long time
ago one Englishwoman was murdered by some
person or persons who were never brought to
justice. Likewise there have been casual scram-
bles, insults, and disagreements in the streets,
and some people have been assaulted and hustled.
Further, it was alleged by the papers that,
after _ the war started, the Boers blew up
a train containing women and children, though
the statement afterwards proved to be a
lie; and another incredible report, which was
never confirmed, told of the Boers spitting on
female refugees. The indignant literature which
has been spread on these matters in Australasia
alone would cover acres. Queensland, which can
show about 25 unpunished murders within the last
few years, and where a policeman was recently
chai’ged with putting out a man’s eye in a prin-
cipal street of Brisbane, has been very angry.
Melbourne and Sydney, the home of the brutish
larrikin who frequently “deals it out ” with a
bottle to the stranger in the streets at night, are
both of opinion that the Transvaal must be a
lawless place, and that the country needs to be
annexed by a strong, law-abiding race. They
have been interrupted a good deal in coming to
this conclusion by the frequent news of “another
push outrage,” and information about how the
ribs of some inoffensive person were stove
in and his head danced on, but it sized up the
evidence and resolved that the Transvaal wasn’t a
safe place to live in. For one outrage in Johan-
nesburg proves that the Boer isn’t fit for self-
government ; while eight outrages in Melbourne
only prove that the force wants strengthening by
the addition of two extra constables at 6s. 6d. a
day. Apropos, the writer of this knows a lady
who lived for a good many years in the Transvaal,
and the very worst thing that happened to her was
snake-bite. Said snake-bite is not set down to
Boer misgovernment. And within a few months
after her arrival in Sydney she was assaulted and
robbed in broad daylight in Surry Hills.
* * * *
But though the indignation has been general,
Maoriland has been, perhaps, the most justly in-
furiated of all the provinces. It was also the only
province where the Premier sang the National An-
them in a hoarse voice all on his own at a public
meeting, and thereby brought his grey hairs down
in sorrow to the level of a street barrel-organ. The
Maoriland press points out strongly as a reason
for interference that the revenue raised in the
Transvaal is burdensome and extortionate (it is
£5 10s. per inhabitant per annum, against Maori-
land’s own £7 2s. Id.), and is “far beyond the
legitimate requirements of the country.” Also,
the Maoriland press has gone mad over the fact that
the big absentee gold-mining companies of the
Transvaal contribute such a very large proportion of
the revenue, and yet the aliens at Johannesburg
haven’t a vote. And it speaks solemnly about
how these aliens have “ made the country.”
Which shows how different a thing may look
when viewed from two different sides. When a
foreign corporation in Johannesburg makes, say,
£500,000 a year out of the strangers’ country, and
pays, say, £50,000 in direct and indirect taxation,
and has £450,000 for itself, it is one of the props
of the country, and ought to have a big share in
the government because of the £50,000 a year it
leaves in the country as a contribution to the
revenue. And when it does the same thing on a
much smaller scale in Maoriland it is just a plain,
ordinary, accursed absentee, which should receive
no consideration at all because of the money it
drains out of the country, so Maoriland hits it
with an absentee tax and speaks of it as an incu-
bus. And Maoriland, which prohibits the alien
from even digging gum until a lengthy period has
elapsed after his arrival, is grieved—deeply grieved
—because the alien in the Transvaal isn’t abso-
lutely on a level with the burgher. And the M.L.
Lower House which approved of a Bill for the
most wholesale and general exclusion of every kind
of immigrant that the Government could possibly
regard as undesirable, was shocked beyond expres-
sion to hear that the Boer Government exercises
the power to expel persons who are regarded as
undesirable citizens.*
* * * *
The anti-Boer literature of Australasia is a
lamentable thing, taking it all round. It is mostly
a bitter complaint that the Boer does exactly what
the people of this country do every day, only they
call it one thing when the Boer does it, and
another when they do it themselves, and they don’t
notice that it is the same thing all the time.
Australasia tries to ruin the commerce of her
suzerain by heavy duties, just as much as the
Boers do. It refuses aliens a vote, just as they
do. It takes aliens without giving them any
Parliamentary representation, just as they do.
When it objects to any special variety of alien,
regarding him as in any way dangerous to the
State, it. goes even further than the Boer, for it
refuses him naturalisation on any terms whatever.
It refuses to let the alien be taught his own lan-
guage in the public-schools. It is more lawless
than Boer-land, only instead of calling its law-
lessness an indubitable evidence of its own inca-
pacity for self-government and of the necessity for
its subjugation by a superior race, it merely
alludes to it as “ larrikin disturbance. ” But the
wildest humor of the situation lies in the attempt
of the Australian to convince himself that he is
lashing himself into this military fury out of pure
love for, and attachment and loyal devotion to,
Britain. Australasia on the whole loves Britain so
well that if it can oblige Britain by doing anything
that it would much rather do in any case, tnen it
is more than willing to oblige Britain by doing it.
And the cordiality about ends there. Britain
asked Australia in bygone days to kindly make
room in its vasty wastes for a few shiploads of
convicts, and Australia promptly and justly
replied that it wouldn’t. Britain further asked
Australia to admit British goods free to its
markets, and the affectionate son again said he
wouldn’t. The mother-country suggested that
Australia might at least give its goods a preference
(it is a concession which would cost Australia
nothing, for where a province now charges 25 per
cent, on British and German goods alike, a
preference could be given to the former by putting
the duty on German goods up to 40 per cent.);
and Australia, so far, has cordially replied that it
would see its aged mother hanged first. It was
requested to let some of the “submerged tenth”
settle on Australian soil, and it replied that it
wasn’t taking any British cripples. Two of the
Australias go out of their way to specially tax the
British capitalist here as an absentee and a curse
and a parasite. The Briton who comes out here
is taxed from the day of his arrival, but he is an
unclean being, and not fit to vote until he has been
purified by a considerable period of residence. In
several of the provinces he is declared quite unfit
to enter the Upper House until he has been puri-
fied by a very long period of residence. When the
consumptive British citizen comes here to recover
his lost lung, some at least of the provinces tell
him to go and die elsewhere —which is brutal
language to throw at any invalid. Australia cer-
tainly pays a small sum for naval protection, but it
is a very small sum, and paid most reluctantly. If
there is any case when Australia’s alleged affec-
tion for Britain induced it to do anything for
Britain’s gratification and advantage that it
didn’t want to do, the address of that case is quite
unknown at this office.
* * * *
But when Britain invited sundry Australian
politicians to go to London and have a lovely razzle-
dazzle and holiday at Jubilee time, these politicians
rose up unanimously and said that blood was thicker
than water, and that owing to the deep affection
which Australia bore to England, they felt it
their duty to go and have a good time. And when
sundry politicians found an opportunity to adver-
tise themselves and probably get titles, by sending
a contingent to the Transvaal; and when a lot of
people saw a chance of a free trip, and the plea-
sant excitement of travel, and good pay, by being
a contingent; and the sporting instinct of a com-
munity which loves to go out and shoot some-
thing saw some excitement in view; and there
was the joy of wearing a uniform and a feather,
and letting off a gun with a loud bang ; and the
commercial community saw in the contingent a
good opportunity to put in a claim for fat army
contracts —Australia’s alleged love for Britain
blazed forth anew. Britain, so far as appears,
didn’t much want the paltry little contingent,
which will probably be rather in the way than
otherwise, but the love which Australia’s poli-
ticians bear to England urged them on to gratify
themselves inthematter, even if they did somewhat
inconvenience the alleged object of their affections
in the process. So Henry Copeland, the N.S.W.
staunch Protectionist, calls to Australia to gratify
Britain by sending a little burlesque force that
Britain apparently doesn’t care about. And in his
overwhelming loyalty he also wants to put a
heavy duty on all British products so as to keep
them out, and damage Britain’s commerce (which
she does care about) as much as possible. And
Lyne is built the same way. Ditto Seddon and
Turner, and Braddon, and Kingston and Dick-
son and Bigjohn Forrest. Ditto everybody, in
fact. Blood is thicker than water right enough—
our own blood, that is.
The Digger in the West.
It is only six years since Fat Hannan pegged
out the first Kalgoorlie claim. To-day the
Great Boulder and other co’s. are burrowing
below 700 ft., and are talking about 1000 ft.
Work that has occupied most mining fields at
least 20 years has been packed into five in the
West. The conflict of alluvial diggers and
lease owning companies formed to work lodes
is a consequence. Elsewhere when alluvial
and reef have occurred together, the “dirt”
has been skimmed off before the reef has been
seriously touched. In Westralia the leases
were taken up and big co’s. formed almost
before the dry-blower had paid the last instal-
ment on his machine. Again, J. Bull was the
principal buyer of leases; and Bull. a
passion for big tracts of country —the mi
that doesn’t consist of 80 or 100 acres is
small for him to see. Country remote from
reefs had therefore to be included to sa is >
John’s earth-hunger. All of which and more
made things worse than usual for the digger,
and rendered a clear law with regard to bis an
the leaseholder’s respective rights all the more
necessary if conflicts were to be avoided. Anc
the law was as clear as mud. First, however,
there was no law. Then, in ’94, the law salt
that the digger might go on lands that were
applied for but not granted under 1 ha ,
of course, gave him only a few months’ grace.
Then, in Oct., ’95, the law said :
Any miner may, subject to the regulations, enter upon
any land the subject of any application for lease already
made or hereafter to be made, or the subject of any 'ease
granted after the passing of this Act which is not held
under miner’s right, to within 50 feet of any reef.
The average digger who read that and tried to
understand it heard strange noises in his head
and saw specks before his eyes, and had other
symptoms ; so, without struggling with it, lie
proceeded to dig up to within 50 feet of any
reef.. Meantime, however, another difficulty
arose What constitutes alluvial ? as
“ pug ” alluvial ? There were good arguments
on either side. One thing certain was that the
cos. had in no case been formed to work this
sort of formation. That fact was recognised,
and the trouble was patched up.
* * * *
But the diggers had hardly got their second
wind when the provision quoted was flung at
them. The wardens held that it meant
that diggers could not go on to any land
the lease of which had been granted before
Oct., ’95. This practically shut them off three-
fourths of the oldest and richest parts of the
fields. An appeal was taken to the Full Court;
and the Warden was upheld. The diggers say
that this interpretation is in the teeth of the
intention of Parliament ; and mostly for that
reason, but partly for a few others that have
squeezed in sideways, there have been
“demonstrations,” and some hotheads have
been mentioning Eureka Stockade. The Bul-
letin does not know what Parliament’s inten-
tion was. But if Parliament meant, as the
Full Court holds it did, that “ any miner may
enter upon land the subject of any application
now pending or to be made in the future and on
any lease to be granted hereafter,” this paper,
in its charity, believes Parliament would have
said so. What, however, it did say, clumsily
no doubt, was that it didn’t matter
whether the application had been made
in the past or was to be lodged in the
future—the digger might still go on. The
Court, however, apparently holds that when
an application has been once dealt with it is
not an “ application already made ” any longer.
In the same way, presumably, when a young-
ster grows into a man he ceases to have been
born. It is, possibly, a fact that an applica-
tion that has been dealt with ceases to be an
application ; but the fact that an application
has been dealt with does not alter the other
fact that the application was made. But The
Bulletin is not a Groper Full Court. ’S’onah
Onslow,, who is part of it, declared that “ in
the absence of very positive language on the
part of the Legislature, this Court must not
curtail the rights of the lessees ” ; and Brother
Stone also thought that “ the Legislature has
omitted to provide in express words for the
alluvial miner in regard to leases granted
before the passing of this Act.” Still, Judge
Stone had some doubt about it apparently, for
he said that “ if the Legislature has not used
plain language ... it is our duty ... to
give the leaseholder the benefit of the doubt.”
And the third Learned Brother concurred.
* * * *
If the alluvial miner were a parasite
this extreme solicitude for the leaseholder
mostly a big company—might be under-
standable. Bub though the digger is not,
as a rule, much afflicLed with the sorrows
of wealth, he is a person of very great
account to the community. He is the lease-
holders’ John the Baptist—he goes into the
wilderness and lives on tinned dog and grubs,
and things, locusts and wild honey not being
plentiful in those parts. Men who know, men
like Manager Hamilton, of the Boulder, are
his readiest champions. They know that to
the men who rocked the cradles on Bendigo
and Ballarat Victoria owes the great and per-
manent industry which set her on her feet, and
has kept her up several times since when she
has been very groggy ; and they know that the
man with the shaker and the blower has done
the same for Westralia. It may seem a smart
thing to take from these men what has been
the recognised reward of their labors in America,
and in every province of Australia —to lock
up the alluvial from them so soon as their
followers, the men who are after the
reefs, show on the scene. But if that
is the game then the digger will give his
old occupation best; and he would truly be a
fool to do months of “perish” in order that
others might come and reap. If there is any
doubt, then, it is the digger who should have the
benefit of it, both as a matter of public policy and
on custom, which is no doubt founded on public
policy. Still, as the Full Court does not think
so and has used its opinion to hack up its read-
ing of the clause, the sooner Parliament makes
its meaning clear the better. And then, Par-
liament having made its meaning clear, if
public opinion is still dissatisfied, all it can do
is to sit. down and wait for a general election—
or, better still, stand up and wait. And if it
goes to sleep when the general election heaves
in sight, as it has done before—well, that will
simply be its own funeral again.
The Disgrace of the Transvaal War.
On Saturday last crowds lined the streets of
Sydney to watch the passage of the N.S.W.
soldiers who are being sent to South Africa to
fight the Boers. There were flags, and cheers,
and the music of rniiitary bands, as through
the pouring rain the men marched for embarka-
tion. And every man who marched, every
man who cheered, added another dark spot to
the dark stain of dishonor with which this ex-
pedition, and the other expeditions like it,
blot the scutcheon of Australia.


For what is their justification ? what is their
defence? There is none worth the name.
Australia has no quarrel with the Transvaal.
Australia has no excuse for becoming a catspaw
of greedy Britain. Australia, with a military
force raised for defence, not defiance, should be
the last country in the world to take arms
against Boer patriots fighting for wife and child
and home, national freedom and independence.
* * * •
Let British speculators gorge themselves with
plunder if they will. Let British politicians
bring town after town, and State after State,
and country after country, within the Imperial
clutch. Let the British drum roll ever round the
world, with its echoes of cant and tyranny and
bloodshed. What affair is it of ours ?—save
to express contempt for the methods, detesta-
tion of the means. If we sympathise, let us
at least sympathise with the patriot, not with
the bully ; let us assist the men who fight for
their fatherland, not the men hired to realise a
bloodstained dream of worldwide empire.
• * * *
The Australias are nominally vassals of the
British Empire ; but they owe it no more than
a nominal allegiance. There is upon us no
constitutional incumbency to defend that
Empire, tottering beneath its burden of ill-
gotten spoils ; no incumbency to aid it in
stealing their country from the Boers in the
Transvaal. Nor is there need : the navies and
armies of Britain are more than sufficient for
the destruction of 30,000 Boers. Without con-
stitutional compulsion, without the sentimental
excuse of necessity, we are sending troops to
aid Britain in an unjust war. For the quibble
of the franchise has already been thrown aside;
and Britain’s reasons are set forth with naked
effrontery—revenge for Majuba ; greed for the
Rand mines; extension of the Empire in
Africa, at whatever cost of human life. They
are reasons discreditable to Britain ; but that
Australia should yield to them is infinitely
more discreditable.
» * * •
The Sydney crowds were delighted with the
spectacle, and deplored the depressing rain.
Ihey have not to pay the piper. That is the
share of the men on the land—the wool-growers,
farmers, miners, and other producers—from
whose industry at the last comes every penny
squandered to gratify Jingo politicians, or to
amuse the Sydney crowd. In this matter, as
all others, the bushmen have no voice. Over-
grown Sydney decides; and Sydney news-
papers. And the metropolis’s lust for sensa-
tion, the military desire for action and dis-
tinction, have led the country to a folly and a
shame worse than those which accompanied the
Soudan contingent.
• • • •
Who believes that the Australian troops are
going for love of Britain ? They are going for
excitement, for change, for vainglory and the
tawdry lustre of military honor ; and because
the man who professes to be a soldier, who is
trained to the soldiering business, naturally
wishes to test his courage and his weapons.
Without war, he conceives that he has wasted
the time spent in preparing for war. It is to
the undying disgrace of Australia that the
Governments should have yielded to the
military clamour—in such a shameful cause.
* * * *
History, proud to chronicle the defence of
the little Swiss republic against the empire
of Austria, will not fail to mark the dis-
honor to the British Empire of the similar war
of aggression against the brave little republic of
the Transvaal. And though Britain may extend
her empire farther than Rome dreamed of, may
depopulate States and destroy communities in
her campaigns for Greed and “ Glory,” upon
her also each fresh aggression will recoil with
ruinous force, and each new conquest will carry
with it the seeds of deserved disaster. The
measure which Britain metes to weaker peoples
will some day be meted to her. Her Goth will
come.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
On. Brigands and Things in General.
“ C.J.” empties forth his contempt for British
righteousness, Australian loyalty and Holman’s
pluck:—
Chamberlain told the Yankees that the British inter-
ference in the Transvaal wa3 similar to that of the great
U.S.A. in Cuba. The U.S. interfered there to release the
reeoncentrados from the intolerable tyranny of the.
Spaniards, and they did so by blockading the island and
promptly starving the reeoncentrados out of existence.
Britain has gone to war to redress the grievances of the
Uitlandersand has ruined most of them in the proceeding.
Truly it isn’t as bad as the great U.S.A. example, but it
is bad enough. Holman complained that the war is un-
just. Nearly every war that the British have fought has
been. The scrap that Hengist and Horsa forced upon
Vortigern was unjust, but it was the first day of the
English in England. John Morley’s earnest appeal to
his countrymen not to be a nation of brigands was mere
rubbish. His nation IS AND ALWAYS HAS BEEN a
nation of brigands. The Brigand Nation in fact. We
started in the Imperial business by robbery and murder
on the Spanish Main (previous attempts were mostly
failures) and we made our national fortune by the whole-
sale and complete robbery of India, which was accom-
plished by more awful murdering. England herself is
stolen property, and the whole empire the result of
robbery-under-arms, and Morley appeals to his
countrymen not to be a nation of brigands ! Surely
the little man was talking sarcastically. It is our nature
and nothing to be ashamed of, and this writer is quite
proud of the fact that he can go to no part of the world
without meeting a man to whom he can say pleasantly,
“Oh yes, I know you, we did you bad at So-and-so,
and got away with a good swag! ” The other
nations would have done it if they could, and when
they get the chance they will make up for lost
time by devouring us—the boss robbers of all
time. We must eat or be eaten, and the only unpleasant
thing about it is that whenever we eat anyone up we tell
him it is for his good and not ours, and that it is only a
high sense of moral duty that compels us to do it—and,
worst of all, we tell ourselves the same old lie and make
ourselves believe it. It is time this race knew its real
character and kept the cant for the outside world. Let
us be frank to ourselves—but the truth of it is that the
Briton can't help snuffling arid canting. He has a small
but tougb-in-the-fibre conscience, and it worries him,
and the only way he can keep it quiet is to drug it with
cant. Therefore he cants horribly. He declares that
his plundering expeditions are to set the captive free
and spread the Gospel of Christ, and when he can’t
make his conscience drunk he pleads that the spirit is
strong and the flesh is weak, and asks pardon for the sin
he is about to commit and cannot live without com-
mitting. With a “God forgive me” on his lips he sends a
hundred thousand nigs, to Hades, and kneels down and
asks for Heaven’s forgiveness alongside the couch of his
friend’s wife.
The Briton is wiping out the Boers to-day chiefly be-
cause there are a few' hundred millions of tons of treasure
in the land which he feels he cannot live without, and
because he can never forgive the common farmers who
beat him black-and-blue at Majuba Hill and Laing’s Nek.
As for Australian loyalty, we are indeed the sons of our
fathers. We shout that we are sending this handful of
troops to show our devotion to the dear old Motherland.
Rats ! We are sending them as carpet-baggers. They
are merely the latest style of Australian Slate com-
mercial-travellers. We are sending them to take their
chance of death by bullet and dysentery because loyalty
to the Empire just now means fat orders for Australian
produce for the invading army in South Africa. There is
going to be a boom in tinned meats, chilled beef and
mutton, fodder, flour, bacon, and many other things that
men and horses eat and drink. We can afford to spend a
few hundred thousand sovs. to get those fat orders and
the splendid advertisement, and lay broad and deep the
foundations of a permanent market. When this “ job ”
is over, someone may rise up in London and point
out that the true Imperial policy is to do without
Chicago stuff and feed the army entirely on Canadian
and Australian produce. Hurrah for loyalty when it
means a boom in tinned dog. All the talk about the Dear
Old Motherland stops suddenly when there is no boom of
guns in places where Australian fodder and tinned dingo
are in big demand. In the piping times o t peace, when
the naval contribution is before Parliament—well, it
gets through, but utterly without enthusiasm and very
grudgingly. But for the fact that it is looked upon as a
sort of national war-insurance and someone benefits a
little by the expenditure, it would probably have been
blocked before now. There is not one scrap of loyal en-
thusiasm about paying the naval contribution.
It was quite refreshing to hear Holman get up in the
House and declare that because he believed the empire to
which he belonged was wrong he hoped it would get
licked. He really believed that when the jingo bishop
prays to God to defend the right he means the right, and
had not grasped the full significance of the British motto
“ GOD and GUIi RIGHT.” Holman’s straight-out
declaration was like a long beer in a weary land, and
would have done him a lot of good as a man with the
courage of his opinion but for his beastly explanation.
His “ mistake ” was magnificent, but his crawl-out was
hideous. He hadn’t the courage of his opinion, and
while he failed to placate his enemies, he disgusted his
friends. In fact he made a horrible mess of it, and, instead
of a statesman, proved himself to be merely a hot-headed
youth whose eloquence runs away with him. All the
hysterical whitewash that, Henry Lawson and the rest
of the revolutionary poets can pour over him won’t cover
the ugly fact that he is a most eloquent young man who
can be upset by the most casual, straight-forward, obvious
question, and that having kicked current-opinion right
in the diaphragm he is frightened at the row that proves
how much the animal is hurt. Holman’s explanation
that what he meant was that in the long duel between
the Boers and the Chartered Co. he hoped the Boers would
win, was the thinnest and most miserable thing he ever
attempted. It was not proposed to send troops to aid the
Chartered Co. and the C.C. was not even mentioned till
Holman dragged it in for his own damnation. That
“ explanation ” and Henry Lawson’s apology are suffi-
cient to convince everybody that Holman is a blank revo-
lutionary cartridge- that he and his friends feel con-
vinced that he has committed some sort of crime, and that,
crime or no crime, having said an unpopular thing, he
had not the courage to stick to it. Still with all his fail-
ings this writer would sooner see N.S.W. Assembly filled
with failures like him than contain two such jingoes as
—ooh !— Sleath and Meagher.

The White Slaves of Victoria.
A Bendigo mining co. (and not a particularly
poor one either) was, through its manager,
charged the other day with failing to provide
decent ventilation. The Govt, doctor presented
a long report on what he had found in the
workings. The surface temperature was
60deg. At 2415 ft. the temperature was
86deg. In a winze below that it was
90deg. At 150 ft. in another winze the tem-
perature was 96deg. “ The air was abso-
lutely still, except when the rock-drill was
working. The sense of breathlessness was
almost unbearable, bordering on suffoca-
tion. ... A wild and irregular pulse. ... A
throbbing sense of confusion about the temples. ”
In more than one working he found a diminu-
tion in the amount of oxygen in the air and an
increase of carbonic acid —“ the gravest evil
to which a practical miner can possibly be
exposed, and to which he mostly succumbs.”
The doctor (Burke Gaffney) “examined men
working both in the levels and the winzes.
They were not engaged in laborious or violent
work. In each case both the respiratory move-
ments and cardiac impulses were very consider-
ably increased beyond the normal limits. Each
man examined had a peculiarly uneven and
irregular pulse, and a most shallow respiratory
movement.” A heart in a normal atmosphere
contracts 32,000 times in eight hours; the
doctor says that the contractions in such an
atmosphere as this would amount to over 50,000
in the period. By way of comment on this, a
couple of days after it was written a man
dropped dead at his work in a neighboring
mine. This particular co. was not fined or
anything of the sort; it was, instead, given
three months in which to improve conditions,
though it was admitted that six months pre-
viously the department had given notice that
something would have to be done. Meantime,
the work in the bad sections has been knocked
off; and it is added that the men complain of
being thrown out ! What a workman’s paradise
indeed in which the power that closes such
places as these is cursed by the workmen them-
selves !
Bussell’s Council Beform.
Maoriland Tory party goes to the country
at the ensuing general election with a pro-
gramme in ten items, whereof Item 1 sets forth
that the said Tory party advocates “ Reform of
the Legislative Council.” It is further explained
that the improvement is to consist in making
the Council elective instead of nominee—which
is a most valuable reform, if the Council is made
elective the right way. If it is made elective
the wrong way then “ reform of the Legislative
Council ” may make the Council twenty
times less reformed than it is now. At
present, the Council doesn’t represent the
people and has no mandate from the people.
Its members are appointed by the Ministry,
for seven years ; so they have more than twice
as long a lease of power as the people’s repre-
sentatives ; and, as they are salaried, the ap-
pointments are a most useful piece of Minis-
terial patronage. If it is proposed to
make Councillors elective by universal
suffrage then the Tory party’s proposed
reform is a reform. If it is proposed to
make them elective on a property-qualification
or any other kind of restricted or Fat Man
franchise, then the proposed reform is merely a
device whereby to surreptitiously fasten up the
Maoriland people with chains that they may not
be able to get rid of in the next 50 years. The
party which says it is in favor of an elective
Council without saying what kind of election is
to be resorted to is just in the position of the
doctor who promises to relieve a patient’s suf-
ferings, but doesn’t say whether he intends
doing it by curing the patient or by killing him.
Premier Seddon, meanwhile, says that he is
against the elective-Couucil idea, and favors
either a continuance of the nominee system, or
else no Council at all. And one of his principal
organs, the Canterbury Times, backs him up
thus :—
Perhaps the best reform, as the Premier himself
suggested, would be to reform the Council out of
existence altogether. The elective system has not
proved, a success in other countries, and there is
no reason to suppose that it would do so here. If
the two Houses of Parliament were elected, as they
ought to be, on the most perfect franchise and the most
desirable term that can be devised, they would be practi-
tieally the same Chamber. To make one franchise more
liberal than the other, or one term longer than the other,
would be to create an artificial difference which could not
be logically defended. It is not easy, indeed, if we must
have a Legislative Council, to suggest any great improve-
ment upon the present method of appointing its mem-
bers.
This is the argument against an elective
Council put in the shortest possible space—and
it is a fool of an argument. Its reasoning is :
Because the existing elective Councils, chosen
on a narrow property franchise which makes
them representative of the rich only, are a
failure, therefore all elective Councils must be
a failure and the idea should be avoided. If
these provinces had reasoned in the same foolish
way when they were ruled by Assemblies
elected by a property franchise, and decided
that, as a property-franchise-elected Lower
House was a failure, then every kind of Lower
House, no matter how elected, must also be a
failure, they would have got back by now to.
the status of Crown colonies, or else to anarchy.
The nominee Council is certainly a failure, and
no really civilised country has any use for
such an institution. The property-elected
Council is just as bad as a property-
elected Assembly. The idea of having
no Council at all means leaving the whole power
in the hands of the road-and-bridge fiend in
the Lower House. The idea of having the
Council elected on the same plan as the Lower
House means having two Houses full of road-
and-bridge fiends instead of one. The idea
that has never been tried yet—though it will
be as soon as the Federal Parliament starts—is
that of having an Upper House elected by the
most liberal possible suffrage, but by the whole
province voting as one constituency. That is
the only vacant billet that a democratic Council
can fill; and it is a billet that badly wants
filling. As things are, the only place in Maori-
land which has no Parliamentary representa-
tive is Maoriland. There is the member who
•is returned for Waihemo, and whose duty it is
to watch over the interests and culverts of
Waihemo; and the similar members for Wai-
taki and Waipawa, and Wairau, and Waite-
mata—but there isn’t a soul who is returned to
watch over the interests of Maoriland as a whole.
It is a piecemeal business ; instead of having
an army of 100,000 men to defend a country of
100,000 square miles, there is one man allotted
to defend each separate square mile as best he
can. The only way to make a useful and
absolutely democratic Council which won’t be a
mere duplicate and ditto of the Lower House,
is to make it the House that represents Maori-
land. But that, it may be safely assumed, isn’t
the kind of Council reform which the Russell
party is dreaming about. “ Reform ” is a
vague term ; some people would call a return
to absolute monarchy reform. Also “ elective
Councils” is a vague term, and it has no real
meaning until it is fully explained who is to do
the electing. The Russian Council of State,
for instance, is elective—the Czar elects it
himself.
The Maoriland press still seems to be of
opinion that Federated Australia will be only
too glad to arrange for the free exchange of
products with Maoriland, so that the latter can
have all the commercial advantages of Federa-
tion with none of the responsibilities. But the
idea is utterly hopeless. One great object of
Federation is to keep the Commonwealth’s
market for the Commonwealth. Among them
the Australian provinces can produce every-
thing of value that Maoriland produces—bar
kauri gum. And everyone of them has joined
the Commonwealth for the express purpose of
having free access to the other provinces for its
special products while the rest of the world is
shut out. The Commonwealth, in fact, says to
each separate State : “ Join us, and we under-
take that if there is anything we want to buy,
and you have it to sell, we will buy from you in
preference to outsiders, and will only buy from
outsiders when you have no more to sell.”
That is what Freetrade inside the Common-
wealth and Protection against the outside world
mean. And the Commonwealth isn’t likely to
make a present of its market to any outsider.
Australia has always bought more from Maori-
land than the latter province has bought from
Australia, so Australia will be a gainer if the
trade closes up. Why should it reciprocate ?
N.S. Wales Socialistic organisation lately
uttered a blast against the degenerate Eight-
Hour demonstration of the day, gibbeting it as
a prostitution to the advertiser. And it is
time to think wheb a country paper talks like
this of the local display in a way-back N.S.W.
town, reckoned a strong Labor centre :—
The procession was only redeemed from absolute
failure and contempt by the brave display made by the
local brewery, whose lorry, heavily laden with hogsheads
and small casks of the excellent beer which Mr.
has made the most popular drink in the town, bore aloft
a bold emblematic figure of a red lion, whose kingly pose
seemed to indicate scorn for the poor brief procession.
And according to “ Mr. James’s ” weekly, some
big casks of wine were the things most worthy
of illustration in the Sydney Eight-Hour show.
The cable syndicate, by means of which the
newspapers have formed a corner in foreign
news, keeps the Australian public in ignorance
concerning any point of news but that favored
by the Melbourne Argus office, which works
the wire at the London end. If Chamberlain
gives utterance to any Jingo fustian it is cabled
out at once, but no attention is paid to such a
fine and memorable argument as the speech of
John Morley (reported in the last mail’s
papers) from the Radical point of view.
Morley protested against England be-
coming “ an empire of brigands ” —a
phrase strong enough to deserve cabling,
coming from a leader of English thought. In
fact, the cable news supplied to the Australian
public is systematically and grossly “ cooked.”
Any regular reader of English files must be
amazed, not so much at the news that is cabled,
as at the important facts that are excluded by
the Syndicate. With Federation, the time
comes for a really Radical Australian daily,
which will fight the cable-ring, not by entering
on the hopeless task of putting down pound for
pound against it, but by now and then in-
dulging in expensive messages at appropriate
times.

“ Falcon,” of Newcastle Herald, the loudest
screamer for blood and Boer-meat in N.S. W.,
and the same person who wrote to The Bul-
letin last week about its ignorance because it
didn’t make the same absurd mistakes that he
did, breaks out again like the sun from behind
a cloud :
As was stated by the writer before—that is the usual
newspaper way of talking—Majuba Hill was no criterion
of either Boer marksmanship or Boer fibre. It is an easy
thing for a couple of thousand men to pot a handful,
said handful being two hundred feet below them, but it
is quite, another matter to meet a legion of the same class
on something like even conditions and with artillery to
aid their inherent daring.
History has so far been under the impression
that the British were on the top of the hill at
Majuba and the Boers below them —but no
matter. “ Falcon ” apparently belongs to
that desperately mean variety of Englishman
who manufactures his history as he goes along
in order to malign a brave enemy, and though
the existence of that kind of mean Englishman
is a trifle rough on England in general, still
such very aggravated specimens are com-
paratively rare. Apropos, by the way,
of “ Falcon’s ” screaming letter pub-
lished last week, in which he claims that
the Transvaal is naturally British property
because it was part of the original Dutch
colony handed over to Britain, it is interesting
to note that the boundaries of that Dutch
colony as they stood at the time of the British
occupation only extended east as far as the
Great Fish River, a little east of Port Eliza-
beth ; and the furthest N.E. limit was marked
by a beacon on the bank of the Plettenburg or
Zeekoe River, where the town of Plettenburg
now stands, about 40 miles south of the Orange
Free State and 200 miles south of the Trans-
vaal. And when Victoria ascended the
throne in 1837 the boundaries were practically
the same. The jubilee maps published with
the Statesman’s Year Book for 1897 show the
situation very clearly. The yarn about the
Transvaal being a part of the original British
South African colony, graciously bestowed by
Britain on an ungrateful Boer, is about the
meanest of the many lies circulated by Jingo
liars in Australia.
♦
Just by way of letting the Australian con-
tingent man know what he is fighting for, these
are the demands of the Uitlander Council at
Johannesburg, as published last July :
1. Simple and immediate enfranchisement of all who
have lived or shall have lived in the Republic for five
years.
2. Representation in the Volksraad reasonably propor-
tioned to the electorate.
3. No distinction between the vote for the Raad and
the vote for the President and Commandant-General.
4. The re-establishment of the High Court as one
branch of the delegated sovereignty of the people.
5. The admission of English to an equality with Taal
in all public functions.
6. The abolition of all distinction between old and new
burghers, both in the central and municipal governments.
7. The restoration of all Government arms to Govern-
ment arsenals, to be re-distributed to volunteer corps,
formed according to local distribution, and without
regard to race.
8. The grant of full control of local affairs to munici-
palities and districts, reasonably determined according
to population, this to include the election of local officials.
9. Government grants in aid of education in proportion
to school attendance, and administered by local boards.
10. Local control of police.
They want to be read slowly. No 1 means the
enfranchisement of the alien majority without
any need for the alien to become naturalised,
to become a Transvaal citizen, or to abjure
allegiance to his own foreign country. No
country on earth gives a vote to the unnatural-
ised alien. This demand means the handing
over of the whole Government to foreigners
who don’t even profess a desire to become
citizens. No 2is to make dead sure, by a care-
ful redistribution of seats, that the foreign
element can command a majority in both
Houses of Parliament. The abolition of all
distinctions between old and new burghers
(clause 6) is a demand that even the Presidency
shall be thrown open to the foreigner, who
refuses to place himself under any allegiance
to the State. Clause 7is a demand for the dis-
arming of the national army and the arming in
its stead of foreigners and citizens indis-
criminately. No. 10 (local control of police)
is a thing unknown even in Australia, and its
most obvious purpose is to enable the Johannes-
burg foreigners to maintain under the name of
police an armed force of their own which would
be wholly uuder the control of said foreigners.
With their wealth they could easily raise a
force which would hopelessly outnumber the
little State army. Considering that the en-
franchised foreigners would command a majority
in both Houses, and practically boss the State
army and the present national police, this extra
demand sounds remarkable. Altogether there
is no painful modesty about the Outlanders of
the golden city.
Never a parson in Bananaland had a word to
say against the iniquity of sending troops to
assist in the Transvaal butchery, but the
moment it was known that the Queensland
warriors were to embark on the Sundays, howl
of parsonic execration went up that well-nigh
rent the welkin. No objection had these gen-
tle disciples of the lowly Nazarene to the
mowing down of their fellow-creatures bv
Maxim-guns, sent at the beck of the South
African Chartered Co. and London Stock Ex-
change ; never a jot did they care if their
fellow-Christians were blown up in scores by
Christian lyddite ; but as soon as the news
spread that the Northern pirates were to
start for the scene of operations on the Saw-
bath, then the trouble started. Letters of pro-
test from indignant devil-dodgers poured
into the Courier ; Ministers of the Crown were
interviewed on the matter; and eventually
parsondom was successful in having the time
of departure shifted on to some day a little less
sacred and a little more in keeping with the
Devil’s business. Some of the clerical letters
were ludicrous in the extreme. “ Let us be
