About the middle of last week a small,
unobtrusive advt. set forth that, to oblige the
French consul, the rather pointless story of Dree-
fizz and Extra-hazy would not be played at Sydney
Criterion after Friday night. It was necessary,
of course, to find something to replace the thin
French shock, and partly from necessity and
partly to gratify the Argentine consul, who, along
with the Peruvian consul, was very anxious about
the matter, “Man for Man” was revived on
Saturday. The present writer hasn’t had time to
see the Bolivian consul since the change of bill,
but he has reason to believe that that gentleman
is much pleased, and that the consuls from
Arabia and Babylon are so overjoyed that they
haven’t been to bed for three nights. Also the
Assyrian consul is drunk. “ Man for Man ” isn’t
quite new, but it hasn’t been played for a long
time, and it is a live piece with a large railway-
accident in it which can bear repetition ; so the
new bill, besides giving pleasure to a large number
of consuls, has some artistic merits of its own. In
the very early days of this story it appears that
Mr. Ellerton’s wife had a son, and after the in-
scrutable manner of the dramatic woman she
promptly swapped him for the baby of Kate
Noel, gipsy. Apparently she lay in bed, very
weak and ill after the birth of her child,
and in a faint musical voice gave orders that the
baby should be swapped, and he was swapped
accordingly. When this lady died the gipsy
woman left the swapped infant on its father’s
doorstep, and he took him in from charitable
motives, and the bogus son planted stolen docu-
ments in his pocket when he grew up, and he was
arrested on his wedding day, and got 14 years.
• * * •
After that the story leaves the ordinary
paths of drivel, and becomes a vigorous and
energetic drama. George Rignold turns up as
a large humorous friendly murderer who has got a
life sentence to go on with ; and when Mr. Diver
arrives to start his 14 years the affable murderer
is chipping at the adjacent rock, and greets him
with a cheerful dash and curse. They escape
together later, on a snowy night, and the large
murderer, being a man of resource, wrecks the ex-
press train in hopes of finding a much-needed
disguise among the effects of the dead. It is a
very fine wreck, and when Bill, the Homicide,
finds some clothes and brandy and a cigar in
the wreckage he is more than satisfied. The hero
also finds his wife and his enemy, the man who put
the papers in his pocket, in the ruins; and Bill,
the Homicide, puts the latter by violence into a
broad-arrow suit, and gives him up to the law.
Thereafter things move rapidly, for the will, or
the indenture, or the protocol, or something (they
are all much the same for dramatic purposes) is
found on the bad man’s person. Also, the bad
man made the great mistake of kicking his mother
(he didn’t know she was his mother, but a man
in a drama ought always to remember that every-
body is liable to be his mother, and shouldn’t kick
anybody), and she rounds on him. And every-
body rounds on him, and everybody proves to be
Bomebody else, and all things go kite nigh.
• * • *
The new piece was put on at the shortest
notice (as soon as it became known that the French
consul objected to the previous piece), almost
without rehearsal, and by a company which seems
to have the influenza badly. Rignold, in particu-
lar, is one large cough and sneeze, and a physical
wreck, and his performance of Bill, the Homicide,
is very creditable indeed under the circumstances.
There are many possibilities about Bill, and
Rignold makes good use of some of them. He will
probably make good use of the rest also when his
cough becomes less tremendous, and he knows
more of his dialogue. Diver plays the hero for
once, and, as is usual with him, he plays the first
act nervously and the other four well. A new
man named Cross does remarkably well as the
worst villain; and Miss Lilian Wheeler faints
beautifully. In a pictorial sense Miss Wheeler is
beyond reproach, and when she lies insensible
after the railway accident, with the limelight on
her, she is a vision whose radiance almost sets fire
to Act IV. If she had done so, and the conflagra-
tion spread to Acts 111. and V., there is no know-
ing what might not have happened. Miss Barton
is another vision. The ladies of Rignold’s company
act only moderately at times, but as a vision this
co. can give points to some much more pretentious
combinations.
** * *
The ever-welcome “Ma Mie Rosette” is
due at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, on Monday (Nov.
6), and the king will have nefarious designs on
Rosette for five nights only. There are few operas
to which Sydney extended a warmer welcome than
to “Ma Mie Rosette’’—but that was in the
glorious days when Brownlow was the King of
Navarre, and Nellie Stewart sang “ I was Dream-
ing.” Now there is no Brownlow, and a different
girl is Dreaming where Nellie Stewart used to have
visions on the straw in the farm yard and leave
her lovely form around to be stepped on by the
passing cow. With Pauli as tli6 white-plumed
monarch, and Kenningham in Tapley’s old billet,
and little Florence Perry where the stately Nellie
Stewart used to be, things will be so very different
tiiat Sydney suspends its opinion awaiting develop-
ments.

At Sydney Lyceum the criminal dwarf
with bow legs will be captured on the Embank-
ment by John Sheridan (disguised as an apple-
woman) to-night (Thursday) and to-morrow; after
which a very cheery production that isn’t more
incoherent than usual will leave off. The bur-
lesque of “Little Christopher Columbus” is
announced for Saturday, and the sn ail and plump
Graeie Whiteford is to be Columbus. The
lamented mariner is in two acts, and is the work
of G. R. Sims and Cecil Raleigh, music by Ivan
Caryll. He had a very long run at the Lyric
Theatre, London, and the Lyceum management
presents him with high hopes and a bounding ’art.
* * *- *
The forlorn Sydney Opera House opens
on Saturday with Charles Faning’s “Powerful
and Brilliant Company ”of variety artistes. Why
the powerful and brilliant company wants to waste
its. glory in the desolate barn down King-street
this paper knoweth not.

On Friday week Cinquevalli ended bis en-
gagement at Sydney Palace. When he finished
whirling his travelling kit in the air and catching
it, and the nimble cigar was at rest, and the army
of billiard balls had ceased from revolving around
him, Cinq, came out and spoke a little piece in
Continental English, and promised to come back
again in 1901. The Palace is invaded this week
by quite an array of new faces. The audience
usually encores Arthur Hahn enthusiastically, but
sometimes that young man does not boom quite
so well as others. His rendering of “ Asleep in the
Deep,” and “Deep, Deep, Down,” go well, but his
“Soldiers of.the Queen” fall flat. And in this
jingoistic period a jingo song has to be desperately
flat to do that. Tom Costelloin uniform boldlypokes
fun at the jingoes. As the warrior, he measures about
6ft. something, and later, as a cockney, he seems to
be 4ft. and a bit. Then he re-appears as a well-to-
do gent who was to be married to-day, and whose
intended wife sent her husband round to the
church to say she had changed her mind. His
last appearance is as a dishevelled Flossie, with
the battered hat, frowsy hair, lean blouse, and
skimpy red skirt of the unprosperous Flossie as
she is in Chinatown. Altogether this writer likes
Thomas.
** * %
“Our Native Home ” which is the latest
drama at Melbourne Royal, will command some-
what less disrespect than “ The Merciless World.”
All the same it is a shockingly illogical drama,
and a sad waste of energy for the eo. Two tall
villains, one of them being a deluded mortal as
regards the other fellow’s sister, conspire to cast
the same old suspicion on Mr. John Saunders,
wherefore that patient hero walks about with a
warrant on his track, and goes to a ball in a flaxen
wig for the purpose of discovering the real culprit or
something. But the villain who adores the wicked
sister stabs hiiiis-' 1 ; with a carving knife before he
has confessed the truth about the stolen notes, also
the other villain gets partly confounded by the
funny man, and none of these events seem to make
much difference to Saunders. The warrant still
holds good (although a policeman tore it up for
the sake of his poor old mother), consequently the
hero goes away and starves on the Wide Ocean,
together with two mariners who propose to kill a
little boy and eat him raw. The small child prays
to his Father in Heaven and the Lord drops a
mail steamer from the clouds to rescue the hungry
ones. After which a casual convincing dredger
dredges on behalf of the management, and is
loudly applauded, and the drama wanders into an
unsubstantial Court of Justice, where the sur-
viving villain is found guilty, leaving Saunders to
marry a blind heiress.
* • * *
Miss de Winton, as the afflicted young
woman, looks very nice, but gets very little oppor-
tunity to shake up the show, neither does the
innocence of Saunders arouse much human in-
terest. “Our Native Home ” is chiefly remark-
able for the dredger, and the sudden mail-
steamer, and the fatuous bank-clerk (W. J.
Montgomery) who stabs himself at the ball. Some
of the “comic relief” is grotesque enough to be
amusing in one or two places, notably the place
where Frank Hawthorne, a stable-boy, appears as
Macgregor, the money-lender, in a kilt. The
Bulletin can’t remember any previous case of a
bogus bill-discounter in a kilt. Macgregor is re-
ceived with thanks. Also the aged villager who
E resents a bouquet and a long speech to the blind
eiress is a meritorious sketch, and the kind feel-
ing that prompts the younger villagers to form
themselves into a birthday-ballet deserves every
encouragement. C. R. Stanford remains much
the saine inflexible villain as before, but taking the
acting in the lump, it seems to be too good for the
story.
** * *
The Comic Opera season at the Princess’s
ends to-morrow (Friday), and the Brough Comedy
co. will be welcomed back on Saturday. The last
time Brough went away from the Princess’s he
thought he had breathed his last before a Mel-
bourne audience, but Sydney circumstances
softened his managerial resentment, and now he is
returning to produce “ Gay Lord Quex ” and
other high-class goods. Melbourne is in real need
of some bright comedy these times, so the Brough
co. can hardly fail to flourish with particular
vigor for the next six weeks. The campaign
opens with revivals of “The Liars,” “Lady
Ursula,” and “ Lord and Lady Algy,” and on the
following Saturday, when Melbourne is about
tired of getting 1 excited over the horse that seldom
wins, Quex ” will be duly set up.
** * *
Last Saturday’s change of bill at Melb.
Bijou introduced the veteran song-writer, G. W.
Hunter, who patters better than he sings, and is
somewhat like George Wood the “somewhat dif-
ferent comedian. Also Paul Pelham reappeared
in a state of gaspy patriotism suitable to the recent
departure of the Vic. Contingent. One of Pelham’s
weird, peculiar warbles was directed against
Kruger and the Boers whom he finally sent to
U 1 in freedom’s cause, after which a large un-
sober section of the audience demanded “Rule
Britanyer,” and Pelham, arming himself with a
Union Jack, scored a distinct success by remarking
that the flag had never yet been wiped out.
When the fervent jingoes in front had shrieked
themselves thirsty, they mostly went off to avenge
Majuba at the ’freshment bars, where they charged
their glasses again and again until every Boer in
the Transvaal lay dead and buried. It was a great
night for England.
* * *
Fitzgerald’s Circus also contributes testi-
mony to the honour and glory of England. Old
friend Martyn Hagan has been laid on as a musi-
cal clown for the occasion, and Martyn,
perched upon the boundary of the arena,
appeals to Himperial sentiment in very tune-
ful style. Otherwise the Circus is as strictly
Australian as usual. Lizzie, the elephant, is now
a naturalised native, likewise the performing lion
and tiger and other beasts of prey. And several
young Australians who have been growing up with
the show—Daisy Shand, Rosie Aquinaldo, and
the bright darkie youth, and others—are artists
who reflect credit on their country and threaten to
rival the elephant as popular attractions. The
Circus will boom for another week or so.

Dante the Great is returning with his
burnt lady and whole bag of tricks to St. George’s
Hall (Melb.), for a brief season. Dante’s neat
entertainment, which is due on Saturday (4th),
will be supplemented this time by a series of
biograph pictures, showing how Grace got out,
and Noble hit a sixer, and Syd Gregory placed
balls to leg in the five test matches. The “ Fight
for the Ashes ” is said to be very clearly illustrated
on the sheet, and unless the “loyalty” of Mel-
bourne revolts against any spectacle wherein All-
England gets rather the worst of the contest, the
new “ Theatrograph,” as it is called, will prove a
great assistance to Dante’s business.

Melb. places, owing to the rush of visitors from
afar off.

“ J'm Pan” : Of Arthur Hahn, the new basso at Syd-
ney Palace Theatre, Signor Hazon said some time ago
that Hahn’s was the best untrained bass-voice he had
ever heard, and that it would have made its owner—had
he been caught young— world-famous. Hahn lias a fine,
manly presence ; and his voice compares most favour-
ably with that of Gerard Miller, of MeAdoo’s Georgia
Minstrels. Not long ago, Hahn was driving a baker’s
cart in Sydney.
The Orangemen of Brunswick fMelb.)dreamed of boom-
ing Lili Sharp on the yellow ticket, as a set-off against
Amy Castles. No money in that game. The Orange
sentiment is purely antagonistic, and the voice of a
“ good Protestant " doesn’t touch it at all. Anyhow, it
doesn’t touch its pocket.
Near Castlemaine (Vie.), recently, died Mrs. Rodger
aged 84 years. She was mother of the late J. It. Greville,
comedian.
John Prouse, the fine resonant singer who help to make
the Trebelli Song Recitals go, is an interesting unprofes-
sional sort of artist—a prosperous Maoriland merchant in
the first place, and a first-class baritone afterwards. Miss
Florence Harding, who acts as pianist to the two singers
(except when Mile. Trebelli plays her own accompani-
ment) is the daughter of John Prouse, and there are four
sons who all promise to rival their bearded parent as plat-
form vocalists. The chances are against any of them
having so fresh a voice as the “governor’s” when they
reach his present age—whatever that may be.

“Shifter” : Be tragedian Burford at last getting on to
the stage-door. One of his old acquaintances was Beau-
champ, stage-doorkeeper at Melh. Royal, 40 years ago.
Beauchamp mysteriously disappeared. He turned up in
Melb. years later as Elder Beauchamp, a Mormon
preacher! Burford was in the cast of the “School for
Scandal,” with which Melt). Royal opened.
“Timi” : Death of old-time actor Charles Burford
awakens interest in other ancient mummers still to the
fore. “ Jimmy ” Hasker, a familiar figure, in King-street,
Sydney, with his single eyeglass and curious head-gear,
will be 80 next birthday, and looks sturdy enough to
nibble his way into the.nineties. As a youth he worked
as a qiiarrymari in the construction of Cape Otway light-
house, and his connection with the stage dates from 1847,
when OU.v Deering’s father, then in management, gave
him a “shop” as property-man at Geelong theatre. It
was, however, several years later before he actually took
to the boards. Old j. J. Walsh —spelt that way,- but
pronounced Welsh —is clear-minded and active, despite a
weight of years. He tecolleets clearly the announcement
in Sydney of the death of William IV., and first appeared
on the stage in 1851, in the old Victoria Theatre, Sydney,
under J. G. Griffiths’ management. The only surviving
member, of that co. is Mrs. Stewart—Nellie Stewart’s
mother—then Mrs. Guerin. Burford first appeared in
Australia in ’53, and in ’54, at Melbourne, he created
the part of Evelyn in the first Australian production of
“ Money,” J. J. Walsh appearing as Sharp, the lawyer,
and making one of his earliest hits. Ol lier members of
this cast were Charles Young, his wife, now Mrs. Her-
mann Veziri, Johnny Hydes, the great Rogers, Hooper,
Webster, Mrs. Thompson and Charles, noted for his ex-
traordinary resemblance to the Duke of Wellington. Of
the first “ Money ” Mrs. Vezin and Walsh are now the
sole survivors.
“Cockaigne”: Burford recalls Denvil, the original
Manfred,, at Drury Lane. When Phelps acted Manfred
there, Denvil was checktaker in the pit.
The beauteous Maud Jeffries is getting very fat—and
Wilson Barrett doesn’t like his leading ladies fat.
All the Gilbert and Sullivan operas want editing for the
present generation. In the old plays there used to be a
person representing “ Prologue,” who came on and ex-
plained the action. In “lolanthe” there is needed a
character who might be called “ Asterisk, ’ to stand down
against the footlights and explain allusions, many of
which are now obscure. When the chorus sings the retrain
“Oh, Captain Shaw,” how many .present-day Australians
know that at the time the opera was composed, Captain
Shaw was chief of London Fire Brigade?
History repeats itself : the British troops
in the Transvaal appear to have sustained the
preliminary reverse which it is always necessary
for John Bull to experience ere he girds up his
loins in earnest for battle. It is cabled that in
the course of a fight at Ladysmith in which about
20,000 Boers and 12,000 British were engaged,
a couple of battalions of Imperial regulars —
apparently detached from the main body—were,
with a mountain battery, captured by the Boers.
The latter need to make the best of the advan-
tages at present at their command, for with the
advent of England's big battalions now nearing
African shores, the last hope of the Re-
public disappears. The Transvaal is doomed :
Britain’s command of the sea utterly precludes
either foreign interference or the replenishment
of the republic’s admittedly-defective war sup-
plies. A transient success of the Ladysmith
order will make no difference in the ultimate
resu 11.
From recent S.M. Herald :
Cold steel is a weapon which the Boer dreads. Thus
we read in the accounts of the engagement at Glencoe
that though the Boers faced the artillery and rifle fire
with great determination, they fled at the sight of the
bayonets.
Now, as the Boer does not usually carry a bayonet,
it would be interesting to know what Sir jam es or
any other magnate of the God-fearing Herald
would do if he found himself on a bare hill side
with an empty gun in his hand while another
fellow with another gun, and a bright bayonet on
it, prepared to jab cold steel into him ?
The first cable story about Boer
“treachery” in firing upon the British from
one side of a hill after a white flag had been
hoisted on t’other side of the hill was soon fol-
lowed by news that British prisoners were getting
kind treatment from their “treacherous” foes.
The alleged brutality of the Boer is simply a
newspaper invention, as far as his quarrel with
Britain goes. Whatever brutality he may have is
reserved for the blacks, whose inferiority to w hite
men is an old-established principle in Dutch
colonial government. Probably the Boer’s
assertion of the right to hustle his nigger tends to
make him all the more self-respecting in conflict
with men of his own color.

Society’s management is not extravagant, yet sound.”*
The Western Mine, Zeehan, Tasmania, has cut
six inches of first class ore at No. 7 level in the south
drive on the main lode. A complete Luhritr concentrat-
ing plant on the gradual reduction principle is erected at
this mine with a capacity of 100 tons per day, and consists
of stone breakers, trommels, rolls, jiffs, classifiers, Luhrig
Vanners, etc. Since the erection of this mill the company
have paid about £106,000 in dividends and the mine still
promises to continue its prosperous career. Luhrig
machinery may be obtained from the General Agents, W.
and J. Lempriere, Melbourne and Broken Hill. A two-to-
five ton experimental plant is erected near Melbourne.*
Tim Austral Otis Engineering Co., of South
Melbourne, have delivered the first set of electrically-
driven triple double-acting plunger pumps for Ihe Junc-
tion Deep Leads. These pumps are a fine sample of
colonial manufacturing skill. The total weight of the
pump is close on 100 tons ; all the gear wheels, which are
Bft. diam., and 12in. face, have machine cut teeth. The
Otis Co. had to design and build a special machine to cut
these wheels. Two other sets of these pumps have to be
built to go down tbe shafts on adjoining mines. They
will all be operated front a central power plant, now near-
ing completion.*
Norman Godkin, of 31 Queen-street, Melbourne,
is prepared to purchase or develope, with option of
purchase,. “big prospecting shows” (either gold or
mineral) in any part of Australia. Properties offered
must be subject to inspection.*
Messrs. Plummer Love and Co., 249 George-
street, Sydney, are large suppliers of all mining requi-
sites. They are sole agents here for the now celebrated
“ British ” brand of explosives, detonators and fuse ; also
for the Ta.vlor Horsfield Improved National and Ingersoll
Rock Drills, Air Compressors, &c., Ac.*
Mr. A. Gilfillan, B.Sc.C.E., is visiting the Cool-
gardie goldfield, and is prepared to report on mining
properties, and to attend to the flotation of approved
propositions on the local and London markets. All com-
munications to be addressed to Fremantle.*
The Engineering Works of Mephan Ferguson
are at present fully occupied, giving employment to a
large number of hands, in the different colonies. In
Western Australia, the patent rivetless locking-bar pipes
for the Coolgardie Water-supply are now being delivered.
The South Australian works are busy manufacturing and
supplying pipes for the Bundaleer Water Scheme, and at
the General Engineering Works, Melb. orders are being
received for bridge work, pipes, fluming, earth scoops,
forgings, refrigerators, machinery and ironwork. A large
number of patent Nelyambo scoops or tank-cleaners are
being manufactured to meet the increasing demand.*

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Vic. Leg. Council has passed the second reading
of a Bill for the abolition of discount coupons, which, con-
sidering that body’s usual tenderness towards capital and
vested interests, is a remarkable circumstance. There
are some thousands of pounds invested in the coupon
business, and the Council’s action tends to “ drive capital
out of the country ” and to “ interfere with private enter-
prise,” and other things which it usually views with
abhorrence. It sets one wondering which member of the
House of Landlords keeps the warehouse where the
coupon co. ought to have bought its prizes and didn’t?
» * * *
The National Bank of Australasia (Melb.) re-
ports a profit for the half-year to 30th Sept., 1899, of
£19,246, which is a solid improvement as compared with
last half-year. Also it is a very solid improvement as
compared with every previous half-year since 1894. The
profits during the last six years has been as under :
March, 1894 .. £31,312 March, 1897 .. £14,712
September, 1894, £25,231 September, 1897, £18,275
March, 1895 .. £15,126 March, 1898 .. £15,471
September, 1895, £13,763 September, 1898, £17,602
March, 1896 .. £10,523 March, 1899 .. £12,324
September, 1896, £11,706 September, 1899, £19,246
Preference shareholders get their usual 5 per cent, divi-
dend which absorbs £7645, and the other £11,601, added
to the £52,117 brought forward from previous half-year,
leaves £63,718 to carry forward. No addition is made to
the reserve-fund, which still stands at £50,000 ; whether
the balance is kept loose with a view to an extensive
writing-off some time soon it is difficult to say. At all
events, the earnings, despite the great improvement,
only amount to £1 ISs. per cent, per annum on the share-
holders’ funds, and if these are really all the earnings the
natural inference is that about £700,000 of the capital
wants to be written off as lost. That would make the
profits equivalent to 3 per cent, per annum on the share-
holders’ funds, and most banks nowadays can earn more
than that if the funds are all real. The National Bank is
somewhat of a mystery in this respect. The Bank of
Victoria, for instance, with half-a-million less capital,
showed a net profit last half-year of £27,637, and its
earnings were at the rate of £3 11s. 6d. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders’ funds. Which is a very wide
difference.
The National Bank has now paid off all the
per cent, reconstructed deposits, barring a trifle of
£473,790, which will be set free very shortly. Its deposit
business has diminished a little during the half-year, but
that is probably owing to the large repayments of the old
4J4 per cent, deposits ; and it has £2,606,000 in cash and
liquid securities—equal to over 38 per cent, on all
liabilities to the public. In fact the National looks ex-
ceptionally sound from a depositor’s point of view. From
a shareholder’s standpoint the most solemn fact at present
visible is that to pay even 2]/> per cent, to ordinary share-
holders after the preference crowd has been provided for,
and making no provision whatever for reserve-fund, the
half-year’s earnings need to be increased to nearly
£29,000. Or to pay 2* per cent, with the earnings at the
present rate, the ordinary capital requires to be written
down by fully £700,000.

The Australian Alliance has evidently been doing a good
deal in the way of writing down its inflated assets during
the year. At 3lst August ’9B the amount of contingency
fund was £20,287 including £lO,OOO added from reserve.
It is now only £6105, so over £14,000 has evidently been
absorbed during the year in writing off depreciation.
Among other things the old item of 127,578 for shares in
the Australasian Insurance Co.—a concern whose whole
capital is only £2s,ooo—has been reduced to £22,578. Yet
even after all this writing down the £233,000 of mortgages
and £95,000 of real estate are still carefully specified as
being put in at book value. Apparently no attempt is
made to re-value them and see how the figures set against
them agree with present prices. The assets now yield
about 3J per cent, all round on their nominal value, so it
seems fairly evident that they haven’t been written down
too much.
In 1803 the Australian Alliance had £2125,000
paid-up capital, £llO,OOO reserve, £24,723 profit-balance,
and £10,366 depreciation-fund, so the shareholders’ funds
were then £270,089. They are now £225,397, so the co.
has faded by nearly £45,U00 in seven years.
The A.A.’s life department still does a very
email business at a very great cost. Last year it issued
four new policies, and the income from premiums on new
policies and from annuities sold—the whole income from
new business, in fact—was £797. The premiums on old
policies were £l6,lol—total, £16,958. And the expenses
were £373l—say, all the new premiums and all the money
received from the sale of annuities, and over 18 per cent,
of the old premiums. In other words, about 22*
per cent, of premiums, or 13* per cent, of total
income, goes in management charges, and a concern
like this, which does practically no new business,
should be managed for less than half the outlay.
This life branch, by the way, is one of the relics of the old
group of proprietary life offices which were practically
killed off by the uprising of the great mutual societies.
Four of them —the Adelaide Life Assurance Go., the
Australasian Insurance Go., the life branch of the Austra-
lian Alliance, and the Victoria Life—are doing practically
nothing now except waiting for their last policy-holder to
die. And meanwhile the proprietary idea has come into
fashion again, and four new concerns—three of them
vastly less solid than any of the old proprietary institu-
tions—are lurking round in search of premiums. It seems
strange under the circumstances that none of the four old
veterans think it worth while to go into active business
again.
• * * *
The Mutual Fire Insurance Co. of Tasmania,
which for many' years only published an annual statement
of assets and Labilities, and kept its revenue intensely
dark, now issues an income and expenditure account like
any other office. It is a very good-looking account, too.
Profits for year ended 30th September, 1899, after writing
off £l9l, were £5223. A 7 per cent, dividend (t 2625) and
dividend tax (£131) absorb £2756 ; £lOOO is added to re-
serve, making it £ll,OOO, and £lOOO to contingency fund,
making it £2390. The remaining £467, with £3020
brought forward, leaves £3487 at credit of profit and loss.
The Mutual of Tasmania is an amazingly strong com-
panv for the size of its business; it has a paid-up
capital of £37,500; reserve £ll,OOO ; contingency fund
£2390 ; reinsurance reserve £2762, and £3487 at credit
of profit and loss —in all £57,139 of shareholders’ funds ;
with £150,000 of uncalled capital behind them ; and all
this for the better security of a business which is repre-
sented by r an annual premium income of £5525. In other
words, it has 11 years’ premiums on hand. The year’s
income is £8461 (premiums £5525 and interest £2936) and
for such a small institution to make a profit of £5223
shows either remarkably good luck or remarkably' good
management. The income from investments alone is
sufficient to pay' 7* per cent, dividends, which is an
unusual circumstance ; and the year’s losses are only
£BB4, whence it may be inferred that Tasmania is either
too damp to burn or too beastly honest to set itself on
fire. A good many institutions of six times the Mutual’s
size would be glad to be doing half as well.
• • * »
The County of Bourke Permanent Building and
Investment Society (Melbourne) presents its first balance-
sheet since it came formally out of liquidation and re-
sumed business. The County of Bourke went the way of
most other big land-dealing institutions in the days when
the boom rolled by—in other words it suspended. But
being sounder than the average, it paid off its depositors
in full, with interest, and then had a large balance left
for the shareholders. So it wrote its capital down to
£96,970, and started business afresh. Its balance-sheet
for the year ended 16th September, 1899, shows a balance
of £6634 to credit of profit and loss, made upas follows :
Business profit for y'ear .. .. £4BSS
Received from debts previously written off
as bad .. .. .. .. 472
Profit on sale of properties which fetched
more than the written-down value .. 1274
£6634
This means that the business made a profit of £4BBB.
And in addition £1746 of the capital previously written
off as lost came back. This item is not ordinary profit;
it only means that when the tremendous writing-down of
capital was made £1746 was written off which, as events
proved, should not have been so treated. Therefore, the
obvious course now, as it can’t conveniently be restored
to capital account, is to put it to reserve fund. But the
management treats it as ordinary profit, and declares a
fine, large 6 per cent, dividend (£5889), so that the whole
of the profits and £lOOl of capital goes in dividend. Then
there is a small balance of £745 to carry forward. There
was sufficient profit to cover a 4* per cent, dividend with-
out using the recovered capital for div.-paying purposes,
but the County of Bourke directors were in haste to
begin booming again, and didn’t mind much how they
did it. When the co. resumed business £10,466 was set
aside out of capital to cover contingencies, and £BO6 of
this goes to pay for rates, reconstruction expenses,
sewerage connections, &c. As the co. has taken £BO6
from its contingency fund, and has added £745 to profit
and loss, it is therefore, on paper, about £6l worse off
than it was a year ago. This premature rush after
dividends isn’t a promising feature.

The Briseis (Tas.) tin-mine, which has lately
been producing 40 tons per month (with 30 men, the
shareholders receive £l3B per month for every man
employed) appears to be improving much as the centre
of the lead is approached. Recent rough weather caused
some damage to the head race, delaying tin-raising
operations for a few days, but with the improving richness
of the faces the monih’s output should be little dimi-
nished. It is stated that the Briseis directors have secured
the Krushka Brothers Home mine, with what object
is not yet known, but an amalgamation of those mines
should have taken place years ago, the Brothers Home
(which has produced £750,000 worth of tin from a com-
paratively small area worked) being the natural outlet
for the Briseis lead. From this latter mine 17 men raised
17 tons of tin last month. The Brothers Home Ex-
tended is also raising good tin ; nine men w'orking on
tribute raised nearly nine tons of tin last month. The
Extended is about to be floated in Scotland.
Tasmanian G. M., 1690 tons 2402 ozs. Eitliera
mistake in the figure—tonnage apparently too small—or
the mine has suddenly gone down in tonnage and up in
average ; Is. div. It is probably the wettest mine in
Australia.

The Mt. Lyell chairman, Bowes Kelly, is now,
with director “Joe” Syme, in America talking to the
copper-combine people, Marcus Daly, Rockfeller and co.
Mt. Lyell has become such an important copper-producer
that it has been invited to join the ring. Last week cop-
per mysteriously fell over £2 : perhaps it was a little jerk
given by the copper trust to indica'e to Kelly and others
what it could really do with the market if it laid on the
whip and how necessary it was for the self-preservation of
a mine turning out, 10,000 tons of copper annually to take
a hand in controlling the world’s production and con-
sumption.

Pambula Mines Limited (N.S.W.) has done one
sensible thing at last— reduced its capital from £115,000
to £50,000. Better still if it could increase its yields. It
has been a big disappointment for several year# to the
English shareholders. Found some rich gold in a narrow
streak recently, but it was always the will-o’-the-wisp
mine in a will-o’-the-wisp camp.

Mt. DrysdaleAt last an important dis-
covery is said to have been made in this mine at 275 ft.
The assay is marvellous in gold and silver ; but the differ-
ence between assaying a sample and crushing a ton is in-
finite. Still, a mine that has turned out as much gold
from a pipe of ore as Mt. Drysdale did four years ago lias
always hope of getting another, and this mine has been
long enough looking for it, goodness knows. You never
can quite tell what may happen at Mt. Drysdale.
* * * *
N.S.W. Govt, proposes a Bill this session to
regulate dredging, which is practically hung up pending
a decision on the many lease applications already received.
There is, however, a force to be reckoned with that
dredging promoters, genuine and bogus, have never
thought of—the Leg. Council. What odds that that body
will not throw out the measure? It excluded copper
from the Mining on Private Property Bill in the interests
of the western squatters, nnd the chances are that it will
throw out the Dredging Bill in the same interests—on the
pretext that the few good water-courses of the country
will be irrevocably impaired. Also the squatters’ repre-
sentatives, as they nearly all are, will pose as the fossicker’s
champions. The d{-edging outlook is ominous.

MiJburn Creek (N.S.W.) ; The old copper-mine
of sinister memory has been un-watered and cleaned out.
“ It is estimated that there are about 60,000 tons of ore in
sight assaying from 10 to 30 per cent, copper.” Then why
on earth was the show left so long unworked ? Surely
somebody must have known about things. Is it reason-
able to suppose that the people who last worked it were
such abject fools ? From a surface examination, Milburn
Creek mine would seem to consist of a number of short
shoots of ore, but to imagine that 60,000 tons have been
placed in sight by un-watering alone is too ridiculous.

Paddy Lackey Deep Levels, N.S.W. — “Samples
from the reef struck in the shaft at 450 ft. assay 4ozs.
lOdwts. per ton. Going to the north, the reef will run
into the Paddy Lackey mine 50ft. from this shaft, hut on
the south side the Deep Level Mine ha 3 a continuation of
the reef for 20 chains, with backs gradually increasing
from 150 ft. to 450 ft." So far very good, but not quite
far enough. Where is most of the gold-shoot—to the
north or to the south? It cannot be both ; it isn’t long
enough for that. If to the south, and this yarn is cor-
rect, the parent mine has no future, and the owners have
been idiots enough to allow the side-on ground to re-
main unpegged for many years and then to be collared by
men having more judgment than themselves.

Latest copper-market letters from Europe ex-
press surprise that the anticipated increase in production
has not come along ; and they add that it seems no nearer
than it was six months before. It must come, they admit;
but meantime people who want copper are tired of wait-
ing, and are buying at the higher prices. Transactions
have been made for delivery up to February at September
quotations.^

Another meeting of Plunderderry shareholders
in London, and Lord F'introll foolishly kept away from it.
Always go to a mining meeting in London. Your pre-
sence will silence a multitude of tongues. In his absence
one shareholder remarked that though the noble Lord
had redeemed his promise in giving up certain interests
in another mine as a refund of his Londonderry promo-
tion profits, the shares received were only worth 3d.,
while the stamp-duty to get them transferred cost Iks.
Myrincr, formerly a N.S.W. rabbit-inspector, another
Plunderderry promoter, last year promised the share-
holder* a deal by way of refund, but ha* performed
nothing to date. Myring had one defence anyhow—he
was not going to adu inault to injury.
W. R. Dunstan, the English scientific coal-man,
haa, at Bigjack’s request, been testing Collie coal. lie
describes it as having an excess of volatile matter; “such
coal burns and disintegrates too freely.” Ash ranges
from 1 to 7J per cent.—small as to weight, but singularly
voluminous. The coal does not coke. The calorific
quality is “rather below the average shown by the best
steam coals ; more Collie coal is needed to convert a given
weight of water into steam than is required by the ordi-
nary steam coal used in England.” He sums up that
“allhough it cannot be put in the first class, it will prove
to he very valuable in VVestralia, where coal is scarce and
wood very largely depended on for fuel. As a steam-
raiser it is greatly to be preferred to wood and also to
many coals of better composition which produce much
smoke when burned." And it will he valuable for domes-
tic use, as it "quickly makes a hot and non-smoking
fire.” The Prof, does not think much of its gas-making
prospects.
Bad luck has befallen the Forrest public-
battery scheme. Prospectors curse both Govt, and
batteries impartially, charging latter with robbery
(gold lost through imperfect treatment) and former
with extortion. Govt, reply by raising crushing
rates to 15s. per ton minimum, and sliding scale
according to ore value, on the ground that the
batteries are not meeting expenses. Truth is that
Forrest entrusted the inception of the scheme to political
deadbeats who, secure from official observation in way-
back hamlets, “played up.” “Looking for water” was
the customary explanation. And it was true enough—
they were after some to mix with their whisky. One
prospective gathering seriously discussed laying a pipe-
line from the local pub. to the battery site to save
manager’s time perambulating to and fro. In other cases,
batteries were erectod where the existence of water was
unproved. Then some of the “plants” were bought
from friends ; while contracts for cartage and erection
were let to other friends, and still other friends were ap-
pointed managers.

The Australian Gold Recovery Co. recently
started a big list of actions in Victoria for the wrongful
use of the Cyanide process, and that against Hans Irvine,
claiming £‘lo,ooo damages, has been compromised—no
doubt much to the indignation of those who propose to
fight.
• • • •
At Bendigo the Lazarus has got its shaft down
below 3430 ft., and is now opening out at that ; overcoats
are not worn there. Shenandoah has a reef running to
15ft. wide at 3134 ft. deep ; not much gold, however.
Still it isn’t only stone in which there is gold staring you
in the face that they treat at Bendigo. St. Mungo has
just put through 240 tons for 220z5., and finds that covers
more than trucking, hauling, and milling exes. Koch’s,
too, of which good things were promised, has slumped
badly, and gets only 670 z. from its last 440 tons.
• *
The lately-issued Q. Mines’ annual report shows
that Northern goldfields still give many accidents. The
Towers alone gave eight fatalities and 23 cases of serious
injury. Carelessness was responsible for several disasters.
One man was using a heavy copper tamping-bar, and
vigorously driving home a plug of dynamite. Result—
one man killed and two injured. Out of 1268 men em-
ployed in coal-mines, notone was injured during the whole
year.
Big .Tack Harvey was at Coolgarra (Q.) recently
and pegged a number of tin shows. The sandfly of the
field, who looks on all newcomers much as the Sand-
gropers of W.A. regarded the Easterners, objected to the
pegging. The sandfly said he had pegged the ground (an
easy matter as he makes his descriptions hazy and shifts
the' pegs to take in every new discovery), but he had
“ forgotten to apply for the lease.” To this Big Jack,
“You blanky miserable scoundrel ! Next thing you’ll do
is to put one peg in Cape York and another in Kalgoorlie
and collar the whole blessed continent."
* » * *
Dear Bulletin.— Re your advocacy of the ap-
prentice system in mines, I know an Australian mine that
has taken apprentices, or cadets, with £2O premium, at
ss. a week for first year, 10s. for second, and the final
year 15s. per week—guaranteeing in return tuition in
theoretical and practical mining. Sounds well enough,
but how’s it carried out? For the first few months the
youths were in charge of a manager, who was supposed
to instruct them in theory and practice—about which he
knew absolutely nothing himself. He was followed by a
competent man, but since the latter left, after three
months, the ’prentices have had no instruction. Also,
underground, they are left entirely to their own devices—
not even shown how to put a hole in, or to break away
dirt to best advantage. Further, two “cadets” are put
in a face together, instead of each one being with a good
miner, and the youths do almost as much for their paltry
6s. per week as some of the men do for 455. Some time ago
the ’prentices applied fora little more pay in considera-
tion of their not receiving any theoretical instruction,
but the proposal is—and is likely to remain—“ under con-
sideration." These facts speak for themselves.— ln The
Know.

Is the Australian utterly humorless ? On
that wet Saturday, when N.S.W. was shipping
117 men and nine officers and some horses to help
the 80,000 British with their Transvaal war (ship-
ping them, too, when the war was already half
over), the streaming ’busses tore up and down the
street with inscriptions such as: “The lion and
the kangaroo will quickly put old Kruger
through.” The lion and the kangaroo, observe.
Ihe German army assisted by John Smith!
A case of the Russian empire and Goat Island
declaring war. Yet nobody laughed ! The damp,
goloshed multitude took it all in the deadest of
dead earnest.

There was actually included in the S.A.
Contingent— only 125 altogether—an “ awkward
squad,” consisting of men who knew nothing
either of drill or of rifle-shooting! So much for
the war-spirit in the land of the Oroweaters.
There was much jingo hurrahing, even in Parlia-
ment, at the announcement that one country
company of Mounted Rifles had volunteered in a
body; but the subsequent discovery that they
stipulated for higher pay than that offered was
dismissed in a whisper.
A circus shared the camping-ground with the
S.A. Contingent while the latter was waiting for
the Medio. It was also observed that an un-
attached donkey would hang round the tents. At
last it was run out and stoned up North Ter-
race towards Parliament House —the nearest
approach to a military operation some of the men
had apparently ever undertaken.

MUZZLING OF DOGS.
London, Oct. 25.
The law requiring- the
muzzling- of dogs in London
as , a precaution against
rabies has been abrogated,
and on and after Friday
next dogs will be allowed in
the streets unmuzzled.
A departmental commit-
tee appointed by the British
Board of Agricultuie in
1896 to inquire into and
report upon the working of
the laws relating to dogs
reported during 1897 that
while muzzling is the only
practicable means for the
extermination of rabies, the
powers of muzzling as exer-
cised since 1892 by local
authorities were inadequate
to eradicate rabies, and only
resulted in temporary and
local checks to its spread.

The Australia Hotel, Coolgardie (W.A.)
over which Gov. His Excellency Knight Smith
has come to such serious grief, was a 14-rocmed
building with three bars and five or six bedrooms
And tins small band-box was floated into a com-
pany with the Governor and the Governor’s aide-
de-camp and secretary among the shareholders
and in some way or other about c-69000 was spent
on its construction. Even then the roof leaked
shamefully. It had a board of directors, and a
managing director at .£4OO a year; and the 14-
roomed structure contrived to get up an overdraft
of £5762. And when it went broke all the furni-
ture, drink, billiard-tables, &c., were only worth
about £750. Governor Smith, coming to a new
and cat-haunted country, might have been ex-
cused if he came to grief over a wild cat, but to
fall over a kitten of this size was a very humble
proceeding.

surprising how many people you would meet in
Sydney who spoke with a nasal twang and had
“ only just arrived ” from the States, or Cuba, or
Manila. Just now, however, the bogus American
lias slumped, whereas the bars abound with men
just back from the Rand.” If Jamieson didn’t
have a force of many thousands, every one of his
followers must be at present in Sydney.

A SYDNEY FAREWELL.
“ Well, good bye! Expect you'll be back again
before the George-street tram passes.”
Grov. Beauchamp is going to be vtry care-
ful for the future. When he had groped his wav
out of the Darling duststorm that had been raised
in his honor, some papers thought they were safe
in saying that, what with drought and dust and
Cobar whisky, the trip had been somewhat un-
pleasant. But they must have heard from His
Ex., for thei’e was a singular emphatic intimation
in next issue that His Ex. had had a most pleasant
time indeed—the people were so good ; it was only
the dust that was not altogether agreeable.

The Salvarmy is nothing if not thorough.
Several of its Melbourne horses wear knee-pads.
The most brazen part of the
Scotchbyterian claim to control the
Melb. Hospitals is the assertion
that the church gives ,£5OOO or
£6oooayear towards the manage-
ment of the medical charities
yet has no voice in their
control. As a mat-
ter of fact, the
church pursues
its usual policy
of never giving
a brass farth-
ing to any-
body. The
.£5OOO or
£6OOO referred
to is the Hospi-
tal Sunday Fund
which, even by
the wildest
stretch of Cal-
vinistic imagina-
tion, cannot be
regarded as
“given ” by the
churches. Before
you can give a
thing i t must
belong to you,
and the churches merely act as
collectors in regard to the Hos-
pital Sunday money. They re-
mit it to the different institu-
tions through a general fund,
but have no more to do with
the giving of the cash than the
postman who delivers the
cheque. The money is at no
time the church’s property.
Under existing circumstances,
however, the churches seem
to hold all the cards in the
present controversy, for there
is nothing to prevent them
following the threatened
policy of paying in the cash
in the form of guinea subscriptions
to each hospital. Then, with dummy subscribers
representing each sovereign, the kirk will be able
to control every hospital election. The end of the
matter will probably be the burst-up of Hospital
bunday, and the churches will have the grim
satisfaction of wrecking another successful
humanitarian institution for its impioty in refusing
to be ruled by the retailers of dogma.

Bulletin envies its respectability. Wrong. The

by too painful a process. 'The Times, before
becoming entirely respectable, tried the other
thing—published the Dead Bird.

To Melb. Paunch the other week came the
news that “ amongst, the unfortunates at Johan-
nesburg are many Melbourne girls who went over
at the end of last year.” The poor girls, it
seems, are unfortunate only in having gone to a
place where business is in a bad way just at pre-
sent. Nothing worse than that.
A BETTER CARVER.
BOARDINGHOUSE LADY : “ Mr. Johnson, will you
kindly carve the duck? ”
JOHNSON (stammering): “Better ask the new
boarder, madam — he’s a retired blacksmith.”
The Bulletin is glad to see that N.S.W.
Photographic Society is steadily extending its in-
Huenoe. This year’s exhibition of photographs
was the best yet held ; though in the desire to
gain support the committee admitted many
crudities which a stronger society would have un-
hesitatingly rejected. But the larger number of
photographs were well worth exhibition, and
formed a very interesting show. For the en-
couragement of amateurs it may be noted that
some of the best work was shown in the small
size of quarter-plates ; the percentage of failures
was larger among the more ambitious efforts. Yet
some of these were both technically and artisti-
cally good. By way of criticism it is suggested
that the society make a clearer division between
the classes of work in the room ; and let the cata-
logue numbers run in numerical order. In the
show just held the task of disentangling a given
exhibit from the irregular catalogue took as much
time as an interior exposure on a rainy day in
winter.

Husband and
wife mutu-
allyisepar-
ated, the
former
from

latter in Syd-

As plaintiff in a
suit before Justice Faucett she was informed that
her husband must be joined with her as there
was no legal separation.
She was asked if
she could produce
him if the case
were postponed _
until next sit-
tings. She could.
It was in the days of
Hall's steamers to ’Frisco. The lady went
in one steamer and returned per next
bringing her husband. She «as allowed to
amend her plaint by joining the husband
and won her action. Then the long-separ-
ated pair went their several ways again.
Recently, when a
crowded excursion - train
stopped at a N.S.W. sta-
tion for refreshments a
young woman climbed out
of a second-class carriage
and returned same way
with a cup of tea, which
had cost 3d. Then a weary
woman with two small children asked, as she
couldn’t leave the carriage, whether the young
lady would mind getting her a cup. Latter said
there wasn’t time, and compromised matters by
selling half her cupful for 6d.

Whenever any member of the Q. police-
force gives evidence in any way damaging to the
reputation of his superiors, some official rises and
tells the Commission that the man is a bit cracked.
Moved to wrath thereby, Chairman Unmack
finally suggested : —“ Do you recommend that the
Govt, medical officer go round and examine the
men in the force, because this is the third man
represented as being off his head ? It'is probably
because they have not given evidence that pleased.”

Olcl-man Panton sniffs contemptuously
when Melbourne Mayor M‘Eacharn mounts the
police-court; bench arrayed in full municipal fig,
and never misses a chance of sitting-on the gor-
geous one. Said Mac., the other day, to the
Chinese complainant in an assault case, ‘ I don t
believe a word you say.” “And I believe every
word he says,” snapped Panton. And the two
glared at each other. But the majority sided
with the Mayor. Great is the power of drapery !
“ Malle ” : Seeing a big crowd in Oxford-
street, Sydney, the other night, I asked a
small boy what the trouble was, and
was informed “it’s only a bloke
tryin’ to get away from ’is bit o
skirt.” Sure enough all the
ruction was caused by a small
woman upsetting her hus-
band’s little picnic with an-
other “lydy.” He jumped on
a moving tram and, finding
the wife ,did ditto, off on the
other side, but so did she.
Then he, after walking beside
her in a for a
while, suddenly made a bolt
round a corner, but had not gone
10 yards before she cleverly col-
lared him. Then she favored the crowd
with her name and address, a concise but
spirited account of her family history, and
her opinion of “ that cat.” It's the big strong
woman who sits at home and weeps over hubby’s
misdeeds, but it’s never safe to fool with five-foot-
nothing. 9
Clearing up a much-discussed matter :
Dear Bulletin. —Re the statement that an alleged
“Batman plate” had been taken from the wall of the
A.M.P. building in Collins-street, Melbourne, and sup-
planted by another plate “ advertising the virtues of the
A.M.P. Society.” It seems that a Batman plate never
existed outside of that writer’s mind. For many years
a copper medallion inscribed with the Society’s coat-
of-arms was fastened to the Collins-street facade of the
A.M.P. building. Immediately beneath it was the inti-
mation cut into the stone that John Batman, founder or
Melbourne, built on this corner. The stone probably
looked to be a foot-piece to the medallion. Then a dividing
wall was removed from the interior of the buildinir, anda
window was let into the place where the medallion had
been, the medallion itself being shifted a few feet further
to the right, in between two windows. The inscribed
stone was left untouched, and it now serves as a sill to
the new window. The copper plate, which is not sup-
planting another plate, but is just the same old plate all
the time, bears no testimony to the virtues of the A.M.P.
It merely bears the Society’s name and trade insignia. F.

A budding novelty in the hotel line in
Sydney is “ Ladies’ private bar ” in gold letters on
the side door. At least one hotel expresses satis-
faction with the experiment.

Sydney Jxchange, the directors of which
have been authorised to add two storeys and
thoroughly modernise the building, was first
opened by Gov. Denison in Dec., 1857- The
foundation-stone was laid by Gov. Fitzroy in ’53.
Many old historic associations are interwoven
with the life of the commercial structure.

“ E.0.E.” : The lady who dusts my desk
(yearly contract) is a buxom jingo of the goriest
type. For weeks before the war she forestalled
every movement of “ that ’orrid old Kruger,” and
breathed patriotic pseans over Chamberlain. Sh 4
gets her paper an hour earlier now, and on Monday
when I came in was swishing the duster angrily)
about the inkstands. “Well!” she said, with)
compressed lips and flashing eyes, “ they’ve got
it this time —them Boers.” (Swish.) “The
British officers suffered terrible, though.” (Dusting
suspended.) “I see Major Symons got ’it.” I
threw in a crumb of sympathetic interest —“ Yes,
he’s badly hurt evidently ”; and was immediately
corrected”: “ Well, the paper said ‘ seriously
wounded in the groin.’ I see the Queen has con-
ferred another honor upon him.” (Dusting re-
sumed.) “I see the Leicestershire regiment
stormed the ’ill. Climbed right up under cover
and killed 500 of ’em.” That’s what they get for
fitting Major Symons. And serve ’em right.”
(I here saved an inkstand from destruction.)
“ They say it’s the best fighting been seen since
the Russian-Persian war of ’7O and ’7l. And
there’ll be a lot more of it too. Fancy them
Boers standing up against the British army ! It’s
like their imperence.” Here the patriotic stop

* * * •
The pathetic story of a Hebrew conva-
lescent. The scion of Israel is in the pawnbroking
and ole clo’ business in Melb., and one warm
afternoon, about a month back, a poor-looking
stranger wandered into Nosenbloom’s shop, carry-
ing over his arm a black frock-coat of ancient cut.
“ How much ? ” asked the customer, throwing
the coat on the counter. Nosenbloom pawed
the article professionally. “Fife shillids,”
he said. “Ten,” said the customer. “Fife
shillids—nod a peddy more.” “ Seven-
an’-six,” said the customer. “ ’Tain’d vort
id.” “Not worth it! . That was my old
uncle’s coat. He gave four pounds for
it, an’ never wore it more’n a dozen
times.” “Can’d help it,” exclaimed No-
senbloom, “ fife shillids is my price.” The
man took up his coat again, and began
to enlarge on its many virtues, and the
magnificent quality of the cloth, trying it
between his fingers as he did so. Sud-
denly he easecd talking, feeling anxiously
at a place under the arm ; his face paled ; he
hastily drew a knife from his pocket, and
Nosenbloom watched him with much concern
whilst he cut the lining. Both uttered exclama-
tions of amazement as the seeker drew forth a
small roll of notes. “Fifties !” he gasped—“ four
of ’em ! ” Then he turned and fled the place.
Nosenbloom took to bed ; for a time his life was
despaired of, and, although he is now convales-
cent, he never expects to be the same man again.
* * * *
The proposal to shift Adelaide’s burying-
place ten miles out of the city evoked a double-
barrelled protest from Labor-leader Batchelor.
He complained that it would make funerals too
costly, and also that it would be inconvenient for
people “ desiring to visit the graves of deceased
relatives.” Plaint No. lis a good reason in favor
of cremation ; burials far outside the city are
costly, and burials inside it are unhealthy, but
cremation inside the city is both cheap and
feasible. Plaint No. 2is also a reason in favor of
cremation, for the morbid practice of “ visiting
the graves of deceased relatives ” is one of the
saddest of the small habits of civilisation. Low-
spirited mooning among the tombs, and study-
ing the inscriptions over our own and other
people’s dead, and being buried in thought
on cold gravestones are all objectionable
customs, And even to people of Batchelor’s
turn of mind the practice has objections. It
may be beautiful goodness knows why —to
visit the last home of our own dead. But it
isn’t at all beautiful to think that objectionable
strangers go and lunch on the mound, and paw
the monument, and teach little Bill a reading-
lesson from the inscription, and leave half a sand-
wich on the grassy covering. Writer once
wandered through a Maori'and cemetery and
found a pale, fat, shiny-faced boy of 17 or so
trying to make it up with his girl on the grave of
the only acquaintance he had there. The fat boy
and his girl had quarrelled, and the girl sat on the
grave and scorned him. When one considers that
burial means putting one’s friends under a green
mound which the butcher, and the tax-gatherer,
and the publican sit on when they have a holiday,
there is no poetry in it.
* * * *
N.S.W. Coroner’s Court jurors are in
future to be paid the same rates as jurors in the
Civil and Criminal Courts. Altogether a levering-
up has taken place in these classes of employment.
For each day for three days the juror will get Bs.,
for the fourth and every subsequent day on same
panel, 12s. Bystanders sworn on any trial, Bs.
per day. The travelling expenses have also been
liberalised.

The employes of Sydney Water Supply and
Sewerage Board have established a horticultural
society and held a show. Interesting to know if
the members of the society are harassed like other
people by the Board’s inspectors, whose instruc-
tions would appear to be to prevent, as far as
Eossible, all attempts at horticulture by gmall
ouseholders.
In Warwick (Q.), the W.C.T.U.
and Y.M.C.A. combined in an endeavor
to Christianise the local Chows, and soon
had a fine crop of market-gardener pro-
selytes. But, later on, came some smart
frosts, doing much damage to the gardens,
and, incidentally, to the “faith” of the
Chow-owners. An assurance that the
frost was a “blessing in disguise,” how-
ever, served to soothe matters over until
a severe hailstorm came along. Then the
“converts” relapsed in a body, on the
ground that their new Joss was altogether
too skilful in disguising his “ blessings.”
* * * *
A vendetta of more than Corsican
intensity rages between two traders—
once partners in the same business,
but now rivals. A little while ago one
of the partners set forth on a tour
abroad fortified with complimentary
testimonials signed by a member of the
Ministry. Then the friends of the
erstwhile partner got in their fine work
with the politician, and official letters
which rendered the testimonials
nugatory were sent forth broadcast.
Skin and hair and knuckledusters may
be expected when the exasperated
tourist returns to Australia.
Melbourne Y.M.O.A. has presented every
member of the Victorian Boer-slaughtering con-
tingent with a testament, and on each testament
is a beautiful and appropriate inscription, “ By
love serve one another.” It is probably a mis-
print for—“By Jove, shoot one another.” The
Bulletin devoutly hopes that the Victorians, at
all events, will never come within 10 miles of a
Boer gun. If they do, our children, and
our children’s children, will be afflicted in
ages yet unborn by long-bearded clergymen
with a tale to tell about the good little man
who carried that testament over his heart, or
in his hat, or somewhere; and how, after the
battle of Potchefstrugerskloofdorp, he found a
bullet in it, which would otherwise have been
fatal, and how he thereafter gave thanks, and
married, and had 45 children who all went to bed
regularly at nine o’clock, and never tasted strong
drink. And against this will be set the awful
case of the other fellow who swapped his testa-
ment for beer, and was killed. These dreadful
yarns can be seen afar off, even now.

Victorian warriors bound for the Trans-
vaal are officially warned that those of them who
have artificial shop teeth must take spare teeth
along with them in case, the original set drops out
during the campaign. And unless a shell lands
right on the waggon-load of Victorian spare teeth
as it lumbers behind the advancing column, the
precaution will probably pan out all right. A
patriotic Melbourne dentist has offered to supply
the extra teeth gratis. Probably when the whole
375 warriors call round for their free molars that
toothsmith will be sorry he spoke. Apropos, the
Victorian military authorities seem to be strong
on teeth. A recruit, otherwise physically perfect,
was found to have one hollow back eater, and was
informed that it was so urgently necessary that
only the very best specimens of gumsucker should
be shot on the veldt that he couldn’t go. When
real war comes this way Australia’s military po-
tentates will probably be less particular; and the
disreputable, the drunken, the bald-headed,
the people with warts and ingrowing nails, the
hump-backed, and even the procrastinator, and
the uncivil, and the people whose Greek
pronunciation is imperfect will be all roped
in together, so long as they can carry a
rifle and shoot. There is a legend about
how Bivas Palacias, in the Mexican War of Liber-
ation, forcibly shoved the whole contents of a
monastery into his ranks when he was desperately
short of material to put up against the French
shooters, and drove the fat peaceful fathers into
battle. But that was a time of bitter stress, when
even a paralysed recruit in a wheelbarrow wouldn’t
have been rejected without serious consideration.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, October 31, 1899.
My Dear Myee.—
Of course, last week was given over to the war-
fever, body and soul. At the shipping offices for
the E. and A.S.O. wharf it was a wild farce on
Friday to see the swelled heads of all the little
clerks and office-boys, refusing wharf tickets for
the setting out of the troops. The little boys on
sixpence a week put on the airs of the big boys in
frightfully stand-up collars, and the boys in the
frightfully stand-up collars put on the airs of the
managers, while the managers themselves kept
out of sight behind locked doors, and the office
was full of indignant female shrieks in fifty dif-
ferent sharps and flats, down to “yous boys, don’t
know a lady when you sees her ! ” The collar-
boys poured forth much cheek, and there was
nearly a scene when the Sydney woman whose
name stands for gold was told by an underdone
errand-hoy, “ Can’t let you have a ticket, madam,
but / shall be knocking about the wharf, and
might get you in if I see you.”
Saturday afternoon saw the war-fever at its
height, although women and babies began to pour
into the town from early in the morning. Any-
thing to mako a day of it, even if it's only to see
Tommy Green or Jimmy Jones going out to be
slaughtered. That’s quite enough excuse for Mrs.
Browne to get out baby’s new pelisse, and the
children’s starched frocks, and her own red hat
with the green roses under the brim, and lock up
the house, and go forth with a bottle of cold tea,
and some mutton sandwiches, and stand all day in
the rain, all for the sake of making a day of it.
The Quay abounded with these little coteries,
rain-sodden and wailful, come to the bottom of the
cold tea, an hour to wait before the soldiers would
pass where none of the Brownes could see them,
the baby crouping, the children crying, the mother
tired and a bit uneasy about Bill’s tea which she
hadn’t prepared, but all understanding that these
things had to be when it was a day out.
. The wharf agents sent out four hundred invita-
tions for a lunch on their wharf. I hear it ran to
a guinea a head, but his Ex. presided, and all the
leading lunch-eaters came along, and the air of the
lunch shed was heavy with champagne and
loyalty, so the wharf-agents got some return for
their guinea anyway, to say nothing of the ad.
As soon after lunch as possible (considering that
things were already a little sodden) the rain began
to fall. It came down in torrents, almost without
ceasing, for hours. Women tried to scramble up
on board the Kent, but a water-policeman coura-
geously drove them back. Everything was as
badly managed as possible. No shelter, a great
fuss over tickets, and everyone regarded as an
interloper. It was quite a study in good inten-
tions (that always lead their owners astray) to see
the frocks and hats women were wearing. Nearly
everyone had dressed for a fine day, with an eye
to the fact that all the soldiers were men, which
also included the thought that there would be
crowds of women about. White frocks and leg-
horns punctuated brown hollands and flower-
toques, and blue and mauve parasols rained blue
and mauve streaks down their wearers’cheeks in
the weirdest way. Item : If you must take a
colored parasol out on a wet day, let me implore
you to let it be pink. Pink splashes can even
be made use of with a handkerchief artfully em-
ployed, but blue drops—and, above all, purple
drops— are death to any complexion, from milk-
white blonde to burnt sienna. I noticed among
the wives of the departing, Mrs. Roth and her
three children saying good-bye to that popular
medico, Dr. Roth. Local artists sent the doctor a
special farewell in the shape of cigars and sketches.
“ Banjo ’’Paterson, going as war-correspondent
to S.M. Herald, looked a little glum about it
I thought, but it might have been the natural ex-
pression of his face, which is awfully lined and
worn-looking in repose. Then there was lovely
Horace Dangar, strewing the way behind him
with broken hearts.
His Ex., in a thin, grey coat and bell-topper,
boarded the Kent about 3 o’clock, and stood in the
rain for half an hour. Women admired the reck-
lessness of Captain Wilfred Smith, who came
without an umbrella and let his top hat go to pulp
in the rain. After half-an-hour these two thin
young men found their smiles drawing near a
close, and departed, as there didn’t seem to be
anything for them to do or say. I thought you
might like to know that the crowd cheered wildly
as His Ex. drove away down the avenues of
people. It hadn’t leaked out then that H. E. had
fired off a little more poetry at the lunch, but His
Ex’s, last quote was on the safe side of everything,
though it looks dangerous when you glance at the
front line only :
“ Slips of the stock there is no up-rooting,
Talking the tongue no tyrants gag.
Like young lions to help the old one,
Swift of footfall and firm of poise.
By Jove that foeman will be a bold one
Who'll face us banded, my boys, my boys.”
(Loud cheers, and everybody beginning to breathe
again.)
“ The Kent ” itself was in a wild state of con-
fusion. Everything half-finished, the horse com-
partments stdl being builded, carrots and wet
straw lying all over the place. When the troops
went on board the crowd on the wharf raised a few
cheers, but enthusiasm was not the leading feature
of the send-off. On the whole, the troops had a
cheerful air, which must not be altogether at-
tributed to their stout hearts, I fear, as whisky
figured largely in the proceedings, and many men
lost their sea-legs before they got them as ir, were.
Towards the last, before the boat moved off, there
was a good deal of weeping among the younger
troops. Onopretty boy named Reggie was hugged
openly by 15 girls in succession, and mingled 15
lots of tears, but the knowledge that the boat was
going to anchor down the harbour till Monday
morning rather took the edge off the farewells.
And some hearts failed them at the last.
The news of General Symons’ death is said
to be responsible for tho scare ; but, any-
way, quite a number volunteered and ac-
cepted “sojers” wero found missing at the
final moment. At the same time our Colonel
Smith is known to be eating his head off because
he can’t get out to the war. He had the hard
luck to got his Colonial Commission just in time
to prevent him going with Kitchener to Khar-
toum, which he has openly lamented ever since ;
and now the chance of slaughter has been snatched
out of his hands a second time. They wouldn’t
let him go from Sydney.
Probably the war scare has had something to do
with Maud MaeCarthy’s concert tour, which
hasn’t been the wild Hibernian or Horsetralian
boom anticipated. Her audiences have been
moderately large only. Wednesday's matinee
drew the best house, composed of women in rose-
pink or turquoise-blue hats. Maud doesn’t fiddle
away the heart and soul and body and legs of her
houses, leaving palpitating masses of millinery in
the chairs ; her playing is not the sort to rouse
uneasy demons, and paint dazzling red lines
across the eyeballs ; but it induces you to shut
your eyes, and listen quietly to interpretations
that satisfy the mind and keep tho emotions
gently gratified. And her freshness is a charm
which cannot be discounted.
Also, Maud Macarthy has been robbed of her
birthright of talent by the wild, red-headed
Hibernian advertising that lias gone on outside
her concert management. A pamphlet was
scattered through the R.C. churches, printed in
green letters, and headed, “The Divine Maud
Macarthy.” One gemtny passago ran : “If in
olden times, when Ireland was famed for its
Musical Culture, Erin’s harp shed the soul of
Music thro’ Tara’s Halls, that soul has come down
through generations to descend upon this little
girl-genius whose genius sheds that soul of musio
in the Town Hall here. Let it not be said here-
after that the Irish people here were insensible to
heavenly strains such as produced by this sublime
artist.” That alone was enough to empty the
house of musical Australians.
Another sufferer from the War was “Dreyfus” at
the Criterion last week. r lhe war scare put him
out of time. A month ago what a sensation that
play might have made ; but a month, and what a
difference! That great chameleonic beast, the
Public, had turned away from the Dreyfus case,
and the play would only crawl for one week. 11 was
said to be taken off out of respect for the French
Consul’s corns, which must be enormous growths
as there was always plenty of room for them to take
their ease in the d.e. Lilian Wheeler as Madame
Dreyfus had the irritating qualities of a picture
without any perspective. To play a woman who
is alive and ordinary to look at, without mimicry
or caricature, is like throwing uncooked vegetables
into the teeth of the audience. Luckily, Miss
Lilian Wheeler always dresses well, and in a lovely
Frenchy-looking black cape with steel pailettes
studding the surface, and a little red and yellow
toque on her head, she carried out admirably
Stead’s portrait of Lucie Dreyfus : “ A lady, tall
and majestic in stature, with large expressive eyes
and luxuriant hair.”
But melo-dramatising the things of the day is a
dangerous experiment, generally resulting in bur-
lesque. For instance, one scene shewed Dreyfus
on tho “lie du Diable,” going through horrible
tortures. Enter the brutal minion with some-
thing in a bottle, “This’ll do for him,” says
he, “ I’ll put it in his bed, and when it stings he'll
rush out, and the guards will think he’s attempt-
ing flight and will shoot him on the doorstep.”
He goes over to Dreyfus’ bed, turns back the
blankets, and tucks away carefully a scorpion. This
is too much for the house, whioh goes off into fits
of laughter.
If all you hear is only half untrue Society
didn’t get much change out of a late venture,
when six Society women tried to stop a town man
from maurying the girl of his choice. They called
on him in a body, clad in their sweetest smiles
and best frocks, implored him to think what
an awful thing lie was doing in marrying a
girl none of them knew, don’t you know, and
insinuated pitilessly that their visiting cards could
never, never by any chance find their way to
her card tray. Result—what might have been
expected. Wedding duly came off. Of course
you can’t expect Society to know all about any-
thing so poor and naked and common and con-
trariwise as human nature. Otherwise the inter-
fering parrots would have urged on tho marriage
all they knew by way of stopping it.
Which reminds mo that a Sydney girl—young,
fascinating—went out into the garden with a Ger-
man officer, who inquired tenderly as they reached
the lawn, “Mees, are you married or single? ”
“ Single, ” she said. He immediately turned round
and led her back towards the ball-room.
Time to turn round and say “Do” to those
about to marry when a round-eyed German
officer, quite underdone and harmless, won’t flirt
unless his partner is married. By the way, the
man who made that London Punch joke, “Don’t,”
got £~) for it, and is still bving in Melbourne. And
his name is Stiffe, if anybody wants any excuse for
the dearth of proposals to Melbourne society girls
lately.
A wedding came off in Sydney last week that
was rewarded with two thousand pounds worth of
silver presents from one jeweller’s alone. Which
suggests the highly moral reflection that tho Jin-
de-sdicle marriage is an awfully losing game for
those who are out of it.
The latest engagement is very young and very
theatrical. Carrie Moore and Sydney Bracy, if
you please.
Paling and Co. send me a rather belated waltz,
“1 he Marguerite,” as simple as a daisy verily,
anti rather sweet. From the same co. conies
“ I ho Wheel Waltz,” by Lydia Larmer, dedicated
to the Cyclists’ Union, a well-written waltz and
spirited enough, to stir a biker to lap in double
time. Paling and Co. send also “Sons of the
Empire,” a good, loud, patriotic song which shouts
for “ Defence, not Defiance,” and puts to tuneful
music every sentiment for the crushing of Britain’s
foes.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The Victorian Contingent had probably gone
through all the hardships of its campaign when it
reached the Medic on Saturday, and hastened to
rest its poor feet. ‘Our Boys’ had had a tough
day, and had suffered many trials and tribulations,
and the greatest of these were ‘ Old Tom ’ and
new boots. ‘Old Tom ’is the irreverent appella-
tion bestowed upon His Excellency by ‘the mili-
tary.’ After mooching about for hours in a parti-
cularly atrocious brand of Govt, boot that pinched
all the enthusiasm out of the troops, the unfor-
tunates were stood on end in a grilling sun in the
barrack yard and talked at till kingdom-come by
* Me Bud,’ whose speech was like the murmur of
innumerable bees. Commandant Hoiled-Smiill’s
short, sharp ten words following sounded very
like a stinging reproach to His Ex., but his lord-
ship ‘was not taking any,’ and for the rest of the
day radiated self-complacence like a little pink
sun.
“They worked the show well up to time, how-
ever, and the Medio moved olf from the pier to
the tick, as per advt. The great boat seemed very
many sizes too large for the job, and when the
two contingents—Vic. and Tas. —ran aboard, they
were almost immediately lost in the immensity of
the terrific steamer. The lot might easily havo
been stowed in her funnel. The engagement of
the Medic was a mistake from a stage-manager’s
point of view ; that thin streak of drab along her
bulwarks, and the dots of red scattered far and
wide on her acres of deck, provided a ruinous
anti climax as the boat put off—the whole silly
business was reduced suddenly to its proper pro-
portions, and nobody on the pier had spirit enough
left to wring out the feeblest cheer. The sailors
on the flagship at some distance cheered to order,
but not one solitary spontaneous whoop signalised
the departure of the saviours of an empire.
“I witnessed only one touch of true feeling
throughout the day, and that was supplied by a
dusty, grey-haired, shabby woman leaning against
a ring-post on the pier, apart from the push, weep-
ing desolately. Other women wept, but they were
nicely dressed, and wept beamingly and with due
consideration for the spectators. That dreary,
sunburnt, weary woman by the post provided a
genuine note, all the rest seemed garish, fussy,
stertorous, red-faced, and somewhat drinky
pretence.
“Like most melodramas, the departure of the
contingent relied upon its comic Irishman for
humorous relief, the part being taken by Dr. Tay
Pay Mclnerney, warden of the University senate.
Dr. Mac’s younger brother (Tim, bedad) was a
lieutenant of infantry and while he headed the
contingent, the fond doctor marched alongside
with expanded chest during the whole route of
the procession. In his right hand he held his
brolly at the ‘carry’ and in turning corners he
marked time with the rest of the men. His black
frock coat and belltopper caused most of the
spectators to regard him as the official undertaker
and the obvious fact that he was the proudest man
in the procession was understood to be due to
business prospects. At the Treasury, a battered
vagrant who had somehow broken through the
cordon of police, didn’t see why there shouldn’t be
two civilians in the show and chipped in behind
the Mclnerney brothers until a dutiful policeman
threw him out.
“ A fig for the romance of war! An over-
womaned world is poor soil for sentiment. These
soldier-massacres entomb men and romance ‘in
one red burial blent.’ Trained nurses, univer-
sally respected nowadays as champion husband-
snatchers, promptly recognised the danger of this
military exodus and volunteered to go marching
with Johnnie—a preferable role to that of the
girls Johnnieleavesbehind them. Talking of attrac-
tive nurses, one of our metropolitan hospitals
habitually sees its junior surgeon woo and win a
member of the nursing-staff, which entails eva-
cuating his position in favor of an unmarried suc-
cessor. One of these victims is now hastening
back from his American home to the side of his
fiancee—an Australian ministering angel. •It
would seem that there are more uncongenial
spheres for women workers than hospital wards,
judging by the fact that a beautiful ex-nurse tired
of her new commercial venture after a month’s
trial.
