“ There’s an epidemic of influenza, and a deso-
late dearth of dressmakers’ hands. What this
means will be understood when it is stated that
one establishment had 500 dresses on order for the
Cup.
“The sudden death of white-vested Zox,
M.L. A., didn’t come as a great surprise to the
Melbourne people who had noted his sick and
shrunken appearance some few months ago. Zox
seemed to pull round again, but when a man of
his habit has once “ broken down ” —in the ordi-
nary bon-vivanl way of speaking—he dies easily.
Rubicund Ephraim succumbed to pneumonia
through not having gone to bed with the influ-
enza. His bachelorhood found him out. Had he
been married his wife would have rushed him
into taking care of himself, and the historic white
waistcoat of.a cheery old'identity would not now
be vacant. Mr. Zox was never married, but 18
years ago—good Jew that he was—he cast a
roving eye on a handsome ‘shikser’ girl—daughter
of a British army bandmaster who served in
Maoriland —and refrained from making her his
wife only because he feared his heart-trouble
might leave her a widow at an early date. But
the Juno-like Nellie MTlrath died many years
before the kindly Hebrew who loved her.

Princess Theatre on Monday night, when a couple
of thousand counter-jumpers crowded in to create
the funniest function of the wholeyear. A starved
lion in his native lair is a mild and unassuming
animal to the average sock-seller when he gets
loyalty had, and the patriotic softgoods youths
amused themselves by bawling the following pro-
gramme, which is well worth giving in full':—

Lion,’ ‘The Battle Eve,’ ‘The Death of Nelson,’ ‘ Com-
rades,’‘ Hoys of the Old Brigade,’ ‘ Let Me like a Soldier
Jail,’ ‘T'lmny Atkins,’ ‘ Remember AJajuba,’ ‘Rule
Britannia,' ‘ God Save the Queen.’
In the interval the brightest joke of the whole
evening took place : The curtain rose and Robert
Reid, M.L.C., and a thin, anaemic-looking young
man in a badly-fitting JChakee suit were “dis-
covered ” in the middle of the Palace of Henri of
Navarre. Strangers in the house thought it was
an “introduced” music-hall item, and they pre-
pared to laugh at Reid’s rendering of “Casey’s Cat”
or something; butitturned outthatthe M.L.C. was
in dead earnest. It was his function to agonisingly
high-falute. about “ the snows of Canada and the
heated Hi ma!ayas,” and to present a silver wliisky-
flask to the anaemic young man.
. To the door of St. Peter’s (Melb.) a prayer-box
is affixed, and into this you may drop a slip of
paper on which you have scribbled your special
need, and then ‘ the prayers of the congregation
are desired and your troubles are carried by
deputation before Heaven. It saves time, of
course, and gives weight to the fact that your soul
is in need of repair or that your body is in poor
trim. For several weeks prayers were desired for
the success of a certain tea-room !
Once upon a time the male sinner always
traced his ruin to “Wine and Women!” The
latter has lost her nip, or man has found an anti-
dote thereto, for the present-day Ulysses is
calmly callous to the siren, whatever form she
takes. It’s nothing new, though hard on the well-
brought-up spinster, that eligibles no longer fre-
quent churches or ball-rooms. But even the stage
h.ouri must content herself with feminine adora-
tion, these times.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
David Syme’s double in Melbourne is a
doorkeeper at the Princess’.
Ex-Premier Mcll wraith, of Queensland,
was seen lately in Hyde Park, London, hopelessly
paralysed.
Four N.S.W. District Court Judges now
receive pensions of .£7so—Forbes, McFarland,
Dowling, Wilkinson.
A NIGHT MEMORY OF THE LATE E. L. ZOX.
The widow of the first Bishop Selwyn, of
Maoriland, celebrated her 90th birthday, at Lich-
field, Eng., last month, four generations of
Selwyns being present.
Of the seven Australasian Premiers one
only—Lewis, of Tasmania —received a University
education. Several University men are found in
the rank and file in all the provinces.
“Bill” Lane leaves Cosm6 (the revised
New Australia) at last. He is “ still in sym-
pathy,” etc. ; but prefers to take his chances in
Maoriland —as a sort of half-way house, no doubt.
One Legislative Councillor wanted to go
to the Transvaal under arms, or said he did—A.
R. Addison, of S.A., who volunteered as a sub-
altern without pay. A good shot, but aged 57, so
they passed him bv.
Dr. Andrew Oar ran, member of N.S.W.
Public Works Committee, ex-Vice-President
N.S.W. Leg. Council, ex-editor S.M. Herald,
will reach his 74th year, Nov. 19. He arrived in
A ustralia 48 years ago.
Chairman of Metrop. Board of Works
Fitzgibbon declares it to be “ a gross slander upon,
and a disgrace and wrong to the colony ” that the
necessity for an enquiry into the workings of his
Board “should have been suggested.” Every
idea of Fitz’s. is an iipmaoulate conception.
Among the newly-appointed N.S. Wales
Js.P. is Daniel Mayne, Esq., of Windsor—who,
by the way, is one of triplets. Dan is a native of
that town, but for many years resided in Bathurst,
where he' owned the intensely decorous Sentinel
newspaper. Jaypees are indeed a mixed lot.
The Lyne Government is likely to be
known as the “ Testimonial Ministry,” on account
of the many letters one Minister appears to have
written testifying to the large number of fatal
complaints of which he was cured at different
times by the use of somebody’s Aggravated Pills
and Ointment.
“Redspinner” Senior, the best-known
angling writer in England, has just retired from
the command of the staff of London Daily News.
He was the first chief of Queensland Hansard,
and writer remembers him in London as a quiet,
iron-grey, kindly little gentleman, very much
inclined to talk fish.
Ernest Giles, on his last exploring expedi-
tion into Centralia, 15 years or so ago, took with
his party a youngster, of whose pluck (they had a
brush or two with the natives) he afterwards
made much. The boy turned up the other day as
an applicant for more shooting—he is Major
V. H. Edwards, of the S.A. Artillery, now. But
his rank, for one thing, barred him.
“G. 8.”: In Bulletin par. dealing with
Tasmanian Premiers, it is stated the late Richard
Dry was not a native of the island. Error, His
father, exiled for ’9B business in Ireland, married
in Launceston, where Dry was born.
Bishop Stone Wigg, of Papua, walks in
the footsteps of “ martyred ” Bishop Patteson, of
Melanesia. He does a little carpentering from
time to time, helps the natives to lay out their
houses, and now and again washes and dresses the
little niggers. This last used to be Patteson s
regular morning’s work.
N.S.W. now sports two ex-
Premiers —Dibbs and Reid. Victoria
hobbles along under five—Berry, Gil-
lies, Munro, O’Loghlen, and Sbiels.
The reopening of the discus-
sion about Hargraves and McGregor,
and other such persons, credited as the
first discoverers of gold in Australia,
should, if the subject is ever to have
an end, suggest a division of titles.
Then there will be a lot of “firsts,”
and the descendants of all will pro-
bably feel satisfied and let the matter
drop. Eor instance, Hargreaves was
the first discoverer of gold in such
quantities and conditions, that N.S. W.
Govt, circulated goldfield regulations,
and proceeded to treat gold-production
as a national industry. No doubt
Mr. M. Cohen is correct in saying he
handled McGregor’s gold in 1847. But
Tolmein was before that with a nugget
(in 1844). Rev. W. B. Clarke handed
over to the Legislature gold found by
him in ’4l. Count Strzlecki found
some in ’39, and convicts found some
at Bathurst in 1813. All these and
others may be made “ firsts ” in such
a controversy, but to keep the contro-
versialists from merely beating air,
each should further define his meaning
of the word “ first.”
At the time of Kipling’s visit to
Australia, he was banqueted by a pro-
minent Melb. club and the late Pre-
mier Patterson, who was then in office,
was invited to preside. On the morn-
ing of the banquet Patterson button-
holed a couple of Age men, and asked,
“ Who is this Kipling ? ” He was told
that the visitor was a poet of Imper-
ialistic tendencies, and, in the re-
porters’ room of the Age, a brief
discourse on his work together with
a few eloquent notes were given to
the Premier. Eight hours later he
turned up at the gorge and delivered
an excellent address, embodying most
of Kipling’s notable points and “ prov-
ing himself,” as one of the reporters
aforesaid stated in his copy, “ a keen
and thorough student of the writer’s
works.” Patterson really carried the
assemblage with him, such a brilliant
bluffer was he, and though he had
really never opened a single book of
Kipling’s his speech, from the opening
quote “ What should they know of
England who only England know?”
down to the final pathetic reference to
“ The Light that Failed ” showed
that he knew his Kipling from A
to Z.
The passing out of Ephraim Zox, M.L.A.,
didn’t eclipse the gaiety of Melbourne, for in the
first place Melbourne was never very gay, and in
the next place punster Zox was not at all an up-
to-date humorist. But it may be said that the
city where Zox lived for 47 years (he was a boy of
13 when he landed there) felt a twinge of regret
on learning that its old acquaintance would never
again straddle up and down Collins-street with
his hands in his trousers’ pockets. Everybody
knew Zox as a much paragraphed and caricatured
identity. Many people knew him as a good
Samaritan in private life, not less than a useful
public character. The name of Zox, the Jew,
came to be respected in connection with all
political work which smacks of what is called
“ Christian Charity,” and though he was but an
easy-going philanthropist who took much liquid
refreshment on the journey he contrived to cover
a good deal of ground. A cockney by birth, Zox
was, among other things, president of the Mel-
bourne branch of the Anglo- Jewish Association,
hence the fervent jingoism of his last speech in the
Vic. Assembly. Naturally enough he barracked
for the Hempire that has been least inhospitable
to the Chosen.
“G.B. I was fellow-passenger once
with Sir John O’Shanassy on a coasting boat. “Big
John ” was then Victorian Premier, and I was
not a little astonished when, having picked
the bones of a fowl in aboriginal fashion, he
threw them on the cabin floor, to the
ineffable disgust of the prim and dandified
cockney steward, who presently picked up
the bones and calmly placed them on a
serviette beside the knight’s plate. The old
warrior looked at the bones, then at the
man—“ Don’t bother yourself, me good mahn,”
said he, “ Oi’ve done with them.”
How old is Wilson Barrett ? The London
Dramatic Peerage recently set the year of his
birth down as 1838, whereat the old mummer
protested that he is ten years behind 61. The
Bulletin will only swear that Barrett has a very
elderly voice, whatever the age of his head and
legs may be.
There are now in Australia several persons
practising both law and medicine at the same
time : among them Dr. Todd, the Sydney deputy-
coroner. The combined enterprise, however, is
not new. Forty years ago Dr. Belinfante dealt
in law and medicine at Mudgee and thereabouts.
He practised both assiduously, going from
the courts to his patients, and sometimes
when he was wanted critically as a phy- ,
sician obtaining a stay of court pro-
ceedings till his return. He tried
to spread into a third sphere (Parlia-
ment), but David Buchanan knocked
him out. Dr. Belinfante fell into the
river at Cudgegong and was drowned.
The champion name for undeciphera-
bility is Cummings. Everybody who is blessed
(or cursed) with this monniker seems to run all his
letters together into a meaningless succession of
mere curves.
On Oct. 21 there died at Richmond,
N.S.W., Mrs. Mary Ann Search, aged 99 years
and nine months. She was a Hawkesbury native
and believed to be the oldest. She was the
mother of 14 children, three only of whom survive
her. Her eldest son, now in his 80th year, is a
resident of Mitta Mitta, Victoria.
Willoughby Andrew having completed
nine years in N.S.W. gaols, chiefly Darlinghurst,
for shooting Bartlett, the King-street draper, is
now pressing might and main for release. The
change of N.S.W. Govt, appears to have inspired
him with fresh hope, and acquaintances and others
are assailed with importunate prayers.
The eldest son of Slangdon Bonython, of
S.A. Advertiser, volunteered for anti-Boer ser-
vice—mainly for the encouragement of the others,
it seems; for the fearfully patriotic newspaper
knight was grieved at the slowness with which
the local slashers come forward. Young Bony-
thon is a lieut. in the Machine Gun Corps.

the scaly Dreyfus business—that Catholics were
right “ not to espouse the cause of a Semite
accused of treason,” London Times makes the
crushing reply that the Catholic Church claims as
its founder “a Semite accused of treason.” A
more perfect “counter ” was never delivered.

aforesaid would have pocketed .£980,000 commis-
sion.

Two weeks back Melbourne Age scoffed at
the Boers as cowards. Now, the Leader man in-
forms the largest circulation in Australia that
they are undoubtedly brave men. By-and-bye,
when these ignorant savages chance somehow,
just by way of a change, to win a battle, they
will be discovered to possess considerable military
genius. David is the king dodger, nowadays.
Time was when any statement once appeared in his
paper, it was held to, though proved to be a lie as
big as Papua. But now —the chameleon isn’t
in it.
The late E. L. Zox, speaking one time at
Melb. Federal Palace Hotel (nee Coffee Palace),
on the question of maintaining temperance prin-
ciples (at a loss) in the management of such
places, remarked that a Gentile director who
objects to supplying his customers with whatever
drink they wane is as ridiculously uncommercial
as a Jew director who might want to debar them
from pork. The harmless old joker never missed
a chance to give a Hebrew turn to his argument.
He played the part of a liberal-minded Jew, and
did it very well.
Lord Hopetoun, travelling in Vic-
toria, was once entertained by a blunt old
farmer, and expressed himself in the usual
after-dinner fashion as hopeful of some time
again enjoying his host’s hospitality. This
brought the farmer to his feet in an in-
stant. “Oh, no, you don’t, my lord!” he
exclaimed. “Once is good enough for me.
I’ve done my best for you, and done it
willin’; but I’d just like you to remember
as there’s plenty of others in this ’ere town
better able to stand the blanky expense
than me. You just give one of them a turn
nex’ time.”
“Dick” : Have often heard my
grandfather (still alive) relate his first ex-
perience on reaching Melbourne, early in
1836, with Gov. Bourke, in H.M.S. Rattle-
snake. A party from the vessel was rowed
up the Yarra Yarra. The first place they
struck was Batman’s shanty, where the pre-
sent railway offices stand. My grand-dad
called for drinks—five brandies —and handed
Batman a Sydney pound-note. He replied:
“Five glasses brandy and two shillings ex-
change. That’s right ; thank you, gentl’inen.”
John D. Cave, who died the other day,
close upon 80 years old, was about the best-known
and physically, perhaps, the biggest figure in S.
Aus. municipal politics outside the city. For 30
years he was at the Burra, for some time as the
colleague of Treasurer Holder, who got all his
financial experience as the local town clerk. Also,
he was an expert flower-raiser—a chrysanthemum
and some other things are named J. D. Cave.
Died, the other day, Philip Sellheim,
Under Sec. for Q. Mines. Sellheim was an Aus-
trian born, of aristocratic lineage. Arrived in
1855 and took his first lessons in colonial experi-
ence on the Dawson. Went exploring in ’59 in
the North (Kennedy), and was appointed warden
of the Palmer in ’74. Served successively in this
capacity at Ch. Towers and Gytnpie, and, finally,
in ’92 received position of Under Sec. for Mines.
An able man, thoroughly versed in mining
matters, and widely known to diggers as a decent
fellow.

Aboriginalities.
Bulletin artists (a correspondent sug-
gests) should be invited to give us a new edition
of the bush parson. Writer has been taking stock
of the good shepherds of the long-way-back ; and
not one ancient person with an ancient moke and
a monkey-shave is so far down on the list. Three-
fourths of them go about on bikes (most of the
machines are named “The Truth ” or “ The Gos-
pel Bearer,” or something), and four-fifths of
them are comparative juveniles either too
young or too illiterate for regular churches. The
allowances made to these by the bodies that send
them out would shame the devil. One, whom
writer got a pannikin of tea from lately, had
run out of coin and shoe-leather and bike-tyres
simultaneously, and had taken a job with a drov-
ing party as cook—the proper cook had, it seemed,
a touch of snakes. He reckoned he would be able
to start in the vineyard again inside a month, and
looked forward hopefully to plucking the cook
from the burning a s a start.
* • • *
“ Snell ” : An identity revives tor me
an early-day Vio. tragedy. An old swagman,
one of the first of his class in Gippsland, had been
engaged for a few weeks on a cattle run, and after
a week there were strange rumors amongst the
men about a bag of gold that the stranger carried
with him. The old man was exclusive,
but he was seen at night in his hut count-
ing a heap of sovereigns. Then came a morning
when a convict named Elliot was missing from
the station, and the old man was found lying in
the hut with his throat cut from ear to ear. On
the table and about the floor were scattered scores
of “sovereigns”—clumsy brass tokens with a
brandy advt. stamped on one side, and the King’s
head on the other.

Some figures from a small Victorian farm.
Area, 109 acres (crop 22 ; grass 87) ; distance from
Melbourne, 75 miles; cattle and horses carried,
54 ; also a small herd of pigs and some sheep.
Milk alone in ’9B realised, <£536 7s. 7d. ; each cow
(40) averaging .£l3 Bs. The sale of fat cows,
calves, bacon, eggs, fowls, and fruit gave another
£ll7, making a total of £653 for the year.
Through careful breeding and culling, the yield
and quality of the milk is steadily improving; so
the owner is sanguine he will do much better this
year. Pays his own girls and boys, who help
work the place, the ruling rate of wages (a sensible
idea that is spreading amongst Vic. dairy-farm-
ers). Originally a ganger on the line ; he has now
got a much better “ collar ” and deserves it.
* • * *
The amendment by Brown, M.L.A., for
Croydon, on the Aborigines Protection Amending
Bill lately carried in committee of Q. Assembly,
prohibiting the employment of natives by colored
aliens, will legislate against one of the most crying
evils in the North. In many parts the Chinese
literally own whole tribes of aboriginals, body and
soul (what there is left of them), the secret of their
influence being, of course, opium. The native will
do anything for the drug, and the women are as
crafty after the inferior charcoal as the men, with
extremely edifying results. One of these is a crop
of virulent diseases among white Northern
nomads that would appal the public, if statistics
were available. No wonder the patent-pill man
flourishes. But what will the next generation be
like?
• * * •
A Two wharves Tragedy. A canecutter
and a timbergetter quarrelled about religion. The
latter unyoked his set of false teeth and gave them
to a friend to hold. Then he jumped into the fray,
which was peculiar and protracted. In the ex-
citement of following the fight, which covered a
few acres, the teeth were lost. After the fight,
victor and vanquished looked for the former’s
teeth. The plate was found at last, badly crumpled
up, and minus several molars. “ Wat can I do
with these?” “ Well, I dinno. Ya might hang
’em on ya watch-chine.” “ I mite hit ya unda th’
ear.” “Ya might not.” “Well—thereyeare!”
And at it the pair went again. This time the
canecutter flaked a win, and later on the com-
batants retired to the Canecutters’ Curse Hotel.
A hawker gave “five bob’’for the plate, and a
beer-chewing contest wound up the situation.
A “steep”

Re the branding of
aboriginals in Wes-
tralia. On a N.W.
station I have seen
several niggers
branded with the
initial letter of their
master’s name. The
branding is usually
on the chest (sex
not considered), but
in one case I saw a
mob of blacks bear-
ing a big “ V ” both
on breast and back.
They were “o w ned ”
by an ex-minister
of the Crown.
* * *
Amongst a well-
known Coolgardie
official’s numerous
crammers he relates
that once, when he
was walking on the
Lighthouse Cliff at
Fremantle, his dog
gave chase to a goat
which made off
down the Cliff with
the dog after him.
Blank scrambled
down himself as best
way could, and
found the goat had
taken to the water
and was making for
Rottnest Island,
the dog swimming
gallantly in chase.
Not wishing to lose
his dog, Blank
promptly stripped
and followed, but
had to cover three
miles or so before he
overtook the pair,
which he did just as
the pup had got its
teeth into the goat.
Blank seized them
both, swam back
with the pup under
one arm and the
goat under the
other, and the
moment he reached
shore again was ar-
rested for bathing
in a publio place—
a charge that cost
him £5.

Pellew from , which is notoriously insalubrious. Now which

“ Yangan Be matters missionary (“ Von
Kotze”and “Old Soldier”) I say broadly that
the system is wrong, (but so are the men—and
more so the women. Admitting bona tides in the
majority, what qualification has the average
missionary ? Merely a laudable desire to do good
combined with a knack of doing incalculable
harm. Also, let me ask, is N.S. Wales so superior
to Heaven that while we cheerfully subscribe to
pass the Chow into Paradise we charge him <£loo
a-head for admission to the province. As for the
girl-missionary, it might interest her to know that
Chinamen (amongst whom I was brought up)
i,rgue that no marriageable girl reniains un-
married without very good cause. A girl-mission-
ary who loses her life in China has her martyrdom
well advertised, but the missions keep a discreet
silence when something that should be even more
precious goes a-missing. John has only one name
for all unattached females —“ prostitute,” so the
girl-missionary’s moral influence may be gauged.
To sum up, hundreds of sincere men and women
in China are simply wasting their lives in stirring
up strife, thereby retarding civilization, making
the lives of lay residents harder, and producing a
crop of absolutely bogus “ converts.”

Dear B. —Re alligator-eating (“F.P.,”
B. 21/10/’99), I have often tasted “ally” in
Borneo and found the flavor, as described, like
ling, but with a musky twang. Swore off alliga-
tor, as well as off Borneo wild pig, after I had
seen both eating dead Chinaman. By the way,
Bulletin writers often confound alligators with
crocodiles. The true alligator, outside America,
is only found as a small species, recently dis-
covered in the Yangtze River (China). I person-
ally remember the capture of the first specimen
near Shanghai in the ’Bo’s. It still puzzles scien-
tists how the alligators got “into than galley.”—
C.

practice by putting a dummy man into his stall.

wards attempted to bite.

“C.R.” on the tick question :
Queensland Minister Chataway says he is not aware that
ticks are being carried by sheep and horses, and is now
applying to the inspectors for information. If N.S.W.
Stock Department has any doubt on the question let a
man endowed with common-sense (no expert required) be
sent, say, only to Rockhampton, and then travel down to
Maryborough and examine the horses en route. I doubt
if he will find many free from ticks. I have not seen
many free. Writer sawa“ticky” horse to which the
owner (in order to take the animal to Sydney by boat)
gave a hard 3-mile gallop, after which, when presented
for examination, no ticks were visible, and the horse was
thus passed. As soon as a horse gets up a good sweat the
ticks either drop off or crawl on to the rider. They do
not thrive as well on horses as on cattle. At present the
N.S.W. authorities have the matter in their own hands,
arid any loose dealing now will be nothing short of
oriminal.
* * * *
There was trouble at a Gippsland bush-
wedding the other night. It began when the
bridegroom rushed from the centre of revelry and
smote a tall man standing out in the moonlight,
and it was busy for about 15min., during which
time the bridegroom and his enemy wore each
others’ clothes off, and worried each other in a
manner shocking to see. The wedding-party
gathered around to see fair-play, and they watched
the tornado in a kindly and impartial spirit. At
length the bridegroom arose up out of the small
duststorm in a dismantled condition, with his
clothes in strips and his features banked up and
inverted, and he shook a mighty fist over his
fallen foe, and gasped, “ That’ll teach you to sing
‘ Ain’t a-goin’ to Tell ’ at my blanky weddin’ 1 ”

“ Bucan ” : Be recent talk about Boer
marksmanship. Near Mt. Jacob, recently, I saw
a youth drop consecutively 12 topknot pigeons
with a Winchester rifle, in every case cutting, or
nearly cutting, the head off. This would take
some beating.
* • • •
The cunning of foxes is exciting remark in
Victoria, and instances have been quoted in the
Leg. Council in support of a heavier scalping fee.
A very cunning animal was met with in the
vicinity of Hamilton. She used to steal fat
pullets from a local hen-roost, and distribute in-
criminating feathers about the hut of a neighbor-
ing Sin Fat. When the Chow was tried for
stealing the chickens he declared that the fox wa3
the real culprit, and that she scattered the
feathers about his hut because he had discovered
and destroyed her litter. Sin got four months
just the same, but it is noteworthy that there
were no feathers to be found anywhere about his
hut during the term of his imprisonment. Pro-
bably the revenge was complete.

“M‘Goosley,” from North Queensland: —
At last the “ very tired” inhabitants of the Gulf Towns
mo.) have taken up a big J-pen and through the press
have aired a grievance which only the people of the
Gulf would have stood so long. There was some trouble
over the mail-contract with the shipping co’s., and the
consequence was a strike on the part of said co’s. There
has been no direct communication with the Gulf from
Brisbane for the last six months. If you wanted to go to
Noruiantou, Croydon, or Burketown, you took China or
Jap. boat to Thursday Island. Then came the picnio.
The only craft plving down the Gulf from the Island was
the s.s. Adelaide. In comparison with the Ade-
laida a North Shore ferry-boat is something like
an ocean-liner. Conceive a little river tub with
no deck accommodation, and a little square-box of
a place termed a “ saloon.” Ye gods! if I could only
describe a passage (taking three days) down the Gulf, in
the saloon of the Adelaide 1 About the size of a decent
room, with shelves (bunks) all round, and a table in the
centre. The table pretty well takes up the floor-space,
and you can squeeze in between it and the bottom
“shelf." No screens or covers to get behind. I dressed
on the table. “Keep your singlet out of the butter!”
After meals (served on enamelware), I climbed back to
my shelf— nowhere else to go. Tucker I! 1 Weather
generally rough. Verily, he who took passage by the Ade-
laide deserved the Victoria Cross; and yet they stood it till
last week, when I see, by Cooribr, the A.U.S.N. Co.
etarted the trade again.

An exhibition in Acetylene Gas lighting
took place on Saturday last, at the warehouse of
Brandt Bros., Lamp Manufacturers, 236 Pitt-
street, Sydney, when some powerful Buggy,
Coach, and Table Lamps were shown. The firm
point out that they can alter any ordinary buggy
lamps so that either candle or acetylene gas can
be burned. A large display of their Hand Ice-
Making Machines, Ice Cream Freezers, and But-
ter Coolers were also shown ; also the Jupiter and
Primus Kerosene Gas Cooking Stoves.*

Interesting to shearers. According to
sttriciTY electrical shears are going to wipe
out the old article entirely. Electricity is em-
p'oyed to make the “cutter” red hot; you push
the cautery along the surface of the skin and the
red-hot knife rapidly mows down the wool, lho
operation is so quick that the heat injures neither
wool nor skin. A boy can, with practice, easily
do a dozen skins per hour. Look out, ye ringers !
Dear B ulletim . — Be various
recent snake-inquiries (1.) I lie small-
est Australian snake I know of is
Pseuddaps Kretfiti, common in the
Tweed district and on the south coast
of Queensland. Adult specimens are
often only 10 or 12in. long. This
snake is venomous; not so largest
Australian snake, Blasts childveyil, of
K.Q. and W.A., which sometimes
exceeds 22ft. in length. (2.) The
green tree snake ( sp.) is
non-venomous, but it isn’t a “ whip ”
snake. The whip snake (Diemenia
reticulata) is a highly venomous dry-
country snake, which doesn t climb
trees. (3.) A snake certainly con
throw itself upwards or backwards to
at least the length of its body. I
once jammed a retreating snake’s tail
to the ground with a paling shorter
than the snake, and in less than a
second it had bitten the hat off my
head. (4.) The “seven-inch death
adder” mentioned, could not have
been an adult, and there is only one
species of death adder in N.S.W. (5.)
Scale-shedding proves nothing, as
snakes, young and old, are continu-
ally shedding their skins when in
captivity.— H. J. Mac.

“Yamba ” on the population question :
About The Bulletin’s recent query as to why the births
in this young and vigorous country are at a standstill. I
live in a prosperous country district in Victoria, and I
have come to the paradoxical conclusion that it is this
prosperity which is responsible for the trouble. The
young men are strong, comparatively wealthy, and above
the average in intelligence. But their homes are so com-
fortable, and their recreations so varied and absorbing,
that there is nothing but the sex-instinct to impel them
to matrimony; and, curiously enough, this seems to be
quite dormant amongst them; a fact probably due to
thiir healthy lives of physical exercise. Illegitimate
births—generally in inverse ratio to the marriage rate —
are in this district practically unknown. (Only one in 14
years, and, by the irony of fate, the father was a married
man witli already a full quiver.) There is very little love-
making, on either side ; and although the young men
and women enjoy each other’s company, the feeling ap-
pears to be one of ordinary comradeship only. At any
rate, nothing further comes of it. The girls, who are
most ly Scotch, come up well to Paraguay Lane’s standard
as to breadth of hip and depth of bust, and are tolerably
good-looking as well ; yet the only people in our district
that get married are the milk-boys—almost all Irish —who
are employed by the marriage-avoiding Scots. McDonagh,
in his recent hook on Irish humor, says that the
Hibernian marries poor and young, because be character-
istically reasons that he can’t be any poorer ; whereas the
canny Scot declines to exchange a present and positive
amount of comfort for a problematical increase thereof by
marriage. Prudent personality, no doubt, is bad for the
community as Scottish old maids are becoming alarm-
ingly plentiful, while Irish old maids are as hard to find
as the bunyip.

—Verus.
There has been serious trouble at Ballan
(Victoria) over the Marquis of Bute. Not the
gentleman cow of that name, but the noble sprig
of British aristocracy. Plaintiff is a teacher of
literature and languages, and also a professor of
music. He was employed by a gentleman who
combines a bakery business with a phosphate
agency (great Scott!) to teach his son and
daughter general culture. While he was wait-
ing for his pupils to gird up their brains and com-
mence their education he had a little discussion
with the man of bread, who said, apropos of some-
thing or other, that the Marquis of Bute was a
blackguard and a low skunk. The man of culture
was inexpressibly pained at these coarse re-
marks, and he reproved the bread man, and told
him that he must not talk like that about the
Marquis of Bute. The Marquis was a personal
friend of the cultured one’s family. Had patted
his head when he was a boy, or reduced the
rent, or something. The man of crust said the
Marquis was something unprintable and the
teacher a toady. Then, the teacher said, the
baker and agent struck him. The bread dealer
said the teacher tried to pitch him into the
fire, or to decapitate him between the chair
and the table. Also, the man with the long
hair had a very dirty left. The baker said
he was quite dazed when the knife-box new up
and hit them both, and cut them, and generally
behaved in an uncivilised way. Just about here
the baker’s finger unfortunately got into the
teacher’s eye. It was force of habit on the part
of his finger—he was not responsible for it—it
always did that whenever he had any little “argu-
ments.” The literary man said the yarn about
the knife-box and the accident was quite unre-
liable. And about here the magistrate felt tired,
and he rose up and smote the baker with many
expenses, damages, and fines, and generally nn-
pressed him that assaulting friends of the Marquis
of Bute must be put down with a strong hand.

EVIDENCE THAT CONVINCES.
Clements Tonic is a Scientific Specific.
-A. TBTTTHFUL IsTABRATIVB.
Hope and Succour for the Afflicted.
The Case ef
MRS. MARIA HAPSOOD.
Reported Jor the “ MarrickviUe Express.”)
The reporter had no difficulty in finding
the residence of Mrs. Maria Hapgood at
No. 60 Shepherd-street, -Vlarrickville, as it
is not far from the Enmore tram terminus.
This lady, a well-known resident, was
busily attending her well-kept flower
garden, in which she takes a great pride.
In answer to an enquiry as to whether she
had not greatly benefited by the use of
Clements Tonic, and would she object to
giving her experience, Mrs. Hapgood
replied :
“Certainly not; I owe so much to that
remedy for all the good it has done to me
and mine that the least I can do is to give
my testimony, and I recommend it ia every
case that I think suitable.”
“ Ho .v did your own sickness arise ?”
“ I do nob know the cause, but three
years ago I began to experience a number
of distressing symptoms. My digest ion was
seriously impaired, so that my food caused
ine pain and distress At night I could not
rest, my sleep was broken and irregular.
Then I had a terrible pain in the back
across the shoulders, and I was nearly
crippled by neuralgic pains in my hands
and wrists, so that it was with the greatest
difficulty I could struggle through my
housework. But there was worse to come.
There was a painful enlargement in my
throat, and the back of my tongue became
so swollen as nearly to fill my mouth, and
joined to these troubles I had great depres-
sion of spirits. I did not seem to care how
things went. I went to a friend, a chemist,
and lie recommended me a much puffed and
advertised remedy, also a mixture of his
own compounding ; but though I took them
both as directed neither did me any good.
My husband insisted on my going to our
family doctor, of whom he had a very high
opinion.”
“ What did the doctor say ?”
“ He gave me no hope or encouragement,
and though I told him all my symptoms, he
did not tell me his opinion of my case or
I what was the matter with me, Of course
he gave me a prescription, which I had
I made up and took without any benefit)
whatever. Then it chanced that I came
across one of the pamphlets of Clements
Tonic, and although I could see no case
there just like my own, it appeared to me
Clements Tonic would be likely to do me
good. When I called on a ohemist to
purchase a bottle of Clements Tonic he
strongly advised me not to do so, and urged
me to take what he called ‘my own tonic. ’
I knew better, he only wanted the extra
frofit; I had been had that way before, so
was resolute in my determination and
insisted on having the genuine article—
Clements Tonic.”
“ And what happened ?” *
, “To my great surprise, I derived great
benefit cot ouct j and I may say that, practi-
cally, that one bottle cured me. All my
distressing symptoms disappeared as though
by magic, the pains in the back, across the
shoulders, the indigestion, the want of
appetite, the neuralgia in the hands and
wrists, even the painful swelling in the
throat and mouth were subdued, and I
knew at once I had found a master for my
trouble. I had been worn, weak, and
miserable ; but now my good spirits and
energy returned; it was the most sudden
transformation I ever heard of. I said one
bottle cured me, but let me add I have
taken more than that. This sickness I tell
you of was three years ago, and I find by
keeping a bottle of Clements Tonic in the
house and taking a dose at the least sign of
my ailment returning it is effectually warded
oft and my naturally good health preserved.
I noticed when I was sick that my distress-
symptoms were largely aggravated by
the hot weather, and it is now during hot
spells that I keep on my guard and protect
my health as I have told you, and you may
judge how my health is restored. You
have seen my brother and heard his case, so
you can understand the faith I have in
Clements Tonic, and why I have persistently
recommended it to my friends.” I
r ‘ And you are willing that this testimony
should be made public * ’
“If it is of value do what you think best,
you have my full permission ; I am only too
pleased to assist any sufferer on the road to
recovery and point out the genuine remedy
against the h ">st of quack medicines now so
freely advertised. ”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Maria Hapgood, of 60 Shspherd-street, Marrick.
vnle, in the Colony of hew South Wale*, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully
read the annexed document, consisting of three folios
and consecutively numbered from one to three, and
that it contains and is a true and faithful account of
my illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also
contains my full permission to publish the
same in any way ; and I make this solemu
declaration voluntarily, and without receiving
any payment, oorscientiously believing the tame
to be true, and by virtue of the provisions erf an
Act made and passed in the ninth year of the reign o!
her present Majesty, intituled “An Act for the mor-
effectual abolition of Oaths and Affirmations taken and
m&de in the various Departments of the Government
of New Mouth Wales, and to substitute Declarations
in lieu thereof, far the suppression of voluntary
and extra-judicial Oaths ami Affidavits."
Declared at Marriokville this 16th dav of Maj

Songs in Public Schools.
Statements appearing in the press that the songs
jn our public schools are set in too high a key
brings to mind a report received from Mr. Geo.
M. Massing, a State school teacher, Hadden Bush.
Southland, N.Z., which is actually a song of
praise, and one that is universally used in all the
Australian colonies. He writes : “I am pleased
to testify to the great benefit I have derived from
the use of Warner’s Safe Cure, when a few years
ago I was suffering from kidney disorder. From
H. Macalister, Chemist, Invercargill I
obtained my last case of Warner’s Safe Cure, and
it completely restored me and put me into grand
form. . Not only in public schools, but in private
life this song of praise is sung in every key, and
can be heard on every hand all over this fair land.*

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
mg extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *

N.S. W. Assembly passed a resolution over
three years ago in favor of uniform penny letter
postage throughout the province, but nothing came
of it. The necessity for carrying newspapers free,
-to placate the rich newspaper proprietors of Syd-
ney, keeps the N.S. W. postal department so hard-
op that it can’t afford penny letter postage. When
the subject cropped up again the other day, P. M.
G. Crick gave the usual answer ; it would mean a
loss of £60.000 a-year, and the sacrifice* called
for serious consideration, See., &o. Yet the sacri-
fice of <£40,000 a year in carrying newspapers gratis
nomehow calls for no consideration at all.

Dear Bulletin,— R e the alleged cruelty
practised upon Dreyfus at Devil’s Island, cannot

received in Portland prison ? Davitt says he had
during the term of his incarceration, to “lap his

over the cruelty °f Boers to Kaffirs ; bu/when

The N.S.W. Govt Advisory Board on
the unemployed, proposes, in connection with
assisted settlement, that improvements on land
taken up shall be paid for at their value to the
assisted selector. Thus, if a selector takes up
slake 2fi C onn b OCk ? tank ° n . ifc sufficie "t to
slake 20,000 acres, he only pays as if the tank were
big enough for the smaller area. This also is the
present law with regard to selections generally
It is fair enough, no doubt, to the selector—for
there is no reason why he should be made to pay
for a big tank when he only wants a small one';
but it is pretty hard on the man who made and
owns the tank. It is this, in fact, more than any-
thing else, that has put the brake hard down on
the improvement of country out back. And it is
this non-improvement of naturally-deteriorated
country which makes stock losses in such bad
seasons as the last three or four so disastrous.
“ Constance Clyde ” writes, and The Bul-
letin stands by and supplies the Amen:—
The arguments against the Old Age' Pensions idea still
occupy columns in the dady papers, and certain gentle-
men are still insisting on individualism, and “self-
reliance, and the wickedness of being too charitable
not realising that individualism pure and simple got its
death-blow some centuries back, when the man-ape dis-
continued shaking his aged relative off a tree because
there was a scarcity of cocoanuts in the primeval forest
e i_? esuelldan k s kh ese apes at present seem uuite
unable to understand that it is not a question of feeding
the aged ones or letting them starve, because owino- to
the beastly humam-tananism of this degenerate age we
actually give them skilly, and a blanket, and the pleasing
society of other aged males and females in large cold
stone residences put aside for their benefit. The issue
does not lie between providing for the old folks and
heaving them out into the gutter, but between providing
for them in the way that hurts their feelings and providing
for them in the other way that doesn’t. Bruce Smith in
a long effusion to a morning paper, quotes Herbert
Spencer and his creed of individualism. Spencer bv the
way, is the philosopher who objected to the State edu-
cating children because this would not be teaching the
P a f' arit |, se J f '. re ] lance - . But this argument cuts both ways
If the State is to remain passive then it has no business to
teach relf-rehance, and something must be done to it
when it is found behaving in this officious manner.
Dicksons ejection from Q. Premiership
may occur any moment. If E. B. Forrest and the
Labor-party have hit it off, it may have occurred
ere this sees print. Labor wants two or three
portfolios; Forrest will be Premier; and Groom
is actually to be a Minister at last ! What with
disaffected Dicksonites, Opposition, and Labor
the majority is waiting; the country is sick of
Dickson and Co. ; and the only question is a
question of terms. The Bulletin advises the
Labor-party to get one democratic foot in and let
the whole man in by degrees. Q. Labor-men have
been singing out “all or nothing!” quite long
enough. Take the half-loaf and grab more when
the time comes.

Many W.A. grants
and land titles contain
a valuable proviso that
the State may, at any
time, resume without
compensation, land need-
ed for roads, canals,
bridges, and other works
of public utility and
convenience. This has
saved the State a large
expenditure for compen-
sation when public works
were under construction.
But lately the Govern-
ment resumed land with-
out compensation for a
recreation - reserve, and
there was trouble. The
C.J. held that this pur-
pose came within the
terms of the grant, but
the Full Court upset the
decision, and ordered
Govt, to pay compensa-
tion. If this decision holds before the
Ir’rivy Council, other “ resumed ” peo-
ple will probably awarm like locusts
on top of Bigjohn.

Fact. (N.B. Over 100,000 adult males in Vic-
House ) 6 n6Ver had a V ° te f ° r th ® U PP er
le .^y ne (N.S.W.) Government’s Early-
closing Bill has the usual disappointing blot. It
is to be applicable to Sydney and Newcastle only
but it may be extended gradually to other placos
if a certain number of people apply for its exten-
sion. Ihe Lyne Government recognises that
early-closing is a good thing—yet it is afraid to
apply its good thing all round. Various NSW
ministries also recognised that Local Government
was a good thing, but they were afraid to apply
it all round—or even a tenth of the way round.
Similarly the Shops and Factories Act was applied
to Sydney and Newcastle, and left at that.
The Lyne (N.S.AV.) Government starts its
career by spending =64500 of loan money on forest-
thinning, so apparently it has just about as bad a
financial conscience as its predecessor. It is
alleged, in excuse, that the work will be repro-
ductive. But as nothing is said about applying
the product, if any, towards paying off the debt
incurred, this doesn’t seem to make things much
better. If the country borrows a million for a
reproductive work which pays interest on the
borrowed money and something over, but the
something over isn’t applied to pay off the debt •
it is just plain swindle, and nothing but swindle. ’
The Venezuelan boundary question, re-
cently settled much in favor of Britain, forms an
interesting parallel case to the row over the Rand.
Nobody apparently troubled about the Schom.
burgk Line till rich gold was discovered beyond.
Ihe small republic would have been bullied out of
her territory long ago, had not the U.S.A. taken
up the cudgels on her behalf. Strange, very
strange, how the British national honor and
pocket always have coincident interests. The
apparently abandoned Venezuela in pav-
!n h e f. for Britain s moral support against Spain.
Ihe boundary was fixed by a British and U S
gave Britain not only all it
asked for, but a little more.
Edward Mulcahy, Tasmania’s new Lands
Minister, promises as an important feature of the.
programme of the Lewis-Bird Government, that
it will spend no more loan-money on repairs and
renewals. And it is notable that every member
of the new Ministry voted against this rotten and
thriftless system of finance when the subject
came up last session. If the Lewis-Bird Ministry
will go a step further, and abolish all loan-money
expenditure on unproductive works, it will be not.
only Tasmania’s best Ministry, but the best Min-
istry in all Australasia.
A MORE OR LESS “SPLENDID
OFFER.’
BUSINESS MANAGER (of co. of street-singers): "I
say, mister, if yer'll come and sing ‘ Soldiers
of the Queen’ for us we’ll 'elp yer with a
chorus, and give yer ’arf the collection
Norman Cameron, the Nemesis of Tas.
ex-Minister Miles and the Braddon Ministry
made a brave showing against the new Lands
Minister, Mulcahy, at Hobart, polling within 240
of the Minister in a 3000-vote constituency.
Cameron was handicapped by the fact that he is
a JNorthern man, and therefore a traditional
enemy to Hobart interests ; also he is a novice at
politics, and was out-manoeuvred by Ministerial
electioneers. And Hobart, which is more Irish
than Scotch, dearly loves to be represented by a
Minister of its very own town pump variety: and
further, it isn’t yet quite sure that the man with
a mania for exposing the crookedness of Ministers
and others, would be quite a desirable person to
have about the premises. Cameron stays out of
las. politics awhile, but will contest a federal
seat.
The Seddon Ministry is obviously hard up
for a new and thrilling war-cry for use at next
election. It has done so many reforms that it has
left itself rather short of possible promises. And
yet the loudest and most thrilling war-cry that
Maoriiand has ever heard is available if a great
financier would only take it up. The man who
went to the country with the promise that he
would complete the North Island Trunk line, the
Midland railway and the Otago Central inside
tour years without borrowing a single penny of
the money at interest could make the whole
province s hair stand up in gratitude and astonish-
ment and admiration. And it could be done by
making bank-notes a State monopoly, by requiring
the banks to keep, say, half their local reserves in
btate notes, by running a State lottery in place of
the private Tattersall, and by a rigid refusal to
let go one penny of current taxation, with, possibly,
a temporary special effort in the way of a few
extra imposts.

Taking the full list of batting and bowling
averages lor the last English cricket season (down to the
end of August), it is hard to figure out a reason why the
Australians didn’t have the worst, instead of somewhat
the best, of those five test-matches. The averages repre-
sent the form of the rival champions under practically
equal conditions. All Kurland men had to bat and bowl
at one tune and another against counties, picked teams,
and the Australians, whilst the visitors were uieelin***
counties, picked teams, and All England. Compare the
nine best Australian averages for all matches with the
same men’s performances in test-matches, leaving deci-
mals out of the calculation

Boxing, which for eight years had been as dead
at Broken ILII as last year’s mushrooms, has sprung- into
vigorous life again—chiefly as the result of the importa-
tion of mine-hands from different provinces, and the
return of others from the West. It was Broken Hill ’alls
in their palmiest days that gave Joe Goddard his first
championship ; after he went everything seemed mild

The magic number. Seven bulls-eyes in seven
shots, at 700 yards, was the remarkable score put up the
other day, at Mudgee, by Private Walsh, N.s.W. volun-
teer for the Transvaal.

Religion has many eyes for the “gambling evil.”
In Roman Catholic France the bookmaker and the tote
h t side b.v side at Sunday race-meetings. 111 England
the home of bookmaking, the law snorts at the idea of
legalising any form of gamble, and sniv.Jsome Noncon-
formists would even “ put down ” racecourses if they
could. In the Transvaal, where the Government to
bed with a Bible under its pillow, the Luth ran conscience
finds 110 fault with the tote. Let Aust aiian punters bear
in mind the fact that Paul Kruter allowed the Uitlanders
to tote as much as they pleased.

“Gipsy Jones,” the noted purse-fakir, obituar
ised in nearly every N.S.W. newspaper, is still alive at
Forbes and ready to let any churchwarden or Sunday-
school superintendent have three ha’pennies when
the pious plungers hand out a pound sterling for
three golden sovereigns covered up in a purse.
Whenever an immaculate investor struck copper
after bargaining for gold, he “performed” like a man
wholly unacquainted with the ten commandments, and
generally called in the police. “Gipsy "would then ex-
plain that he sold the purse with its contents, and if the
purchaser thought he was stealing a march on Jones he
was mistaken. The police only smiled and recommended
the purchaser to open the purse in future before invest
ing. And the crowd laughed. If a prosecution followed,
Jones always produced a hawker’s licence and the case
was dismissed.

Result-slip of “ Tatt’s.” No. 1 Melb. Cup sweep
shows curious spokes in the wheel of blind chance. Many
investors, singly or combined, take fancied numbers Oi
buy bulk lots. An order reaching Tatt. for 3014 ticket-,
when 4729 had just gone would strike a snag : not a horse
in the whole boiling—their only solatium being £195 in
various cash prizes. As a counterblast a luekv gambler
taking three consecutive tickets from 11,912 would get.
Uvo horses, Strath joy and Snapshot; 14,000 and 15,000 no
horses, and only i 65 in small cash prizes. Any takfer of
the last thousand would get £l7O in cash prizes and five
horses, including three probable starters, for his £250.

“ Wa J ”: Bulletin, *2B/10/’99—“A fighter is the
poorest judge of a fighter’s capabilities.” That’s rmht
Peter Jackson had a wonderful opinion of Tom Kelly auc
Paddy Ketle.v, neither of whom could put a dent in a
pound of butter. Jackson also brought out Steve O’Don
''ell, and thought he could bfat George Seale. Seale met
O Donnell twice, and on each occasion simplv smothered
the big fellow. Another bit of bad judgment on Jack-
son -s part was the matching of Paddy Moran against
Jack Hall. Moran, at the time, was a clever youngster
without ring experience, and Hull was considered by
many the smartest light-weight Svdnev had seen Hall
gave Moran a terrible drubbing, and it was touch and-go
that an inquest was not necessary. Moran was the
makings of a first-class boxer, but Jackson’s bad judg-
ment tn putting him against Jack Hall at the outset of
his career cowed him, and the ring knew him no more

Griffith, M.L.A., on the Boer question :
“ The conduct of the (Jutlanders’ Committee from
start to finish proves that they don’t want the

Premier Kingston has been dispossessed
of the family mansion in which he was born and
has lived all his life—an old-fashioned place built
by his father, George K., when the latter was
a struggling architect in Adelaide. The son has
now to leave it owing to family litigation and his
own impecuniosity, born of the pursuit of politics
and consequent neglect of his once splendid legal
practice. There is no private subscription to help
Kingston out of his troubles. His political ft iends
—the workers—are as poor as he is himself, and the
opulent class is against him to a man.
James Tobias Ryan—“ Toby” Ryan
who died in Woollooinooloo, Oct. 17, aged 82,
ambitioned authorship in the far past, and had
his hand on a new venture when his career closed.
Re was member of N.S.W. Parliament nearly 40
years ago. The chief value of his “ Reminis-
cences of Australia” lies in the character and
local sketches. Being almost a Sydney native
he was born near Penrith- —lie knew nearly every-
body and chats in a free and easy way about per-
sons and tilings now prominent at head-quarters.
In Parliament he showed some knowledge of
what should be a suitable Land Law. He also
took interest in stock-raising, and though never a
political power earned a reputation for shrewd
sense. In his later life he lost most of the money
gains of earlier days, but cultivated a cheerful
philosophy, and took the end without murmur.
The wail of the Jingo in his agony. It
was at Toowoomba (Q.), and Lieut.-Col. Ricardo
had requested Transvaal volunteers to stand
forth. The “ death-devoted ” were then addressed
by a local major, who leaned upon his sword and
lifted up a voice choked with emotion :
This is the bitterest moment in my life, because I know
now lam not going to South Africa. I lived in hopes I
might at least have an opportunity of proving before I
die l hat I am a soldier, but now I know it is not so. . . .
I know Queenslanders will give a good account of them-
selves, and we are proud to be the first to give support
to the mother country in her trouble. I understand
those who have left the Mounted Infantry will have a
chance of going if they desire it. I envy them, their
ehance. I again say this is the bitterest moment in my
life; I want to fight for my country before I die.
The whole corps was moved to blow its collective
nose, and things looked very damp till the gallant
Colonel, choking down his sobs, seized the wail-
ing Jingo by his sunburnt paw and tried to com-
fort him w th the reflection that there might be a
fight in Afghanistan or Russia and the gory
Kangaroo might bo invited to rip a diaphragm,
and lie urged his dear friend not to despair. But
the strong Jingo in his agony refused to be com-
forted. And yet all his woe didn’t make him pay
his own passage by mail steamer, and be an
honorary volunteer in some of the local corps.
Even in the bitterest moment of his life he
wouldn't shift without having his expenses paid.
The Criminal Evidence Act just passed in
Westralia (though it hasn’t-yet come into force)
allows a prisoner for the first time to give evidence
on his own behalf. Hitherto Westralia has stuck
manfully to the good old idea that when there are
two persons involved—the accuser and the accused
—the accuser should be allowed to tell his side of
the case at full length, and the accused shouldn’t
be allowed to swear to his version of the
affair. It will be interesting to see how the new
idea is worked in Westralia. In Victoria it works
very simply and very beautifully. If an tinin-
fluential prisoner charged with a serious crime
gives evidence on his own behalf and is convicted,
he is prosecuted for perjury. If fm gives evidence
and is acquitted he is prosecuted for perjury. If
he doesn’t give evidence at all, Chief Justice
Madden draws the attention of the jury to the
fact that he has made no serious effort to deny
the charge brought against him, and hints that
he thereby practically admits his guilt.
Captain Miles, Tasmania’s fallen Lands
Minister, who resigned his seat in order to appeal
to liis constituents for a clear certificate of charac-
ter, didn’t appeal after all. Two small nonentities
ran for the seat, but the bluff sea-captain who was
going to clear his name till it shone like a bright
new billy didn’t turn up.
Janies Johnson, lighthouse - keeper at
Nobby’s, Newcastle (N.S.W.), sole survivor of
the wrecked Dunbar, has got six months' leave
with a view to retirement from the lighthouse ser-
vice at the end of that time. He has been 36
years at Nobby’s, and altogether 42 years in Govt,
employment—during which time he has not had
three weeks’ holiday in all.
A Perth shopkeeper has found himself
between the devil (if municipal law and the deep
sea of Parliamentary law. The former says that
he shall not sweep his shop-front after 8 ; the
latter that he shall not employ servants in his
shop before 8. “ Then must I sweep out my shop
myself?” he pleaded. “But the magistrate’s
only answer was—“ Fined one shilling.”
A man at Gundagai wanted badly to go on
the land. The inspector reported against his
application. He appealed to the local Land
Board, which, after a lengthy hearing, granted the
application. Thereupon the man fell on his knees
in court and in loud and emphatic phrases fer-
vently blessed the members of the Board.

Queensland's new Criminal Code Bill, as
originally drafted, made stealing a will punishable
by 1 m prison men t for life with hard labor and with
or without solitary confinement at the Judge s
option. On the motion of a Labor member the
solitary was left out. Concealing a will was also
punishable by life imprisonment, hard labor (ituL
solitary. Demanding property by threats, ditto.
Obtaining execution of a deed by threats, more
ditto. In fact as originally drafted the law was
so comprehensive that if the harmless necessary
tailor demanded property (t.c., the .£4 4s. for a
tweed suit that had been owing for six months) by
the threat that failing payment he would apply to
the debtor’s father, it is difficult to say what
dreadful thing might not happen to him.
N.S.W. Attorney-General Wise, at Asli-
field, said that “ whatever this Govt. (Lyne’s)did,
it would never accept power without responsi-
bility. Yet hardly a man amongst the Govt, has
ventured a straight-out opinion upon the Trans-
vaal expedition. At most they have carefully
explained that they only kept the chariot
rolling which had been started along by their pre-
decessor Reid. When the re-action comes along—
which will probably be when the bill comes in
(the real bill), with the butcher’s-bill tacked on to
it, not the temporary advance of =£3o,ooo—the
Lyne Government will lie able to wholly saddle
the matter on Parliament. This is what Wise on
another occasion, and alluding to his opponents,
called “ a complete abnegation of responsibility.”
The Q. Labor-party is reputed at present
to be greatly divided by personal animosities,
resulting partly from the recent Federation cam-
paign, partly from motives of private jealousy.
There are certain members who notoriously come
late, absent themselves from critical divisions, and
wag a free leg generally. Others again provoke
comment by attending gubernatorial banquets got
up in style hitherto considered sacred and peculiar
to the accursed Fat Man. Noticeable also, that
the individuals who thus distinguish themselves
were the most rabid of rabid extremists a few
years ago.

A distressing incident occurred in Melb.
District Court, the other day, when a perspiring
legal person rushed breathlessly into the presence
of the Judge, hitching up his outer garments, and
took up the defence of the accused after the
manner of a man who has missed two trains and
fallen over three dogs in his efforts to make up for
lost time. The case was completed, and defend-
ant let off lightly in consideration of the able de-
fence, and then another person charged with a
similar offence was called upon to face the music.
At this stage the hurried lawyer m m uttered a
wild cry, and kicked himself several tunes m
rapid succession. “ Good heavens,! he said, 1
have defended the wrong man.” Then he caught
up his skirts and went wildly off in pursuit of the
former defendant to gather a fee under a threat ot
proceedings.
A Melb. artist who was in Johannesburg
16 months ago to a Bulletin scribe
“There was no discontent amongst the foreign working
population of the Rand until it was cunningly worked up
bv the capitalists. The Uitlander mechanic is too well
off to desire any change if left to progress entirely on his
own He gets £6 a week, and can save £4. " here can
he do that under the British flag, and enjoy the ad van-
ta*»'es, climatic and social, that are available in the
Transvaal? The mine-owners are at the bottom of the
agitation; the miners did not care a straw about their
political disabilities, and found the laws not a scrap more
irksome than in ‘ Dear Old’ or any of her ‘colonies.
There was not a murmur till the lo<al Cockney Fat men
shut down the mines, and gave the miners and mechanics
distinctly to understand that the closure would continue
until a big demonstration was worked up and a petition
sent to London. Then the men demonstrated wildly, and
petitioned with all their might, but the most intelligent
recognised sadly that ‘ British interference would surely
lead to assisted immigration and a rattling downi of wages.
Nigger labor is plentiful and dirt-cheap, and the mine-
owner wants competition that \vill make the white
worker as biddable and as cheap.”
“Gunner”: Be the battle of Glencoe.
The numbers on each side were practically equal,
but in every other respect the odds were
enormously in favor of the British who brought
into action 18 cannon to the Boers’ 4 or 5 and the
Boer shells did not burst because their gunners
either had no fuses, or did not know how to use
them The whole thing merely sums-up that a
mob of 4000 farmers attacked the same number of
regular soldiers—with the inevitable result. Poor
old Joubert, playing at high strategy, ordered a
combined attack on Ladysmith an 1 Glencoe,
which did not come off. What happened at
Glencoe was simply this : The Boers were drawn
up along the crest of a hill, the British on the flat.
Both sides blazed away with their cannon till
those of the Boers were knocked out. Then the
British worked their way up one end of the hill
and rushed the Boers with the bayonet. This the
Boers having no bayonets, couldn’t face, and they
“got” Meantime, the British cavalry, having
got round the back of the hill, drove off the Boers
horses, and were thus enabled severely to cut up
the retreating enemy ; which sufficiently accounts
for the Boer loss being so great.

“ On the 13th, a train was made up in the
usual way, with two trucks'and an engine, and
was despatched some distance out of the town.
The trucks were loaded with dynamite, of which
there is a very large quantity in store at Mafeking.
The expedition was entrusted to the charge of one
man, a courageous engine-driver named Byferry.
As was expected, the train at once attracted the
attention of the Boers, who opened a hot fire upon
it. An appalling explosion followed, spreading
death and dismay among the enemy for a great
distance around. No fewer than fifty Boers are
reported to have been blown to a tom s. Of Uyftrvii t
fate nothin // is known.” —Budded cable.
Dear Bulletin. — lie your quotation from
Canon Tucker at the head of “ The Jingo Par-
son’s Creed ” last week : Either the reporter was
an ass or the parson an unskilful borrower. Rus-
kin wrote of young men in the “Crown of Wild
Olives ” (War, pages 166-7) that he would “ much
rather that they should ride WAR-horses than back
RACE-horses ; and —I say it sternly and deliber-
ately—much rather would I have them slay their
neighbors than cheat them.” —W.
We sing of Byferry, in charge of a train
With dynamite carefully loaded ;
He drove it along on a Boer-studded plain
Till the joke had been fully exploded.
Then the enemy fled, frightened out of their wits
(This yarn sounds remarkable very)
Save some fifty, or so, who were blown into bits,
And we don't know the fate of Byferry.
Perhaps he sped on with his engine unharmed,
And is now lying dark in a tunnel ;
Or perhaps he came back (whilst the Boers were alarmed)
Singing “ God Save the Queen ” on the funnel.
But, whatever his fate, he’s a brilliant success—
O, our true British heart maketh merry
As we read in the daily reliable press
Of the pluck of good driver Byferry.
To shake up one’s liver, there’s nothing like war
The reports are so loud and exciting
As long as Great Britain is teaching the Boer
The beauty of up-to-date fighting.
Let us hope that the victims of lyddit-ful shell
Will be more than their comrades can bury.
Let us hope all the Boers will be shattered to h—l,
Whilst the Lord’s taking care of Byferry.
Come gather, come gather in front of the bar
And rejoice in our victories latest;
For we are qnite llinglish, at present, we are—
Of all the Earth’s people the greatest.
We’ll drink to our prowess in brandy and rum,
And whisky with soda, and sherry ;
We will drink to the cable, although it be dumb
Concerning the end of Byferry.

Mr. W. E. Smith, the well-known Sydney
printer and stationer, who was on his way to
England when the disastrous fire occurred at his
fine establishment, lias now returned to Sydney.
_ If in A. Hall and Co.’s establishment
(SGI-7 George-street, Sydney) you won’t find
‘‘everything, from a needle to an anchor,” it is
simply because neither of those interesting articles
is usually considered an indispensable article of
household furniture. Did a rhinoceros and a
cataract come into that category, however, the
chances are that they would be duly on view in
these spacious show-halls, filled with a diversity
of furniture-goods, in which every taste—and,
what is still more important, every purse—has
been considered. The difficulty with the freshly-
manufactured “ house-father ” would here be, not
so much what to buy, as what not to buy ; and
indeed, without any purchasing intentions at all,
such an establishment as Hall’s will repay a stroll
through its numerous and varied departments.*
Rev. G. Buchanan, of St. Andrew's,
Goulburn (N\S.\V.), preached a strong pro-Boer
sermon, t’other day. “The war was comparable
to Britain mobilising an army-corps, and getting
contingents from India and all the colonies to
give Tasmania a thrashing for the Braddon Blot.”
Though the rev. gentleman openly expressed a
hope that the Boers would wipe the veldt with
the British, his congregation merely gathered
itself into knots to discuss his sentiments in an
amicable manner. Which shows that it is safer
for a parson to tramp'e on his flock’s patriotic
sentiments than to criticise its motives in join-
ing the giddy dance.

NOBODY WILLSTEAL THEM
There are two reasons why the Crown jewels art
never stolen from the Tower of London. Thej
are in a strong place, well guarded, and are dan
gerous things to handle when dishonestly come by
Most losses of valuables are due either to care
lessness on the part of the owners or custodians,
or to bribery. Perhaps the burglar’s vocation is
under no circumstances an absolutely safe one.
but not infrequently he chances upon a “job’
which is both easy and lucrative. Ido not men
tion this fact for the unworthy purpose of induc-
ing any of the youth of the land to enter the
profession but solely to throw illustrative light
upon quite another theme. Still, there is a rela-
tion between them, and we shall hit it after we
have talked a bit about the case of Mrs. Mary
Rowlands, who lives in Wales.
The lady says that no longer ago than February
(1898) she had a severe attack of influenza, fol-
lowed by bronchitis. At this point we are con-
cerned to know whether the influenza was in any
sense responsible for the bronchitis, as cause and
effect. If so, why ? It is well understood, of
course, in England, where influenza is so com-
mon, that other ailments do follow it; yet pro-
bably, the majority of us have not settled in our
minds the reason for it.
At all events, Mrs. Rowlands had a low, bad
time. The trouble lingered along as, sometimes,
winter does, away down into the period proper for
May blossoms. She could not seem to get the
better of the throat ailment nor master the
lethargy and weakness left her as a legacy by the
influenza. Her condition may be likened unto
i that of a ship which has indeed survived a gale
but finds herself stripped of the sails needed to
enable her to take advantage of gentler winds.
The lady’s hope of getting back her lost
strength by taking plenty of solid, nourishing
food was illusory. Every meal of that kind caused
fearful distress in the stomach and acute pain
at the chest. Nature distinctly repudiated the solid
food, and almost as strenuously objected to the
liquids, such as broths, teas, and milk. It looked
as if Mrs. Rowlands must starve at her own table.
Then came on asthmatic symptoms, so bad she
was often obliged to gasp for breath. Between
this combination of complaints she got but little
rest day or night. Soon after arose a threat of a
still more serious disease—a threat happily not
fulfilled. I refer to the cold, clammy sweats that
broke out upon her, pointing to a fatal decline.
The fear was natural, as grim Consumption with
his poisoned dart, often approaches by that same
road.
“I became so reduced and feeble,” the lady
writes, “ that my daughter had to nurse me con-
stantly. I could not get into or out of bed with-
out her help. In the house, or to others, I was
of no use at all. The doctor who attended me
left nothing untried that he knew of; but, in spite
of all he did, I grew worse and worse.
“Where I should have been to-day, whether in
the land of the living or not, who can tell, if a kind
Providence had not intervened to save me ? In
some way my husband heard of Mother Seigel’s
Syrup, and persuaded me to try it. I began
taking it and soon felt better. My appetite re-
turned, and all kinds of food agreed with me.
With plenty of nourishment I gained strength
rapidly. The asthma and the bronchial trouble
vanished and almost before I could realise it, I
found myself in the best of health, and have con-
tinued so to be ever since. For my remarkable
and unexpected recovery I am under obligation to
Mother Seigel’s Syrup ; and so I tell all whom I
know.”—(Signed) Mary Rowlands, Deri, via
Cardiff, August 31, 1898.
One thing Mrs. Rowlands says in her letter
which I have reserved for this place, namely, that
she was a dyspeptic. Her system was weakened
and her blood rendered impure by indigestion.
That is the main point. That is why she was open
to the attacks of influenza, bronchitis, and asthma.
Her body castle being weak, the precious jewel of
health was stolen.
Keep the digestion strong and the blood clean
and pure, by the frequent use of Mother Seigel’s
Syrup and there will be no soil for the germs of
disease to grow in. The system thus guarded,
protects the health as the walls and door of the
Tower hold safe the Crown jewels.

BIL BEANS CURE BILIOUSNESS
“ They Would Almost Save You From Your Coffin,”
says Mrs. Shergold.
A STRONG CASE FOR BILE BEANS.
The following interview, with a Newcastle Herald reporter shows the value the Aus-
tralian Press places on this remedy : ~ ~
L “ Thev would almost save you from your coffin, said
Mrs. Shergold, of Wilton Street, Merewether, when asked

An Obstacle to Beauty.
PIMPLES, AND HOW THEY MAY BE CURED.
PIMPLBS are an abomination to a pretty girl or handsome young man. To have perfect
features spoiled by little red blotches all over the face is exceedingly annoying, to say the
least yet nearly all young people of the present day are troubled more or less with this com-
plaint which, by the way, cannot be treated properly by the use of cosmetics, as we will ex-
plain Pimples, face sores, etc., are caused by the impure matter contained in the blood
striving to escape from the system. The skin contains thousands of little blood vessels very
minute in size, which are called capillaries; when one or two of these get stopped up by
foreign particles in the blood, a pimple is the result. On the pimple coming to head, this
refuse matter is squeezed out, the capillary heals, and in a day or two all trace is removed.
It will be clearly seen, therefore, that cosmetics cannot remove the cause of pimples, while
they may hide their appearance for a time. The only real remedy for pimples, therefore, is
the one that will prevent impure matter circulating in the blood vessels, and this can only be
done bv acting on the liver and various digestive organs, enabling them to clear all waste
matter out of the body. This latter is the especial object of Bilk Beans, and acting, as they
do direct on the liver, causing it to create the natural purgative for the body ( i.c ., the bile),
they obtain a beneficial result in the safest, surest, and quickest manner.
Influenza and Colds.
Nothing so suddenly obstructs the perspiration as sudden transitions from heat to cold.
Heat rarifies the blood, quickens the circulation and increases the perspiration, but when
these are suddenly checked the consequences must be bad. The most common cause of
disease is obstructed perspiration, or what commonly goes by the name of catching cold.
Coughs colds, sore throat, &c., if attended to in time, are easily subdued, but if allowed to
run their own course generally prove the forerunner of more dangerous diseases. Nine-tenths
of the consumptives date their affliction from a neglected cold, and the diseases that are
caused by wet feet, damp clothes, or exposure are more numerous than are generally sup-
posed. One of the most eflicacious medicines for the prevention of influenza and colds is
Bile Beans are an undoubted specific for biliousness, sick and nervous headache, indiges-
tion constipation, influenza, colds, piles, female weakness, pale-faced girls, bad breath,
pimples, dizziness, all liver and kidney troubles, dyspepsia, pam in the back and side, fullness
after eating Bile Beans are obtainable from storekeepers and chemists generally, price
Is. lid., per Box, post paid. Australian Depot of the Bile Beans Manufacturing Co., 39
Pitt street, Sydney.

“ 0.F.”: At recent Sydney poker party,
“ Straddle” held two cards (ace and king of clubs). Of
three new cards dealt him, one (6 of clubs) was faced.
This “Straddle” of course declined, and was dealt another.
On show of hands “Straddle” had a “royal flush (in
clubs)! On former occasion the same “school’’had a
young player who held ace, king, queen and jack of
clubs’(again) throwing to draw' a club. Result, royal
flush in clubs again. The said player saw a man holding
“ fours ” and reckoned he was beaten.

The modern mania for collecting things has now
extended to historic cricket-bats, and some of those for
which tail prices are asked are bats which have figured
in Anglo-Australian matches. A recent catalogue—
Wisden’s—contains entries and pictures of the bat on
which Johnny Briggs claimed to have made 3500 runs in
first-class cricket. “ I played with it,” says Bnggs in-
scription, “ both with Lord Sheffields arid Stoddarts
teams, and three summers at home. It was the best bat
I ever played with.” Brown’s bat, with which he made
his 140 in Stoddart’s Australian team in 1895, is called
“ Brown’s grave-digger,” and the catalogue says, ‘ Brown
often has a look at it when he is in London.”
The Vic. Church magnates who are preaching
against gambling will find it hard to make converts after
the experience of a youthful newsvendor during the past
few weeks. The lad went to Moonee Valley with haii-a-
crown in his pocket, and managed to back every winner
of the day. He returned regretful only that he had not
“ plaved it up ” and made a few thousands; but, with a
substantial sum banked, he attended Flemington the
following Saturday, and again performed the miraculous
feat of picking the whole card. He now swells it round
in clothes of the newest cut, smokes big cigars, and has
blossomed forth as a full-blown bookmaker, with an eye
on a plaoe in the paddock and a membership in the Vio.
Olub at an early date. Like many others connected with
the press he has deserted his old calling for that of pure
flotion.
* * • •
If a stay-at-home punter decides to have a
sovereign on the horse that will start second favorite for
a certain race, and agrees to accept the market-price of a
certain newspaper, and if the said newspaper reports
4 to 1 each against A and B, and 7 to 1 against 0, which
horse carried the punter’s money? According to the
only rational rule of “system” betting the sovereign in-
vestment was divided between A and B, for nothing can
be more certain than the fact that they were equally
better favorites than 0, who started third favorite.
A follower of the first-favorite system is also bound to
back both A and B, either of them being just as much a
second as a first favorite. To say that, because there was
no distinct-second favorite, the £1 ought to go on 0 is the
stupidest of arguments. How about the followers of the
third-favorite system, if there are any such idiots ?
Their money goes to O without a doubt.
