“Snaffle”: Recent mention of the South
American jockey in England who rides without stirrups
recalls a feat of Victorian jock., Mick Carey. In a steeple-
chase at Launceston, a few' years back, he was riding a
mare called Rebecca, and at the first jump broke a
stirrup-leather. One stirrup was of no use, so he rode
the race without stirrups, and although his mount hit
almost every fence, he landed her a winner. Writer also
remembershow Jack Smith, after falling and re-mounting,
negotiated a couple of fences before getting his feet into
the irons; and knows a Vic. crack steeple jock, who,
when riding over big timber, always removes his feet
from the irons—but that’s funk. •
“Pug.”: Worth a line or two. Mick Dunn,
winner of recent middle-weight boxing championship in
Sydney, wore green trunks; L. Foley was referee.
Chatting after the fight, Foley remarked to a reporter on
an evening paper : “It w’ent dead grain, 3 _ou
know’—being a red-hot Orangeman—to give the verdict
to a man w’earing green trunks.” An adjournment to the
Metrop. followed; fight was talked again, and a crowd
gathered round.- By-and-by, the innocent scribe wanted
to have his say, and chipped in with, “ Well, one thing is
certain—Foley’s the fairest referee living. What other
Orangeman but he would have decided in favor of a man
wearing green trunks?” Then the audience nearly’
choked.

noets. and was soon led through Shakespeare to Tenny-
son, and from Kipling back to Byron. The astounded
paragraphist was eventually “ knocked endways when
he endeavored to counter with Dryden and received the
full force of a slab of Chaucer on the point of Jiis jaw.

Field and trap shooting are very different
games. A young and very promising trap-shot went out
with a party from Cooma for wallaby. Trapman was laid
on his beat and game was plentiful, but when a count
was made the crack shot had not made a kill, w’hilst. the
yokels had all big scores to their credit, although trap-
shot fired double the number of cartridges. Judging
distance was the trouble.
Two Auckland bookmakers, recently charged
with omitting to furnish Income Tax returns, ingeniously
pleaded that as their calling was not recognised by law,
they had been under the impression they were not
liable for Income Tax. The Bench at once disabused
their minds of this comfortable fallacy with a fine ot a
fiver. *

on their feet some good S.A. sporting men, who for a few
years past have had to almost drop out of the list or
owners. One of the best-known of these has just sold
part of his wool-clip at an advance of 80 per cent, on the
price he realised five years ago.

• * * •
Foster Rogers, of surplus-wealth-tax notoriety,
the eccentric ex-M.L.A. for South Yarra in Vic. Assembly,
claims to have played in the very first team of cricketers
that met an English team. In 1852 he was sent from
Australia to the Jesuits College at Stony hurst, Lancashire,
to be educated. In ’6l there were 20 Australian boys in
the College, and 230 English. The Australians challenged
a picked team of English, and were beaten after a close
contest. Rogers says that was the first Ang.o-Australian
match ever played.
* * * *
Brisbane now boasts a Chow spieler. A Sydney
detective once told writer that the only really wealthy
spieler in Australia is a half-caste Chinese who owns
several thousand pounds worth of city property, all of
which he indirectly spieled. He still keeps on spieling
for sheer love of excitement. But after all the spieler is
only the commercial type deprived of the cloak of Respect-
ability.
• * *
The swimming boom has got even to Klondyke.
A couple of Canadians, Gleason and Burwash, have crossed
the Yukon River from side to side—a feat ihought im-
possible, the water being icy and the river very rapid.
They were carried nearly three miles bv the current.
And so they show' a few more fools a hard way to die.
An Indian racing authority (back from a visit
to Australia) remarked of Flemington (Vic.) Grand
National, that horses and men fell at every fence, and
ambulances and doctors were rushing around to pick up
the broken fragments. Tackled on the question of the
morality of the Australian turf, he said it was so hot that
grass would not grow on the tracks. He might have
casually' mentioned that Caulfield Cup is sometimes
spoken of as the Undertakers’ Stakes.

[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
He was of studious habits, a scientist ; views
broad, shoulders narrow; and living the life ot
nther men, but wrapped-up in his study, his
books (hit public-house), his microscope (his
th Yet her heart misgave her as she prepared for
tW moonlight flitting with his friend. He was
Sid hi manner, this lord of hers, but there was a
fire in his eye, a fire rarely seen, but when, seen
not easily to be forgotten, showing in the inner
recesses of his nature a strength of passion that
might yet work its will. , . . f .. .
And he loved her—a woman s instinct could not
, nfoiron Tf he never caressed these small
white hands'of hers, or kissed the light curls that
would stray out on her forehead (having been
carefully trained to stray), it was simply because
he felt the shiver of repugnance with which she
rank from every advance.
She hated to give him pain (she belonged to the
soft-hearted variety of bad woman), but she
could not help the repugnance, though she felt
a dull pity for his grief. And now she was going
to leave him. She was going with her only friend
—and his • the one visitor whose coming had
brightened’the dull house of the studious recluse ;
a man younger than her Professor, less earnest,
vet nevertheless with the stronger brain-power
that often accompanies and is dependent on the
greater strain of animality. With him the
brilliance of the scientist was in exact proportion
tn the ardour of the lover.
So with hisstronghand enfolding her weak one,
she left the dreary home behind her ; afraid, yet
glad to be free. . , ~
g Six months later she experienced the same sen-
sations once more, but this time in relation to her
lover. She had snapped the legal tie ; the illegal
one was still more frail—it snapped of itself. She
was not bad enough for the life-and he was not
g °So Te" stood by the Gate of Bondage once
more; would this other, her husband, take her
back •’ If he loved her still, he would, for he was
not one to be bound by narrow conventions. Put
could he love her still ? .
In the darkness of the summer evening she
stole round to the study window. The gas was
lighted, but the blinds were still up.
Of course he would be working in there, work-
ing as he had often worked in the days that now
seemed so long ago, working and forgetting her.
With a chill fear at her heart she raised herself on
a clod of earth and looked in.
He was not working, he was not reading; lie
was sitting by a dying fire, his greying head upon
his hands. Once he raised his face and she saw in
its worn features a look of hopeless weariness and
grief that wrung her heart—and rejoiced it.
S bbing with mingled happiness and grief, she
stumbled round to the front of the house. Push-.
ing open the doors, she passed through the dis-
ordered hall and stood on the threshold of the
room she had been viewing. .
“James, dearest James, forgive me! 1 have
come back ; I shall never see him again.
His startled eyes met hers, and she saw his gaze
look past her, as if seeking the hated figure of his
betraver.
“James, forgive me!
He rose from his seat, and his black eyes
gleamed with one of his rare fits of passion.
“Forgiveyou?—never! If you had gone with any
other man, perhaps I should have overlooked it.
But you have taken away from me the only man
who could sympathise with my ideas, who could
have worked with me. You have used your posi-
tion in this house to steal that man’s affection
from me. Go !—woman! you have lost me my
friend for ever 1 ”

The “ Penny-in-the-slot Gramophone
lately to hand at the Melbourne agents’, is calcu-
lated to reap a rich harvest of coppers for country
hotel-keepers, some of whom, by the way, charge
a penny a time for the ordinary Gramophone per-
formance. The new machine makes its own
charge. After a “record” has been duly fixed,
the sound thereof is prepared to come through
the funnel for a penny, a pen’orth of internal
wheel-work being sufficient to exhaust the
“ record’s ” resources. Then a bolt shoots out,
and the tricky little Gram, refuses to do anything
more until another copper has been slipped down
the slot.

Morituri te salutant! Turn your thumb, 0
mighty Britain.
For the mob is losing patience, and is howling
for the fray,
And the mercenary grumbles at his leash, with
war-lust smitten ;
Hear the Jewish jackal howling as he scents
the easy prey !
See ! another foolish people who would fain be
left to loiter
’Mong the by-ways of tradition, careless of
revengeful fate,
Standing in the road of commerce, of the syndi-
cate exploiter,
Till the Juggernaut of Progress shall have
crushed them with its weight.
Break them, Britain ! that the jobber may con-
tinue in his gamble ;
That the Stock Exchange may profit, scourge
them with the iron rod !
Head your solemn ultimatum with a sanctified
preamble,
And beneath your declaration forge the signa-
ture of God I
In the name of Light and Freedom—bah ! the
modern convict-collar
That you force upon the vanquished presses
harder than the old.
Oh, the slavery of Mammon ! Oh, the serfdom
of the Dollar !
Oh, the grinding, racking torture of that
shameless Moloch, Gold !
Morituri te salutant l But methinks they see,
O Britain !
In your sweated children’s faces, through the
thunder of your guns,
With the second-sight of death, a gruesome
Mene-Tekel written,
And they fall in the assurance of a reckoning—
for your sons !
At Rockhampton (Q.) Police Court, a ten-
year-old boy was publicly presented by the P.M.
with the Royal Humane Society’s certificate of
merit for saving life, whereupon he handed up the
following written acknowledgment:—
“To Mr. P. VV. Pears, P.M. Dear Sir, —I hereby beg to
convey, through you, my best thanks to the Royal
Humane Society for the splendid testimonial I have this
day been presented with in recognition of my saving the
life of a fellow-creature, and I am proud to say that I
shall ever prize it as marking- an important and eventful
epoch in my life’s history.— John Fraser.’’
The Bulletin would sooner be that boy than the
stodgy prig who inspired the above acknowledg-
ment.
A War-Office in Trouble.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Soene War-Office, London. Telephones are
ringing, typewriters clicking, clerks in dozens
rushing hither and thither at express rate. An
atmosphere of feverish unrest hangs over every-
thing. In the passages and lobbies crowds of
military contractors, officials, newspaper-men, and
inventors of patent guns have been waiting for
hours to get a few minutes’ interview with those
in authority. Mounted messengers dash up to the
door every few seconds. In the innermost room
of all a much-decorated military veteran with a
bald head, a grizzled moustache and an eyeglass is
dictating to three shorthand-writers at once, while
clerks rush in and out with cablegrams, letters,
and cards from people waiting. On the table are
littered a heap of lists of troops, army-contracts,
and tenders for supplies, all marked “Urgent.”
A clerk rushes in : “ Cablegram from Australia
offering troops, sir 1 ”
Military Veteran : “ No ! can’t have ’em. It
has been decided not to use blacks, except as a
last resource. ”
Clerk: “But these are white troops, sir—the
local forces. It is officially desired that they be
taken if possible.”
Military Veteran: “Who is the Australian
Commander-in-chief ? I didn’t know they had an
army at all. I knew they had police, of course ! ”
. Clerk : “ There are seven distinct cablegrams,
sir; seven distinct Commanders-in-chief all offer-
ing troops.”
M. V. (roused to excitement): “ Great Heavens!
are they going to take the war off our hands?
Seven Commanders-in-chief ! They must have
been quietly breeding armies all these years in
Australia. Let’s have a look at the cables.
Where’s this from? ”
C. : “Tasmania, sir. They offer to send a
Commander-in-chief and ’’—pauses, aghast—“and
eighty-jive men ! ”
M.V. (jumping to his feet): “ What! Have I
wasted all this time talking about 85 men 1 You
must be making a mistake ! ”
C. : “ No, sir. It says 85 men !”
M.Y. : “Well I am damned! Eighty-five
men ! You cable back and say that I’ve seen
bigger armies on the stage at Drury Lane theatre.
Just wire and say this isn’t a pantomime. They
haven’t got to march round and round a piece of
scenery. Tell ’em to stop at home and breed ! ”
(Resumes dictation) “ At least five thousand extra
men should be sent from India in addition to ”
C. : “ Cabinet instructions are to take these
troops, whether they’re any good or not, sir.
Political reasons ! ”
M.V. (with a sigh): “Well, let ’em come. Let
’em all come—the whole eighty-five ! But don’t
let it leak out, or the Boers will say we’re not
playing ’em fair. Tell ’em to send infantry, any-
how; we don’t want horses eating their heads
off.”
C. (interrupts again): “These other colonies,
sir—are we to accept ’em all ? ”
M.V. : “Yes ! Didn’t I say let ’em all come.
There’ll be plenty of room for ’em in South Africa.
They won’t feel crowded.” (Resumes dictation.)
“ The expenditure of a hundred thousand pounds
at least will be needed to——.”
C.: “ They want to know, if they pay the men’s
fares over, will the British Government pay their
return fares ? ” ■
M. Y.: “Yes ! I should think we would. We’ll
put ’em in the front and there won’t be so many
of ’em left to go back. If the colonies had any
sense they'd have paid the return fares. Now
please go away and let me get to work.”
Ten minutes later. Clerk timidly re-appears.
“ If you please sir, another cable from New South
Wales. They say they would sooner send
artillery 1 ’’
M.V.: “Oh, blast it all 1 What does their
artillery amount to?”
C. : “One battery, Sir.”
M. V.: “ One battery! Well, they’ve got to come,
I suppose. ‘ Cry havoc and let slip the dogs of war.’
Stop their one battery if you can ; but if not, let
it come. And now go, and don’t let me have any
more of you.” Resumes dictation, and has just
got to “ the purchase of ten thousand horses,”
when the clerk reappears.
C. : “ Fresh cable, sir ! Two circus-proprietors
in Sydney have presented six circus-horses ”
_ M.V. : Shivering Sheol! This is theclimax ! Six
circus horses ! Didn’t they say anything about a
clown and pantaloon ? Surely they wouldn’t see
the Empire hurled to ruin for want of a clown 1
Perhaps they could let us have a few sword-
swallowers to get off with the Boers’ weapons?
Look here, now—hand the whole thing over to
one of the senior clerks, and tell him to do exactly
what he d well pleases in the matter, but that
if he comes in here to ask any questions about it,
I’ll have him shot ! Now go, and don’t you come
here any more, or I’ll have you shot too ! Take
this cheque for a hundred thousand to the petty-
cash department, and tell that contractor outside
that his tender is two millions over the estimate,
and don’t let me hear any more of this blessed
Australian army.”
Cable message : “ Some difficulty exists in ascer-
taining from the War-Office whether the colonial
troops will be expected to take their own saddles
or not, and whether the officer commanding shall
take one horse or two. It is not definitely known
whether the offer of Fitzgeralds’ six circus-horses
will be accepted. Great enthusiasm prevails.”
B.
Artists and Souls.
“ Maddy M.” sends to The Bulletin this lovely
piece of malice :
Some people are inclined to look upon artists as beings
whose morals are never above reproach. That is because
the males and females mix so much together. They do
the same work, and they have the same affinities, or
think they have. If a male artist paints a picture and
wants to hear what somebody else thinks of it, he will
not take it to his brother artist, as his brother artist is
always his worst enemy, and “doesn’t know a good thing
when he sees it.” He will take it to his sister artist;
and, of course, as she has no occasion to be jealous of
him, she will gush and gurgle over it (to his face) and
tell him she can see his whole soul is in it. (It is a daub,
but she does not mean to insinuate anything against
his soul). He will shake his head sadly and say, “ Ah, we
never realise our ideals ! ” and then she says, “ Are you
an Impressionist or a Realist?” (I think these are the
terms), and he generally says he is an “ Impressionist,”
as an “Impressionist” is a more toney affair than a
“ Realist.” And then they say something about “ Pre-
Raphaelitism ’’—but by this time I have had enough.
At first, when I used to visit my artist friend, Miss
Draggles, I was rather amazed to find her tete-d-tSte at
any hour with what I thought was a man ; but it wasn’t
a man, it was only an artist : and people who don’t
know this will do well to digest it, and not scandalise
these poor harmless creatures as they do.

BUTTER
The same remarks that apply to milk apply equally to butter. Pasteurised butter is made from the very finest
cream, consequently the butter has a fine aroma that only Pasteurisation can give. There are a few, we are
pleased to say, only a few, idiotic folks who will not be convinced of the value of the process; but it is satisfactory
to know that these are getting less daily. The difference in price between Pasteurised, beautifully-flavored butter and the
ordinary make is only per lb. It would pay-’consu.mers well to give six times this difference. Heads of households should insist
that their grocer supplies Pasteurised butter, and if he will not, send your Orders to US, and we will, deliver it at your doors.
Good butter such as we nave described is appieciated always, whereas at afternoon teas or for ordinary use
at any price. The famous “Berry” brand Pasteurised butter can be obtained only from this Company.
